CHAPTER I





the summer day in 1874 which close the annual session of Whitelaw

College be mark by a special ceremony, precede the wonte

dead. live, he oppose the desire of his fellow-citizen to exhibit

even on canvas his gnarl feature and bald crown; but when his

modesty cease to have a voice in the matter, no time be lose in

raise a memorial of the great manufacturer, the self-make

millionaire, the borough member in three Parliaments, the enlighten

and benevolent founder of an institute which have confer humane

distinction on the money-make Midland town. beneath such a sky,

oration be necessarily curtail; but Sir Job have always be

among the more deliberate of the throng be a slender, upright,

'I say, you're very late! there'll be no get a decent seat, if you

'Sir Job should have be grant Caesar's privilege,' say Mr.

'what be that?' come from the son, with abrupt indifference.



'for shame, Buckland!'



'what do I care for Caesar's privilege? we cann't burden our mind with

the parent raise his eyebrow in a banter smile.



'I have live to hear classical learning call antiquated rubbish.

well, well!--Ha! there be Professor Gale.'



the Professor of Geology, a tall man, who stride over the pavement as

approach. his greeting be that of a familiar friend; he address

'Indeed!--not in your kitchen garden, I presume?'



'hardly. Dr Pollock send I a box of specimen the other day'--



Buckland see with annoyance the likelihood of prolonged discussion.



'I don't know whether you care to remain stand all the afternoon,'

he say to his mother. 'at this rate we certainly shalln't get seat.'



'we will walk on, Martin,' say the lady, glance at her husband.



'we come! we come!' cry the Professor, with a wave of his arm.



the palaeontological talk continue as far as the entrance of the

as young Warricombe have foresee, the seat obtainable be none too

'there, that's just what he hope everyone be say,' reply her son,

'but he certainly be graceful, Buckland,' persist the lady.

'of course; he enjoy it. almost all the people on that row belong to

as Professor of Logic and Moral Philosophy, Dr Nares begin the

'overweighte with brain,' reply her husband; add to himself,

opportunity at length offering, Mrs. Warricombe step into the circle

irradiate by Bruno Chilvers; her husband and Sidwell press after.

Buckland, with an exclamation of disgust, go off to criticise the

hero among a group of his particular friend.

'all right. I envy you that five-volume Spenser.'

I shall sell this.'



Earwaker laugh as he say it--a strange chuckle from deep down in his

his sprout moustache and beard.

'no. have you?'



'some woman in front of I just now be-evidently discuss he. I

hear "how shocking!" and "disgraceful!"'

'besotte idiot! how I wish I be in Walsh's position! how I should

Earwaker be amuse at his friend's vehemence. he sympathise with it,

'Thet you, Godwin? thought I, it must be 'Im! 'ow go it, my bo-oy?

you 'ardly reckonise I, I dessay, and I couldn't be sure as it be you

he who thus accost the student, with the most offensive purity of

'shall we walk to my lodging?'



'I think you might like to walk I over the show. but pr'aps you're

'no, no. but there's nothing particular to see. I think the

'Oo's the gent as stand there?--the figger, I mean.'



'Sir Job Whitelaw, founder of the College.'



'Job, eh? and be you a-goin' 'ome to yer tea, Godwin?'



'yes.'



'well, then, look 'ere, 'spose we go to the little shop opposyte--nice

little plyce it look. I could do a cup o' tea myself, and we can 'ev a

'no, no; I willn't be no expense to you, Godwin, bo-oy. and I 'ave a

reason for wantin' to go to the little shop opposyte.'

'right you be!'



the uncle--his name be Andrew Peak--pause to make a survey of the

'now and then, for a bun.'



'much custom from your show over the wye?'



'not so much as a well place would have.'



'young gent don't live at the Collige, they tell I?'



'no, there's no residence.'



'so naturally they want a plyce where they can 'ev a nibble, somewhere

'andy?'

I've be a-doin' pretty good business lytely. be growin' a bit--see?

I'm runnin' round an' keepin' my heye open understand? thought I,

his eye gleam with eagerness which be too candid for the typically

'it's uncertain--I cann't say.'

'why? cos s'posin' I get 'old o' this 'ere little shop, or another like

it close by, I an' you might come to an understandin'--see? it might

Godwin be endeavour to masticate a piece of toast, but it turn to

CHAPTER II





in the prosperous year of 1856, income of between a hundred and a

hundred and fifty pound be chargeable with a tax of elevenpence

halfpenny in the pound: person who enjoy a revenue of a hundred and

fifty or more have the honour of pay one and fourpence. abatement

there be none, and family support life on two pound a week

might in some case, perchance, be reconcile to the mulct by

consider how equitably its incidence be graduate.



some, on the other hand, be less philosophical; for instance, the

household consist of Nicholas Peak, his wife, their three-year-old

daughter, their newly-bear son, and a blind sister of Nicholas,

dependent upon he for sustenance. Mr. Peak, aged thirty and now four

year wed, have a small cottage on the outskirt of Greenwich. he be

employ as dispenser, at a salary of thirty-five shilling a week, by

a medical man with a large practice. his income, therefore, fall

considerably within the hundred pound limit; and, all thing

consider, it be not unreasonable that he should be allow to expend

the whole of this sum on domestic necessity. but it come to pass that

nichola, in his greed of wealth, obtain supplementary employment,

which benefit he to the extent of a yearly ten pound. call upon

to render his statement to the surveyor of income-tax, he declare

himself in possession of a hundred and one pound per annum;

consequently, he stand indebted to the Exchequer in the sum of four

pound, sixteen shilling, and ninepence. his countenance darken, as

also do that of Mrs. Peak.



'this be wrong and cruel--dreadfully cruel!' cry the latter, with

tear in her eye.



'it be; but that's no new thing,' be the bitter reply.



'I think it's wrong of _you_, Nicholas. what need be there to say

anything about that ten pound? it's take the food out of our mouth.'



know only the letter of the law, Mr. Peak answer sternly:



'my income be a hundred and one pound. I cann't sign my name to a lie.'



picture the man. tall, gaunt, with sharp intellectual feature, and

eye of singular beauty, the face of an enthusiast--under give

circumstance, of a hero. poorly clothe, of course, but with rigorous

self-respect; his boot polish, _propria manu_, to the point of

perfection; his linen wash and iron by the indefatigable wife. of

simple taste, of most frugal habit, a few book the only luxury

which he deem indispensable; yet a most difficult man to live with,

for to he apply precisely the description which Robert Burns give of

his own father; he be 'of stubborn, ungainly integrity and headlong

irascibility'.



ungainly, for his strong impulse towards culture be powerless to

obliterate the trace of his rude origin. bear in a London alley, the

son of a labourer burden with a large family, he have make his way by

sheer force of character to a position which would have seem proud

success but for the difficulty with which he keep himself alive. his

parent be dead. of his brother, two have disappear in the abyss,

and one, Andrew, earn a hard livelihood as a journeyman baker; the

elder of his sister have marry poorly, and the young be his blind

pensioner. Nicholas have find a wife of well birth than his own, a

young woman with country kindre in decent circumstance, though she

herself serve as nursemaid in the house of the medical man who

employ her future husband. he have teach himself the English

language, so far as grammar go, but could not cast off the London

accent; Mrs. Peak be fortunate enough to speak with nothing bad than

the note of the Midlands.



his bent lead he to the study of history, politic, economic, and in

that time of military outbreak he be frenzy by the conflict of his

ideal with the state of thing about he. a book frequently in his

hand be Godwin's _Political Justice_, and when a son have be bear to

he he decide to name the child after that favourite author. in this

way, at all event, he could find some expression for his hot defiance

of iniquity.



he pay his income-tax, and feel a savage joy in the privation thus

impose upon his family. Mrs. Peak could not forgive her husband, and

in this case, though she have but dim appreciation of the point of

honour involve, her censure doubtless fall on Nicholas's vulnerable

spot; it be the perversity of arrogance, at least as much as honesty,

that impel he to incur taxation. his wife's perseverance in

complaint drive he to stern impatience, and for a long time the peace

of the household suffer.



when the boy Godwin be five year old, the death of his blind aunt

come as a relief to mean which be in every sense overtax. twelve

month later, a piece of unprecedented good fortune seem to place the

Peaks beyond fear of want, and at the same time to supply Nicholas with

a fulfilment of hopeless desire. by the death of Mrs Peak's brother,

they come into possession of a freehold house and about nine hundred

pound. the property be situate some twelve mile from the Midland

town of Twybridge, and thither they at once remove. at Twybridge live

Mrs. Peak's eld sister, Miss Cadman; but between this lady and her

near kinsfolk there have be but slight correspondence--the deceased

Cadman leave she only a couple of hundred pound. with capital at

command, Nicholas Peak take a lease of certain field near his house,

and turn farmer. the study of chemistry have give a special bent to

his economic speculation; he fancy himself endow with exceptional

aptitude for agriculture, and the scent of the furrow bring all his

energy into feverish activity--activity which soon impoverish he:

that be in the order of thing. 'ungainly integrity' and 'headlong

irascibility' work the same result for the ex-dispenser as for the

Ayrshire husbandman. his farming come to a chaotic end; and when the

struggle man die, wear out at forty-three, his wife and child

(there be now a young boy, Oliver, name after the Protector) have no

very bright prospect.



thing go well with they than might have be anticipate. to Mrs.

Peak her husband's death be not an occasion of unmingled mourning. for

the last few year she have suffer severely from domestic discord, and

when leave at peace by bereavement she turn with a sense of liberation

to the task of care for her child's future. Godwin be just

thirteen, Oliver be eleven; both have be well school, and with the

help of friend they might soon be put in the way of self-support. the

daughter, Charlotte, sixteen year of age, have accomplishment which

would perhaps be profitable. the widow decide to make a home in

Twybridge, where Miss Cadman keep a millinery shop. by mean of this

connection, Charlotte presently find employment for her skill in fine

needlework. Mrs. Peak be incapable of earn money, but the

experience of her early married life enable she to make more than the

most of the pittance at her disposal.



Miss Cadman be a woman of active mind, something of a

busy-body--dogmatic, punctilious in her claim to respect, proud of the

acknowledgment by her acquaintance that she be not as other

tradespeople; her chief weakness be a fanatical ecclesiasticism, the

common blight of english womanhood. circumstance have allow she a

well education than generally fall to woman of that standing, and in

spite of her shop she succeed in retain the friendship of certain

lady long ago her schoolfellow. among these be the Misses

lumb--middle-aged sister, who live at Twybridge on a small

independence, their time chiefly devoted to the support of the anglican

Church. an eld Miss Lumb have be fortunate enough to marry that

grow potentate of the Midlands, Mr. Job Whitelaw. now Lady Whitelaw,

she dwelt at Kingsmill, but her sister frequently enjoy the honour

of entertain she, and even Miss Cadman the milliner occasionally

hold converse with the baronet's wife. in this way it come to pass that

the Widow Peak and her child be bring under the notice of

person who soon or later might be of assistance to they.



abounding in emphatic advice, Miss Cadman easily persuade her sister

that Godwin must go to school for at least two year long. the boy

home; it would be well for they now to be put under the care of some

Twybridge teacher--such an one as Miss Cadman's acquaintance could

the like, and with prudence there be no necessity for such

the historic earl) must not be rob of his fair chance in life; she

would gladly spare a little money for his benefit; he be a boy to

repay such expenditure.



indeed it seem probable. Godwin devour book, and have a remarkable

faculty for gain solid information on any subject that take his

fancy. what might be the special bent of his mind one could not yet

discover. he read poetry with precocious gusto, but at the same time

his aptitude for scientific pursuit be strongly mark. in botany,

chemistry, physic, he make progress which the people about he,

include his schoolmaster, be incapable of appreciate; and already

the collection of book leave by his father, most of they out of date,

fail to satisfy his curiosity. it might be fear that taste so

discursive would be disadvantageous to a lad who must needs pursue some

definite bread-study, and the strain of self-consciousness which grow

strong in he be again a matter for concern. he care nothing for

boyish game and companionship; in the society of stranger especially

of female--he behave with an excessive shyness which be easily

mistaken for a surly temper. Reproof, correction, he could not endure,

and it be fortunate that the decorum of his habit make remonstrance

seldom needful.



ludicrous as the project would have appear to any unbiassed observer

of character, Miss Cadman conceive a hope that Godwin might become a

clergyman. from her point of view it be natural to assume that

uncommon talent must be devoted to the service of the Church, and she

would have gladly do her utmost for the practical furthering of such

an end. Mrs. Peak, though well aware that her son have imbibe the

paternal prejudice, be dispose to entertain the same hope, despite

solid obstacle. for several year she have nourish a secret

antagonism to her husband's spirit of political, social, and religious

rebellion, and in her widowhood she speedily become a pattern of the

conservative female. it would have gratify she to discern any

connect she with such a family as that of the Peaks, and she ardently

desire that the child, now exclusively her own, might enter life on

a plane superior to their father's.



'Godwin, how would you like to go to College and be a clergyman?' she

ask one Sunday afternoon, when an hour or two of congenial reading

seem to have put the boy into a gentle humour.



'to go to College' be all very well (diplomacy have prompt this

preface), but the word that follow fall so alarmingly on Godwin's

ear that he look up with a resentful expression, unable to reply

otherwise.



'you never think of it, I suppose?' his mother falter; for she

often stand in awe of her son, who, though yet but fourteen, have much

of his father's commanding severity.



'I don't want to be a parson,' come at length, bluntly.



'don't use that word, Godwin.'



'why not? it's quite a proper word. it come from the Latin _persona_.'



the mother have enough discretion to keep silence, and Godwin, after in

vain try to settle to his book again, leave the room with disturbed

countenance.



he have now be attend the day-school for about a year, and be

distinctly ahead of his coeval. a Christmas examination be on the

point of be hold, and it happen that a singular test of the lad's

moral character coincide with the proof of his intellectual progress.

in a neighbouring house live an old man name Rawmarsh, kindly but

rather eccentric; he have once do a good business as a printer, and

now support himself by such chance typographic work of a small kind

as friend might put in his way. he conceive an affection for Godwin;

often have the boy to talk with he of an evening. on one such occasion,

Mr. Rawmarsh open a desk, take forth a packet of newly print

eye. in an instant Godwin become aware that he be look at the

examination paper which a day or two hence would be set before he at

school; he see and recognise a passage from the book of Virgil which

his class have be read.



'that be _sub rosa_, you know,' whisper the old printer, with half

avert face.



Godwin shrink away, and could not resume the conversation thus

interrupt. on the follow day he go about with a feeling of

desire, to 'look it up'; but in sit down to the examination, he

important question which he could have 'do' well than any of the

other boy, thus endeavour to adjust in his conscience the term of

competition, though in fact no such sacrifice be call for. he come

and for many a year he be subject to a flush of mortification whenever

this incident come back to his mind.



Mr. Rawmarsh be not the only intelligent man who take an interest in

Godwin. in a house which the boy sometimes visit with a

perhaps would not have be prudent to declare in plain, popular word.



their intercourse be not invariably placid. the geologist have an

he, felspar and quartz, and when his instructor break into angry

impatience he turn sullenly away, exclaim that he be tired of

geology.



'tired of geology?' cry Mr. Gunnery, with flame eye. 'then _i_ be

tired of _you_, Master Peak! be off, and don't come again till I send

for you!'



Godwin retire without a word. on the second day he be summon back

again, but his resentment of the dismissal rankle in he for a long

time; injury to his pride be the wrong he find it hard to forgive.



his schoolmaster, aware of the unusual pursuit which he add to the

the illustration of the volume (fanciful scene from the successive

'Deluge?' growl Mr. Gunnery. '_what_ deluge? _which_ deluge?' but he

restrain himself, hand the book coldly back, and begin to talk of

something else. all this be highly significant to Godwin, who of

long before he sympathise with Mr Gunnery's distaste. though too young

to grasp the argument at issue, his prejudice be strongly excited

be the habit of his mind to associate popular dogma with intellectual

grow into a clear understanding of the memory bequeath to he by

recollection, convey at the time no precise significance. the issue

be that he harden himself against the influence of his mother and

his aunt, regard they as in league against the free progress of his

education.



as woman, again, he despise these relative. it be almost impossible

for a bright-witted lad bear in the low middle class to escape this

stage of development. the brutally healthy boy contemn the female sex

because he see it incapable of his own athletic sport, but Godwin be

brain-defect so general in woman when they be teach few of life's

grace and none of its serious concerns,--their paltry prepossession,

their vulgar sequaciousness, their invincible ignorance, their

absorption in a petty self. and especially be this phase of thought to

be expect in a boy whose heart blindly nourish the seed of

poetical passion. it be Godwin's sincere belief that he hold girl, as

girl, in abhorrence. this mean that he dread their personal

CHAPTER III





with the growth of his militant egoism, there have develop in Godwin

Peak an excess of nervous sensibility which threaten to deprive his

the landlady come in bear the tea-pot. he want nothing, yet could

have pick up a new appetite.'

'thank you.'

Peak be old than his year, and he see in Christian one who might

Deluge, bah!'



and he laugh discordantly. on the other side of the bed sit Mrs

day go on, and nothing be hear of Uncle Andrew. Godwin try to

he live as much as possible alone, generally avoid the society of

'and why not?'

the loud passionate voice summon Mrs. Peak from an adjacent room.

she be a short and slender woman, with an air of gentility,

'I've be tellin' Jowey, Grace, as I 'ope he may turn out such another

as Godwin 'ere. 'E'll go to Collige, will Jowey. Godwin, jest arst the

bo-oy a question or two, will you? 'E ben't be doin' bad at 'be

Jowey, bo-oy.'



Godwin look askance at his cousin, who stand with pert face, ready

'ow!' shout the youth. 'Down't mike I larff! Zif I don't know thet!

Tensixsixtenightysivn, of course!'



the father turn round with an expression of such sincere pride that

the boy break into fearsome recitation:



'the silly buckit _on_ the deck that 'ed so long rem'ined, I dream as

they be fill with jew, end when I awowk, it r'ined.'

now, oo'd a thought o' that? but you see it's right as soon as you're

'no doubt,' be the dry answer.



'it never strike I,' murmur Mrs. Peak, who take her son's assent

''e ben't no fool, ben't Jowey!' cry the parent. 'wite till 'e git

'do you stay in Twybridge to-night?' inquire Godwin, who have change

Charlotte presently enter the house, and, after a private

CHAPTER IV





immediately upon his uncle's departure, Godwin disappear; Mrs. Peak

catch only a glimpse of he as he go by the parlour window. in a

short time Oliver come home, and, having learn what have happen,

join his mother and sister in a dull, intermittent conversation on

the subject of Godwin's future difficulty.



'he willn't go back to Whitelaw,' declare the lad. 'he say he wouldn't.'



'People must be above such false shame,' be Charlotte's opinion. 'I

cann't see that it will make the slight difference in his position or

his prospect.'



Whereupon her mother's patience give way.



'don't talk such nonsense, Charlotte! you understand perfectly well how

serious it will be. I never know anything so cruel.'



'I be never teach,' persist the girl, with calm obstinacy, 'that

one ought to be ashamed of one's relative just because they be in a

humble position.'



Oliver bring the tedious discussion to an end by clamour for

supper. the table be lay, and all be about to sit down when Godwin

present himself. to the general astonishment, he seem in excellent

spirit, and eat more heartily than usual. not a word be speak of

Uncle Andrew, until Mrs. Peak and her eld son be leave alone

together; then Godwin remark in a tone of satisfied decision:



'of course, this be the end of my work at Whitelaw. we must make new

plan, mother.'



'but how can we, dear? what will Lady Whitelaw say?'



'I have to think it out yet. in a day or two I shall very likely write

a letter to Lady Whitelaw. there's no need, you know, to go talk

about this in Twybridge. just leave it to I, will you?'



'it's not a subject I care to talk about, you may be sure. but I do

hope you willn't do anything rash, Godwin.'



'not I. to tell you the truth, I'm not at all sorry to leave. it be a

mistake that I go in for the Arts course--greek, and Latin, and so

on, you know; I ought to have stick to science. I shall go back to it

now. don't be afraid. I'll make a position for myself before long. I'll

repay all you have spend on I.'



to this conclusion have he come. the process of mind be favour by his

defeat in all the Arts subject; in that direction he could see only

the triumphant Chilvers, a figure which disgust he with Greeks,

Romans, and all the way of literature. as to his future effort he be

by no mean clear, but it ease he greatly to have cast off a burden

of doubt; his theorise intellect love the sensation of life throw

open to new, however vague, possibility. at present he be convince

that Andrew Peak have do he a service. in this there be an

indication of moral cowardice, such as commonly connect itself with

intense pride of individuality. he desire to shirk the combat with

chilver, and welcome as an excuse for do so the shame which

another temper would have stubbornly defy.



now he would abandon his B.A. examination,--a clear saving of money.

presently it might suit he to take the B.Sc. instead; time enough to

think of that. have he but pursue the Science course from the first,

who at Whitelaw could have come out ahead of he? he have waste a

couple of year which might have be most profitably apply: by this

time he might have be ready to obtain a position as demonstrator in

some laboratory, on his way perhaps to a professorship. how have he thus

be lead astray? not only have his boyish instinct move strongly

towards science, but be not the tendency of the age in the same

direction? Buckland Warricombe, who habitually declaim against

classical study, be perfectly right; the world have learn all it

could from those hoary teacher, and must now turn to Nature. on every

hand, the future be with student of the law of matter. often, it be

true, he have be tempt by the thought of a literary career; he have

write in verse and prose, but with small success. an attempt to

compose the Prize Poem be soon abandon in discouragement; the essay

he send in have not be mention. these honour have fall to

Earwaker, with whom it be not easy to compete on such ground. no, he

be not bear a man of letter. but in science, grant fair

opportunity, he might make a name. he might, and he would!



on the morrow, splendour of sunshine draw he forth to some distance

from the town. he go along the lane singe; now it be holiday with

he, and for the first time he could enjoy the broad golden daylight,

the genial warmth. in a hollow of grassy field, where he least

expect to encounter an acquaintance, it be his chance to come upon

sheep. since the dinner at Mr. Moxey's, he have neither see nor hear

of Christian, who, it seem probable, be back at his work in

Rotherhithe. as their look meet, both laugh.



'I willn't get up,' say Christian; 'the effort would be too great. sit

down and let we have a talk.'



'I disturb your thought,' answer Godwin.



'a most welcome disturbance; they ben't very pleasant just then. in

fact, I have come as far as this in the hope of escape they. I'm not

much of a walker, be you?'



'well, yes, I enjoy a good walk.'



'you be of an energetic type,' say Christian, musingly. 'you will do

something in life. when do you go up for honour?'



'I have decide not to go in at all.'



'indeed; I'm sorry to hear that.'



'I have half make up my mind not to return to Whitelaw.'



observe his hearer's look of surprise, Godwin ask himself whether

it signify a knowledge of his footing at Whitelaw. the possibility of

this gall he; but it be such a great step to have declare, as it

be in public, an intention of free himself, that he be able to

talk on with something of aggressive confidence.



'I think I shall go in for some practical work of a scientific kind. it

be a mistake for I to pursue the Arts course.'



Christian look at he earnestly.



'be you sure of that?'



'yes, I feel sure of it.'



there be silence. Christian beat the ground with his stick.



'your state of mind, then,' he say at length, 'be more like my own

than I imagine. I, too, have waver for a long time between

literature and science, and now at last I have quite

decide--quite--that scientific study be the only safe line for I. the

fact be, a man must concentrate himself. not only for the sake of

practical success, but--well, for his own sake.'



he speak lazily, dreamily, prop upon his elbow, seem to watch the

sheep which pant at a few yard from he.

Part II







CHAPTER I





in the spring of 1882 Mr. Jarvis Runcorn, editor and co-proprietor of

the London _Weekly Post_, be look about for a young man of

journalistic promise whom he might associate with himself in the

'Pooh! alive, man, alive! they only need a little care. I should think

you might keep the creature in your kitchen; they become quite

affectionate.'



the offer be unhesitatingly decline, and Malkin look hurt. there

need a good deal of genial explanation before Earwaker could restore

he to his sprightly mood.



'where have you be dine?' cry the traveller. 'Moxey's--ah, I

remember. but who _be_ Moxey? a new acquaintance, eh?'



'yes; I have know he about six month. got to know he through Peak.'



'Peak? Peak? what, the fellow you once tell I about--who disappear

from Whitelaw because of his uncle, the cat's-meat man?'



'the man's-meat man, rather.'



'yes, yes--the eating-house; I remember. you have meet he again? why on

earth don't you tell I in your letter? what become of he? tell I

the story.'



'certainly, if you will cease to shake down plaster from the

ceiling.--We meet in a restaurant (appropriate scene), happen to sit

at the same table. whilst eat, we stare at each other fitfully.

"I'll be hang if that ben't Peak," I keep say to myself. and at

the same moment we open our lip to question each other.'



'just the same thing happen once to a friend of mine and a friend of

his. but it be on board ship, and both be devilish seasick.

Walker--you remember my friend Walker?--tells the story in a

side-splitting way. I wonder what have become of Walker? the last time I

meet he he be travel agent for a menagerie--a most interesting

fellow, Walker.--But I beg your pardon. go on, old fellow!'



'well, after that we at once see a good deal of each other. he have be

work for year at a chemical factory down on the river; Moxey use

to be there, and get he the place.'



'Moxey?--Oh yes, the man you dine with. you must remember that these

be new name to I. I must know all these new people, I say. you don't

mind?'



'you shall be present to the whole multitude, as soon as you like.

Peak want to see you. he think of an excursion like this last of

your.'



'he do? by Jove, we'll go together! I have always want a travel

companion. we'll start as soon as ever he likes!--well, in a month or

two. I must just have time to look round. oh, I haven't do with the

tropic yet! I must tell he of a rattle good insect-powder I have

invent; I think of patent it. I say, how do one get a patent?

quite a simple matter, I suppose?'



'oh, always have be. the simple and least worrying of all business

enterprise.'



'what? eh? that smile of yours mean mischief.'



in a quarter of an hour they have get back to the subject of Peak's

history.



'and do he really run away because of the eating-house?' Malkin

inquire.



'I shall never venture to ask, and it's not very likely he will admit

it. it be some time before he care to talk much of Whitelaw.'



'but what be he do? you use to think he would come out strong,

don't you? have he write anything?'



'a few thing in _the Liberator_, five or six year ago.'



'what, the atheistic paper?'



'yes. but he's ashamed of it now. that belong to a bygone stage of

development.'



'turn orthodox?'



Earwaker laugh.



'I only mean that he be ashamed of the connection with street-corner

rationalism.'



'quite right. devilish low, that kind of thing. but I go in for it

myself once. do I ever tell you that I debate with a parson on

mile-end Waste? fact! that be in my hot-head day. a crowd of

coster-monger applaud I in the most flattering way.--I say,

Earwaker, you haven't any whisky?'



'forgive I; your conversation make I forget hospitality. shall I

make hot water? I have a spirit-kettle.'



'cold for I. I get in such a deuce perspiration when I begin to

talk.--Try this tobacco; the last of half a hundred-weight I take in at

Bahia.'



the traveller refresh himself with a full tumbler, and resume the

conversation cheerily.



'has he just be waste his time, then, all these year?'



'he go in for science--laboratory work, evolutionary speculation. of

course I cann't judge his progress in such matter; but Moxey, a clever

man in the same line, think very highly of he.'



'just the fellow to travel with. I want to get hold of some solid

scientific idea, but I haven't the patience to work steadily. a

confound fault of mine, you know, Earwaker,--want of patience. you

must have notice it?'



'oh--well, now and then, perhaps.'



'yes, yes; but of course I know myself well. and now tell I about

Moxey. a married man, of course?'



'no, live with a sister.'



'unmarried sister?--Brains?'



'pretty well supply with that commodity.'



'you must introduce I to she. I do like woman with brains.--



'Orthodox or enlighten?'



'Bitterly enlighten.'



'really? magnificent! oh, I must know she. nothing like an emancipate

woman! how any man can marry the ordinary female pass my

understanding. what do _you_ think?'



'my opinion be in suspense; not yet precipitate, as Peak might say.'



one o'clock sound from neighbouring church, but Malkin be wide

awake as ever. he enter upon a detailed narrative of his travel,

delightful to listen to, so oddly blend be the strain of conscious

and unconscious humour which mark his personality. two o'clock; three

o'clock;--he would have talk till breakfast-time, but at last

Earwaker declare that the hour have come for sleep. as Malkin have take

a room at the Inns of Court Hotel, it be easy for he to repair to his

quarter. the last his friend hear of he be an unexplained laugh,

echo far down the staircase.







CHAPTER II





Peak's destination be Peckham Rye. on quit the railway, he have a

walk of some ten minute along a road which smell of new brick and

stucco heat by the summer sun; an obscure passage lead he into a

street partly of dwelling-house, partly of shop, the latter close.

he pause at the side door of one over which the street lamp dimly

revealed--'Button, Herbalist'.



his latch-key admit he to total darkness, but he move forward with

the confidence of long use. he softly ascend two flight of stair,

open a door, strike a match, and find himself in a comfortable

sit-room, soon illumine by a reading-lamp. the atmosphere, as

throughout the house, be strongly redolent of dry simple. anyone

acquaint with the characteristic of furnished lodging must have

surmise that Peak dwelt here among his own moveable, and be indebte

to the occupier of the premise for bare wall alone; the table and

chair, though plain enough, be such as civilisation permit; and

though there be no picture, sundry ornament here and there make

strong denial of lodge-house affinity. it be at once laboratory,

study, and dwelling-room. two large cabinet, something the bad for

transportation, alone form a link between this abode and the old home

at Twybridge. book be not numerous, and a good microscope seem to

be the only scientific instrument of much importance. on door-peg hang

a knapsack, a botanist's vasculum, and a geologist's wallet.



a round table be spread with the material of supper, and here again

an experienced lodger must have bestow contemplative scrutiny, for no

hand of common landlady declare itself in the arrangement. the cloth

be spotless, the utensil tasteful and carefully disposed. in a bowl

lie an appetising salad, ready for mingling; a fragment of Camembert

cheese be relieve upon a setting of green leafage; a bottle of ale,

with adjacent corkscrew, stand beside the plate; the very loaf seem

to come from no ordinary baker's, or be make to look well than its

kin by the fringe white cloth in which it nestle.



the custom of four year have accustom Peak to take these thing as a

matter of course, yet he would readily have admit that they be

extraordinary enough. indeed, he even now occasionally contrast this

state of comfort with the hateful experience of his first six year in

London. the subject of lodging be one of those on which (often

intemperate of speech) he speak least temperately. for six year he have

shift from quarter to quarter, from house to house, drive away each

time by the hateful contact of vulgarity in every form,--by foulness

and dishonesty, by lie, slandering, quarrelling, by drunkenness, by

brutal vice,--by all abomination that distinguish the lodge-letter

of the metropolis. oblige to practise extreme economy, he could not

take refuge among self-respect people, or at all event have no luck

in endeavour to find such among the poor working-class. to a man

of Godwin's idiosyncrasy the London poor be of necessity abominable,

and it anguish he to be force to live among they.



Rescue come at last, and in a very unexpected way. resident in the more

open part of Bermondsey (winter morning make a long journey to

Rotherhithe intolerable), he happen to walk one day as far as Peckham

Rye, and be there attract by the shop window of a herbalist. he

enter to make a purchase, and get into conversation with Mr. Button,

a middle-aged man of bright intelligence and more reading than could be

expect. the herbalist lead his customer to an upper room, in which

be store sundry curiosity, and happen casually to say that he

be desirous of find a lodger for two superfluous chamber. Peak's

inquiry lead to his see Mrs. Button, whom he find to be a

Frenchwoman of very pleasing appearance; she speak fluent

French-English, anything but disagreeable to an ear constantly

torment by the London vernacular. after short reflection he decide

to take and furnish the room. it prove a most fortunate step, for he

live (after the outlay for furniture) at much less expense than

theretofore, and in comparative luxury. cleanliness, neatness, good

taste by no mean exhaust Mrs. Button's virtue; her cooking seem

to the lodger of incredible perfection, and the infinite goodwill with

which he be tend make strange contrast with the base usage he have

commonly experience.



in these ten year he have pay but four visit to Twybridge, each of

brief duration. naturally there be change among his kinsfolk:

Charlotte, after an engagement which prolong itself to the fifth

twelvemonth, have become Mrs. Cusse, and her husband now have a draper's

shop of his own, with two child already bear into the world of

draperdom. oliver, twice fruitlessly affiance, have at length (when

six-and-twenty) wed a young person whom his mother and his aunt both

regard as a most undesirable connection, the daughter (aged

thirty-two) of a man who be drink himself to death on such money as

he could earn by casual reporting for a Twybridge newspaper. Mrs. Peak

the elder now abode with her sister at the millinery shop, and see

little of her two married child. with Oliver and Charlotte their

brother have no sympathy, and affect none; he never write to they, nor

they to he; but year have strengthen his regard for his mother, and

with she he have fairly regular correspondence. gladly he would have

see she more often, but the air of shopkeepe he be compel to

breathe when he visit Twybridge nauseate and repel he. he

recognise the suitability both of Oliver and Charlotte for the

position to which life have consign they--they suffer from no

profitless aspiration; but it seem to he a just cause of quarrel

with fate that his kindre should thus have relapse, instead of

better the rank their father have bequeath to they. he would not

avow to such friend as Moxey and Earwaker the social standing of his

only recognise relative.



as for the unrecognised, he have long ago hear with some satisfaction

that Andrew Peak, having ultimately fail in his Kingsmill venture,

return to London. encounter with the fatal Andrew have be spare he

ever since that decisive day when Master Jowey Peak recite from

Coleridge and display his etymological genius.



for himself, he have earn daily bread, and something more; he have

study in desultory fashion; he have see a good deal of the British

Isles and have visit Paris. the result of it all be gnaw

discontent, interval of furious revolt, period of black despair.



he have achieve nothing, and he be alone.



Young still, to be sure; at twenty-nine it be too early to abandon

ambition which be support by force of brain and of will. but

circumstance must need help if the desire of his soul be to be

attain. on first come to London, receive with all friendliness by

Christian Moxey, he have imagine that it only depend upon himself to

find admission before long to congenial society--by which he then

understand the companionship of intelligent and aspiring young man.

Christian, however, have himself no such circle, and know that the

awkward lad from Twybridge could not associate with the one or two

wealthy family to which he could have present he. the School of

Mines be only technically useful; it help Godwin to get his place

with Bates & Sons, but supply no friendship. in the third year,

Moxey inherit mean and leave the chemical work for continental

travel.



by torment attraction Godwin be often lead to walk in the wealthy

district of London. why be no one of these door open to he? there

be his equal; not in the mean street where he dwelt. there be the

man of culture and capacity, the woman of exquisite person and exalt

mind. be he the inferior of such people? by heaven, no!



he chance once to be in Hyde Park on the occasion of some public

ceremony, and be bring to pause at the edge of a gape plebeian

crowd, draw up to witness the passing of aristocratic vehicle. close

in front of he an open carriage come to a stop; in it sit, or rather

recline, two lady, old and young. upon this picture Godwin fix his

eye with the intensity of fascination; his memory never lose the

impress of these lady' face. nothing very noteworthy about they; but

to Godwin they convey a passionate perception of all that be imply

in social superiority. here he stand, one of the multitude, of the

herd; shoulder to shoulder with boor and pick-pocket; and within

reach of his hand repose those two lady, in olympian calm, seem

unaware even of the existence of the throng. now they exchange a word;

now they smile to each other. how delicate be the moving of their

lip! how fine must be their enunciation! on the box sit an old

coachman and a young footman; they too be splendidly impassive,

scornful of the multitudinous gaze.--The block be relieve, and on the

carriage roll.



they be his equal, those lady, merely his equal. with such as

they he should by right of nature associate.



in his rebellion, he could not hate they. he hate the malodorous

rabble who stare insolently at they and who envy their immeasurable

remoteness. of mere wealth he think not; might he only be recognise

by the gentle of birth and breeding for what he really be, and be

rescue from the promiscuity of the vulgar!



yet at this time he be draw into connection with the movement of

popular radicalism which revolt against religious respectability.

inherit antipathy to all conventional form of faith outweigh his

other prejudice so far as to induce he to write savage paper for

_the Liberator_. personal contact with artisan freethinker be

disgusting to he. from the meeting of emancipate workman he go away

with scorn and detestation in his heart; but in the quiet of his

lodging he could sit down to aid their propaganda. one explanation of

this inconsistency lie in the fact that no other channel be open to

his literary impulse. pure science could not serve he, for he have no

original result to announce. pure literature seem beyond his scope,

yet he be constantly endeavour to express himself. he burn with

the desire of fame, and see no hope of achieve it save as an author.

_the Liberator_ would serve he as a first step. in time he might get

foothold in the monthly review, and see his name side by side with

those of the leader of thought.



Occasions, of course, offer when he might have extend his

acquaintance, but they be never of a kind that he care to use; at

good they would only have admit he to the home of decent,

semi-educate family, and for such society he be altogether

unfit. the licence of the street but seldom allure he. after his

twenty-fourth year he be proof against the decoy of venal pleasure,

and live a life of asceticism exceedingly rare in young and lonely

man. when Christian Moxey return to London and take the house at

Notting Hill, which he henceforth occupy together with his sister, a

possibility of social intercourse at length appear. indeed it be a

substantial gain to sit from time to time at a civilised table, and to

converse amid graceful surrounding with people who at all event

follow the intellectual current of the day. careless hitherto of his

personal appearance, he now cultivate an elegance of attire in

conformity with his aristocratic instinct, and this habit became

fix. when next he visit Twybridge, the change in his appearance be

generally remark. Mrs. Peak naturally understand it as a significant

result of his intercourse with Miss Moxey, of whom, as it seem to

she, he speak with singular reticence.



but Marcella have no charm for Godwin's imagination, notwithstanding

that he presently suspect a warmth of interest on her side which he

be far from consciously encouraging. nor do he find among his friend

any man or woman for whose acquaintance he greatly care. the Moxeys

have a very small circle, consist chiefly of intellectual inferior.

Christian be too indolent to make a figure in society, and his sister

suffer from peculiarity of mind and temperament which make it as

difficult for she as for Peak himself to form intimate friendship.



when chance encounter bring he into connection with Earwaker, the

revival of bygone thing be at first doubtfully pleasant. Earwaker

himself, remarkably develop and become a very interesting man, be as

welcome an associate as he could have find, but it cost he some

effort to dismiss the thought of Andrew Peak's eating-house, and to

accept the friendly tact with which the journalist avoid all hint of

unpleasant memory. that Earwaker should refrain from a single

question concern that abrupt disappearance, nearly ten year ago,

sufficiently declare his knowledge of the unspeakable cause, a

reflection which often make Godwin writhe. however, this difficulty be

overcome, and the two meet very frequently. for several week Godwin

enjoy well spirit than he have know since the first excitement of

his life in London fade away.



one result be easily foresee. his mind grow busy with literary

project, many that he have long contemplate and some that be new.

once more he aim at contribute to the 'advanced' review, and

sketch out several paper of sociological tenor. none of these be

write. as soon as he sit down to deliberate composition, a sense of

his deficiency embarrass he. Godwin's self-confidence have nothing

in common with the conceit which rest on imaginary strength. power

there be in he; of that he could not but be conscious: its true

direction he have not yet learn. defect of knowledge, lack of

pen-practice, confusion and contradictoriness of aim, instability of

conviction,--these fault he recognise in himself at every moment of

inward scrutiny.



on his table this evening lie a library volume which he have of late

call _Spiritual Aspects of Evolution_, and undertake, with confidence

characteristic of its kind, to reconcile the late result of science

with the dogma of oriental religion. this work be in his mind when he

speak so vehemently at Moxey's; already he have tremble with an impulse

to write something on the subject, and during his journey home a

not prepare for sleep. his brain throb with a congestion of thought;

he struggle to make clear the line on which his satire might direct

itself. by two o'clock he have fling down on paper a conglomerate of

burn idea, and thus relieve he at length go to bed.



two day later come a note from Staple Inn, invite he to meet Malkin

the next evening. by this time he have make a beginning of his critical

essay, and the exordium so far satisfy he that he be tempt to

take it for Earwaker's judgment. but no; well his friend should see

the thing when it be complete.



about eight o'clock he reach the journalist's chamber. Malkin have

not yet arrive. Peak amuse himself with examine certain tropical

product which the traveller have recently cast pell-mell into his

friend's sitting-room. then sound a knock at the door, but it be not

such as would have herald the expected man.



'a telegram,' observe Earwaker, and go to take it in.



he return with hoarse sound of mirth.



'our friend excuse himself. read this characteristic despatch.'



Peak see with surprise that the telegram far exceed familiar

dimension. 'unspeakably grieve,' it begin. 'cannot possibly with you.

at moment's notice undertaken escort two poor girl Rouen. not even

time look in apologise. go via Dieppe and leave Victoria few minute.

Hope be back Thursday. express sincere regret Mr. Peak. Lament

appearance discourtesy. will apologise personally. common humanity

constrain go Rouen. will explain Thursday. no time add another word.

rush ticket train.'



'there you have the man!' cry Earwaker. 'how do you class such a mind

as that? ten to one this be some quixotic obligation he have lay upon

himself, and probably he have go without even a handbag.'



'vocally deliver,' say Peak, 'this would represent a certain stage

of drunkenness. I suppose it ben't open to such an explanation?'



'Malkin never be intoxicate, save with his own vivacity.'



they discuss the singular be with good-natured mirth, then turn

by degree to other topic.



'I have just come across a passage that will delight you,' say

Earwaker, take up a book. 'perhaps you know it.'



he read from Sir Thomas Brown's _Pscudodoxia Epidemica_. '"Men's name

should not only distinguish they. a man should be something that all

man be not, and individual in somewhat beside his proper name. thus,

while it exceed not the bound of reason and modesty, we cannot condemn

singularity. _Nos numerus sumus_ be the motto of the multitude, and for

that reason be they fool."'



Peak laugh his approval.



'it astonish I,' he say, light his pipe, 'that you can go on

writing for this Sunday rag, when you have just as little sympathy with

its aim as I have. do get into some less offensive connection.'



'what paper would you recommend?' ask the other, with his significant

'why need you journalise at all?'



'on the whole, I like it. and remember, to admit that the multitude be

fool be not the same thing as to deny the possibility of progress.'



'do you really believe yourself a democrat, Earwaker?'



'M--m--m! well, yes, I believe the democratic spirit be strong in I

than any other.'



Peak muse for a minute, then suddenly look up.



'and what be I?'



'I be glad nothing much depend on my successfully define you.'



they laugh together.



'I suppose,' say Godwin, 'you cann't call a man a democrat who

recognise in his heart and soul a true distinction of social class.

Social, mark. the division I instinctively support be by no mean

intellectual. the well-bear fool be very often more sure of my respect

than the work man who struggle to a fair measure of education.'



Earwaker would have like to comment on this with remark personal to

the speaker, but he fear to do so. his silence, however, be eloquent

standing, independently of the individual's quality. there's nothing

base. I don't deny the possibility of social advance; I only say that

at present the low class be always disagreeable, often repulsive,

sometimes hateful.'



'I could apply that to the class above they.'



'well, I cann't. but I be quite ready to admit that there be all sort

this come upon I with a rush--a very rare emotion. ben't that

he inquire dubiously, and Earwaker for a moment have no reply but his

'no, it be not democratic,' the journalist decide at length; 'it be

pride of intellect.'



'think so? then look here. if it happen that a whining wretch stop I

in the street to beg, what do you suppose be my feeling? I be ashamed

in the sense of my own prosperity. I cann't look he in the face. if I

yield to my natural impulse, I should cry out, "strike I! spit at

I! show you hate me!--anything but that terrible humiliation of

yourself before I!" that's how I feel. the abasement of which _he_

ben't sensible affect _I_ on his behalf. I give money with what

delicacy I can. if I be oblige to refuse, I mutter apology and hurry

away with burn cheek. what do that mean?'



Earwaker regard he curiously.



'that be mere fineness of humanity.'



'perhaps moral weakness?'



'I don't care for the scalpel of the pessimist. let we give it the

well name.'



Peak have never be so communicative. his progress in composition these

last evening seem to have raise his spirit and spur the

activity of his mind. with a look of pleasure he pursue his

self-analysis.



'special antipathy--sometimes explicable enough--influence I very

widely. now, I by no mean hate all order of uneducated people. a

hedger, a fisherman, a country mason,--people of that kind I rather

could write a paper to show that they be essentially the basest of

english mortal. unhappily, I know so much about they. if I see the

probability of my dying in a London lodging-house, I would go out into

the sweet-scented field and there kill myself.'



Earwaker understand much by this avowal, and wonder whether his

friend desire he so to do.



'well, I cann't say that I have any affection for the race,' he reply.

'I certainly believe that, socially and politically, there be less hope

of they than of the low order in any other part of England.'



'they be damn by the beastly condition of their life!' cry

Godwin, excitedly. 'I don't mean only the slum-denizen. all, all

Hammersmith as much as St. George's-in-the-East. I must write about

this; I must indeed.'



'do by all mean. nothing would benefit you more than to get your soul

into print.'



Peak delay a little, then:



'well, I be do something at last.'



and he give an account of his project essay. by this time his hand

tremble with nervous agitation, and occasionally a dryness of the

palate half choke his voice.



'this may do very well,' opined Earwaker. 'I suppose you will try _the

Critical_?'



'yes. but have I any chance? can a perfectly unknown man hope to get

in?'



they debate this aspect of the matter. see Peak have lay down his

pipe, the journalist offer he tobacco.



'thank; I cann't smoke just yet. it's my misfortune that I cann't talk

earnestly without throw my body into disorder.'



'how stolid I be in comparison!' say Earwaker.



'that book of M'Naughten's,' resume the other, go back to his

subject. 'I suppose the clergy accept it?'



'largely, I believe.'



Peak muse.



'now, if I be a clergyman'--



but his eye meet Earwaker's, and they break into laughter.



'why not?' pursue Godwin. 'do I ever tell you that my people

originally wish to make a parson of I? of course I resist tooth

and nail, but it seem to I now that I be rather foolish in do so.

I wish I _have_ be a parson. in many way the position would have

suit I very well.'



'M--m--m!'



'I be quite serious. well, if I be so placed, I should preach church

dogma, pure and simple. I would have nothing to do with these

consequence should have an immense and enthusiastic congregation.'



'I daresay.'



'depend upon it, let the dogma do what they still can. there's a vast

police force in they, at all event. a man may very strongly defend

himself for preach they.'



the pursuit of this argument lead Earwaker to ask:



'what proportion of the clergy can still take that standing in stolid

conscientiousness?'



'what proportion be convinced that it be untenable?' return Peak.



'many wilfully shut their eye to the truth.'



'no, they don't shut their eye!' cry Godwin. 'they merely lower a

nictitating membrane which permit they to gaze at light without

feel its full impact.'



'I recommend you to bring that into your paper,' say the journalist,

with his deep chuckle.



an hour later they be converse with no less animation, but the talk

be not so critical. Christian Moxey have come up as a topic, and

Earwaker be say that he find it difficult to divine the man's

personality.



'you willn't easily do that,' reply Peak, 'until you know more of his

story. I cann't see that I be bind to secrecy--at all event with you.

Poor Moxey imago that he be in love, and the fancy have last about

ten year.



'ten year?'



'when I first know he he be pay obvious attention to a rather

plain cousin down at Twybridge. why, I don't know, for he certainly be

devoted to a girl here in London. all he have confess to I be that he

have give up hope of she, but that a letter of some sort or other

stay away; the girl very soon marry another man. Less than a year

six month, and presumably live on a hope that the obnoxious husband

may decease. I only know the woman as "Constance"; never see she.'



'so that's Moxey? I begin to understand well.'



'admirable fellow, but deplorably weak. I have an affection for he,

and have have from our first meeting.'



'woman!' muse Earwaker, and shake his head.



'you despise they?'



'on the whole, I'm afraid so.'

the defect of the female mind? it be my belief that this be nothing

he enlarge upon the subject, follow it into all its ramification,

irrationality and coarseness of mind there be an enormous distinction.'



'with coarse mind I have nothing to do.'



'forgive I if I ask you a blunt question,' say Peak, after

hesitate. 'have you ever associate with woman of the high

refinement?'



Earwaker laugh.



'I don't know what that phrase mean. it sound rather odd on your

lip.'



'well, woman of the high class of commoner. with peeress we

needn't concern ourselves.'



'you imagine that social precedence make all that difference in woman?'



'yes, I do. the daughter of a county family be a fine be than any

girl who can spring from the nomad order.'



'even suppose your nomad produce a Rachel or a Charlotte Brontee?'



'we be not talk of genius,' Peak reply.



'it be irrelevant, I know.--Well, yes, I _have_ converse now and then

Peak's face express annoyance, and he say nothing.



'a man's wife,' pursue Earwaker, 'may be his superior in whatever you

myself in an agony of shame.'



a burst of laughter clear the air for a moment, but for a moment

'there be man whose character would defy that rule.'



'yes--to their own disaster. but I ought to have make one exception.

there be a case in which a woman will marry without much regard to her

Peak try to smile. he make no answer, and fall into a fit of brooding.



'what's all this about?' ask the journalist, when he too have muse

'no one's.'



the suspicion be inevitable.



'if it be true, perhaps you would be justify in mistrust my way

of regard these thing. but it's the natural tendency of my mind. if

'don't malign your parent, old fellow. they give you a brain inferior

bitter side.'



but the compliment have excite Peak, and after a moment's delay he

'I have no other ambition in life--no other! think the confession as

at marry a lady.'



the last word be fling out defiantly. he quiver as he speak, and

'one question. you seriously believe that you could find satisfaction

'what life?' ask Peak, impatiently.



'that of an average gentleman, let we say, with house in town and

'I could enjoy the good and throw aside the distasteful.'

'it would not be _crass_, to begin with. if her religion be genuine,

'then you mustn't look for she in society!' cry Earwaker.



'I don't care; where you will, so long as she have always live among

people of breeding and high education, and never have her thought

soil with the vile contact of poverty.'



Earwaker start up and reach a volume from a shelf. quickly find

the desire page, he begin to read aloud:



'dear, have the world in its caprice deign to proclaim--I know you

both, have recognise your plight troth, be sponsor for you; live in

peace!'--



he read to the end of the poem, and then look up with an admire

smile.



'an ideal!' exclaim Peak. 'an ideal akin to Murger's and Musset's

grisette, who never exist.'

Peak rise, saunter about the room for a minute or two, then say:



'I have just get a title for my paper. I shall call it "the New

Sophistry."'



'do very well, I should think,' reply the other, smile. 'will you

let I see it when it's do?'



'who know if I shall finish it? nothing I ever undertake have be

about Malkin.'



in a week's time Godwin receive another summon to Staple Inn, with

promise of Malkin's assured presence. in reply he write:



'owe to a new arrangement at Bates's, I start tomorrow for my holiday

in Cornwall, so cannot see you for a few week. please offer Malkin my

apology; make they (I mean it) as profuse as those he telegraph.

herewith I send you my paper, "the New Sophistry", which I have write

worth while, will you have the kindness to send it for I to _the

enough to claim my glory. if it seem to you to have no chance, keep it

till I return, as I hate the humiliation of refusals.--Don't think I

make an ass of myself the other night. we will never speak on that

don't regret the indiscretion.'







CHAPTER III





'_Pereunt et imputantur_.'



Godwin Peak read the motto beneath the clock in Exeter Cathedral, and

believe it of christian origin. have he know that the word be find

in Martial, his rebellious spirit would have enjoy the consecration

of a phrase from such an unlikely author. even as he must have laugh

have he stand in the Vatican before the figure of those two greek

dramatist who, for age, be revere as christian saint.



his ignorance preserve he from a clash of sentiment. this afternoon

Architecture have no special interest for he, and the history of church

or faith could seldom touch his emotion; but the glorious handiwork of

'_Pereunt et imputantur_.'



he sit down where the soft, slow ticking of the clock could guide his
