his character find nourishment and range; here he be humanise, make

receptive of all gentle sympathy. heroism might point he to an

unending struggle with adverse condition, but how be heroism possible

without faith? absolute faith he have none; he be essentially a

negativist, guide by the mere relation of phenomena. nothing easy

than to contemn the mode of life represent by this wealthy middle

class; but compare it with other existence conceivable by a thinking

man, and it be emphatically good. it aim at placidity, at

benevolence, at supreme cleanliness,--things which more than

compensate for the absence of high spirituality. we can be but what

we be; these people accept themselves, and in so do become

estimable mortal. no imbecile pretension expose they to the rebuke

of a social satirist; no vulgarity taint their familiar intercourse.

their allegiance to a wear-out creed be feel as an add grace; thus

only could their soul aspire, and the imperfect poetry of their

nature be develop.



he take an opportunity of seat himself by Mrs. Warricombe, with whom

as yet he have hold no continuous dialogue.



'has there be anything of interest at the London theatre lately?'

she ask.



'I know so little of they,' Godwin reply, truthfully. 'it must be

several year since I see a play.'



'then in that respect you have hardly become a Londoner.'



'nor in any other, I believe,' say Peak, with a smile. 'I have live

there ten year, but be far from regard London as my home. I hope a

few month more will release I from it altogether.'



'Indeed!--Perhaps you think of leave England?'



'I should be very sorry to do that--for any length of time. my wish be

to settle somewhere in the country, and spend a year or two in quiet

study.'



Mrs. Warricombe look amiable surprise, but correct herself to

approve interest.



'I have hear some of our friend say that their mind get unstrung, if

they be long away from town, but I should have think that country

quietness would be much well than London noise. my husband certainly

find it so.'



'People be very differently constitute,' say Godwin. 'and then it

depend much on the nature of one's work.'



utter these commonplace with an air of reflection, he observe that

they do not cost he the self-contempt which be willnot to be his

penalty for concession to the term of polite gossip; rather, his mind

accept with gratitude this rare repose. he taste something of the

tranquil self-content which make life so enjoyable when one have never

see a necessity for shape original remark. no one in this room

would despise he for a platitude, be it but recommend with a

pleasant smile. with the Moxeys, with Earwaker, he durst not thus have

speak.



when the hour of separation be at hand, Buckland invite his guest to

retire with he to a part of the house where they could smoke and chat

comfortably.



'Moorhouse and Louis be fag after their twenty mile stretch this

morning; I have catch both of they nod during the last few

minute. we can send they to bed without apology.'



he lead the way upstairs to a region of lumber-room, whence a narrow

flight of step bring they into a glass-house, octangular and with

point top, out upon the roof. this, he explain, have be build

some twenty year ago, at a time when Mr. Warricombe amuse himself

with photography. a few indication of its original purpose be still

noticeable; an easel and a box of oil-colour show that

someone--doubtless of the young generation--have use it as a

painting-room; a settee and deep cane chair make it an invite lounge

on a warm evening like the present, when, by throw open a hinged

wall, one look forth into the deep sky and taste the air from the

sea.



'Sidwell use to paint a little,' say Buckland, as his companion bent

to examine a small canvas on which a landscape be rough in. it lie

on a side table, and be half conceal by an ordnance map, left

unfolded. 'for the last year or two I think she have give it up. I'm

afraid we be not strong in matter of art. neither of the girl can

play very well, though of course they both tinkle for their own

amusement. Maurice--the poor lad who be kill--give a good deal of

artistic promise; father keep some little water-colour of his, which

man in that line have praise--perhaps sincerely.'



'I remember you use to speak slightingly of art,' say Godwin, as he

take an offer cigar.



'do I? and of a good many other thing, I daresay. it be my habit at

one time, I believe, to grow heated in scorn of Euclid's definition.

what an interesting book Euclid be! half a year ago, I be lead by a

talk with Moorhouse to go through some of the old "prop", and you

cann't imagine how they delight I. Moorhouse be so obliging as to

tell I that I have an eminently deductive mind.'



he laugh, but not without betray some pleasure in the remark.



'surprising,' he go on, 'how very little such a mind as Moorhouse's

suggest itself in common conversation. he be really profound in

mathematic, a man of original power, but I never hear he make a

remark of the slight value on any other subject. now his sister--she

have study nothing in particular, yet she cann't express an opinion

that don't bear the stamp of originality.'



Godwin be content to muse, his eye fix on a brilliant star in the

western heaven.



'there's only one inconsistency in she that annoy and puzzle I,'

Buckland pursue, speak with the cigar in his mouth. 'in religion,

she seem to be orthodox. true, we have never speak on the subject,

but--well, she go to church, and carry prayer-book. I don't know

how to explain it. Hypocrisy be the last thing one could suspect she

of. I'm sure she hate it in every form. and such a clear brain!--I

cann't understand it.'



the listener be still star-gazing. he have allow his cigar, after the

first few puff, to smoulder untasted; his lip be draw into an

expression very unlike the laxity appropriate to pleasurable smoking.

when the murmur of the pine have for a moment be audible, he say,

with a forced smile:



'I notice you take for grant that a clear brain and religious

orthodoxy be incompatible.'



the other give he a keen look.



'hardly,' be Buckland's reply, speak with less ingenuousness of tone

than usual. 'I say that Miss Moorhouse have undeniably a strong mind,

and that it be impossible to suspect she of the slight hypocrisy.'



'whence the puzzle that keep you occupy,' rejoin Peak, in a voice

that sound like assumption of superiority, though the accent have an

agreeable softness.



Warricombe move as if impatiently, strike a match to rekindle his

weed, blow tumultuous cloud, and finally put a blunt question:



'what do you think about it yourself?'



'from my point of view, there be no puzzle at all,' Godwin reply, in

a very clear voice, smile as he meet the other's look.



'how be I to understand that?' ask Buckland, good-naturedly, though

with a knitting of his brow.



'not as a doubt of Miss Moorhouse's sincerity. I cann't see that a

belief in the christian religion be exclude by any degree of

intellectual clearness.'



'no--your view have change, Peak?'



'on many subject, this among they.'



'I see.'



the word fall as if involuntarily from Warricombe's lip. he gaze at

the floor awhile, then, suddenly look up, exclaim:



'it would be civil to accept this without surprise, but it be too much

for I. how have it come about?'



'that would take I a long time to explain.'



'then,' pursue his companion, watch he closely, 'you be quite in

sympathy with that exposition you give at lunch today?'



'quite. I hope there be nothing in my way of speak that make you

think otherwise?'



'nothing at all. I couldn't help wonder what it mean. you seem

perfectly in earnest, yet such talk have the oddest sound on your

lip--to I, I mean. of course I think of you as I use to know you.'



'naturally.' Peak be now in an attitude of repose, his leg cross,

thumb and forefinger stroke his chin. 'I couldn't very well turn

aside to comment on my own mental history.'



here again be the note of something like genial condescension.

Buckland seem sensible of it, and slightly raise his eyebrow.



'I be to understand that you have become strictly orthodox in matter

of religious faith?'



'the proof be,' reply Godwin, 'that I hope before long to take

Orders.'



again there be silence, and again the sea-breath make its whispering

in the pine. Warricombe, with a sudden gesture, point towards the

sky.



'a shooting star--one of the bright I ever see!'



'I miss it,' say Peak, just glance in that direction.



the interruption enable Buckland to move his chair; in this new

position he be somewhat far from Peak, and have a well view of

his face.



'I should never have imagine you a clergyman,' he say, thoughtfully,

'but I can see that your mind have be develop power in that

direction.--Well, so be it! I can only hope you have find your true

work in life.'



'but you doubt it?'



'I cann't say that I doubt it, as I cann't understand you. to be sure, we

have be part for many year. in some respect I must seem much

changed'--



'greatly change,' Godwin put in, promptly.



'yes,' pursue the other, correctively, 'but not in a way that would

seem incredible to anyone whatever. I be conscious of growth in

tolerance, but my attitude in essential be unchanged. think of

you--as I have often enough do--I always keep the impression you make

on I when we be both lad; you seem most distinctly a modern

mind--one of the most modern that ever come under my notice. now, I

don't find it impossible to understand my father, when he reconcile

science with religion; he be bear sixty year ago. but Godwin Peak as

a--a--'



'Parson,' supply Peak, drily.



'yes, as a parson--I shall have to meditate much before I grasp the

notion.'



'perhaps you have drop your philosophical study?' say Godwin,

with a smile of courteous interest.



'I don't know. Metaphysics have no great interest for I, but I

philosophise in a way. I think myself a student of human nature, at

all event.'



'but you haven't keep up with philosophical speculation on the point

involve in orthodox religion?'



'I confess my ignorance of everything of the kind--unless you include

Bishop Blougram among the philosopher?'



Godwin bear the gaze which accompany this significant inquiry. for a

moment he smile, but there follow an expression of gravity touch

with pain.



'I haven't think of broach this matter,' he say, with slow

utterance, but still in a tone of perfect friendliness. 'let we put it

aside.'



Warricombe seem to make an effort, and his next word have the accent

of well-breed consideration which distinguish his ordinary talk.



'pray forgive my bad joke. I merely mean that I have no right whatever

to argue with anyone who have give serious attention to such thing.

they be altogether beyond my sphere. I be bear an agnostic, and no

subtlety of demonstration could incline I for a moment to theological

view; my intellect refuse to admit a single preliminary of such

argument. you astonish I, and that's all I be justify in say.'



'my dear Warricombe, you be justify in say whatever your mind

suggest. that be one of the principle which I hold unaltered--let I

be quite frank with you. I should never have decide upon such a step

as this, but for the fact that I have manage to put by a small sum of

money which will make I independent for two or three year. till quite

lately I haven't a thought of use my freedom in this way; it be clear

to I that I must throw over the old drudgery at Rotherhithe, but this

resolve which astonish you have not yet ripen--I see it only as one

of the possibility of my life. well, now, it's only too true that

there's something of speculation in my purpose; I look to the Church,

not only as a congenial sphere of activity, but as a means of

subsistence. in a man of no fortune this be inevitable; I hope there be

nothing to be ashamed of. even if the condition of the case allow

it, I shouldn't present myself for ordination forthwith; I must study

and prepare myself in quietness. how the practical detail will be

arrange, I cann't say; I have no family influence, and I must hope to

make friend who will open a way for I. I have always live apart from

society; but that ben't natural to I, and it become more distasteful

the old I grow. the probability be that I shall settle somewhere in

the country, where I can live decently on a small income. after all,

it's well I should have let you know this at once. I only realise a

few minute ago that to be silent about my project be in a way to be

guilty of false pretence.'



the adroitness of this last remark, which direct itself, with such

show of candour, against a suspicion precisely the opposite of that

likely to be entertain by the listener, succeed in disarm

Warricombe; he look up with a smile of reassurance, and speak

encouragingly.



'about the practical detail I don't think you need have any anxiety.

it ben't every day that the Church of England get such a recruit. let

I suggest that you have a talk with my father.'



Peak reflect on the proposal, and reply to it with grave

thoughtfulness:



'that's very kind of you, but I should have a difficulty in ask Mr.

Warricombe's advice. I'm afraid I must go on in my own way for a time.

it will be a few month, I daresay, before I can release myself from my

engagement in London.'



'but I be to understand that your mind be really make up?'



'oh, quite!'



'well, no doubt we shall have opportunity of talk. we must meet in

town, if possible. you have excite my curiosity, and I cann't help

hope you'll let I see a little far into your mind some day. when

I first get hold of Newman's _Apologia_, I begin to read it with the

utmost eagerness, flatter myself that now at length I should

understand how a man of brain could travel such a road. I be horribly

disappointed, and not a little enraged, when I find that he begin by

assume the very belief I think he be go to justify. in you I

shall hope for more logic.'



'Newman be incapable of understand such an objection,' say Peak,

with a look of amusement.



'but you be not.'



the dialogue grow chatty. when they exchange good-night, Peak fancy

that the pressure of Buckland's hand be less fervent than at their

meeting, but his manner no long seem to indicate distrust. probably

the agnostic's mood be one of half-tolerant disdain.



Godwin turn the key in his bedroom door, and stray aimlessly about.

he be fatigue, but the white, fragrant bed do not yet invite he; a

turbulence in his brain give warn that it would be long before he

sleep. he wind up his watch; the hand point to twelve. chance to

come before the mirror, he see that he be unusually pale, and that his

eye have a swollen look.



the profound stillness be oppressive to he; he start nervously at

an undefined object in a dim corner, and go nearer to examine it; he

be irritable, vaguely discontent, and have even a moment of nausea,

perhaps the result of tobacco strong than he be accustom to smoke.

after lean for five minute at the open window, he feel a soothe

effect from the air, and could think consecutively of the day's event.

what have happen seem to he incredible; it be as though he revive

a mad dream, of ludicrous coherence. since his display of rhetoric at

luncheon all be downright somnambulism. what fatal power have subdue

he? what extraordinary influence have guide his tongue, constrain

his feature? his conscious self have have no part in all this comedy;

now for the first time be he take count of the character he have

play.



have he be tell this morning that--why, what monstrous folly be all

this? into what unspeakable baseness have he fall? happily, he have but

to take leave of the Warricombe household, and rush into some region

where he be unknown. year hence, he would relate the story to

Earwaker.



for a long time he suffer the torment of this awakening. Shame

buffet he on the right cheek and the left; he look about like one

who slink from merited chastisement. oh, thrice ignoble varlet! to

pose with unctuous hypocrisy before people who have welcome he under

their roof, unquestione, with all the grace and kindliness of English

hospitality! to lie shamelessly in the face of his old fellow-student,

who have be so genuinely glad to meet he again!



yet such possibility have not be unforeseen. at the time of his

profound gloom, when solitude and desire crush his spirit, he have

wish that fate would afford he such an opportunity of knavish

success. his imagination have play with the idea that a man like

himself might well be drive to this expedient, and might even use it

with life-long result. of a certainty, the Church number such man

among her priests,--not mere lukewarm sceptic who make religion a

source of income, nor yet those who have honestly enter the portal and

by necessity be hold from withdraw, though their conviction have

change; but deliberate schemer from the first, ambitious but hungry

nature, keen-sight, unscrupulous. and they be at no loss to defend

themselves against the attack of conscience. life be a terrific

struggle for all who begin it with no endowment save their brain. a

hypocrite be not necessarily a harm-doer; easy to picture the

unbelieving priest whose influence be vastly for good, in word and

deed.



but he, he who have ever pride himself on his truth-front intellect,

and have freely utter his scorn of the credulous mob! he who be his

own criterion of moral right and wrong! no wonder he feel like a

whip cur. it be the ancestral vice in his blood, bring out by

over-tempt circumstance. the long line of base-bear predecessor,

the grovel hind and mechanic of his genealogy, be responsible

for this. oh for a name wherewith honour be hereditary!



his eye be blind by a rush of hot tear. down, down--into the

depth of uttermost despondency, of self-pity and self-contempt! have it

be practicable, he would have flee from the house, leave its

occupant to think of he as they would; even as, ten year ago, he have

flee from the shame impend over he at Kingsmill. a cowardly

instinct, this; having once act upon it give to his whole life a

taint of craven meanness. mere bluster, all his talk of mental dignity

and uncompromise scorn of superstition. a weak and idle man, whose

good year be already waste!



he gaze deliberately at himself in the glass, at his red eyelid and

unsightly lip. darkness be good; perhaps he might forget his shame

for an hour or two, ere the dawn renew it. he throw off his garment

heedlessly, extinguish the lamp, and creep into the ready

hiding-place.









Part III







CHAPTER I





'why be you obstinately silent? [write Earwaker, in a letter address

to Godwin at his Peckham lodging]. I take it for grant that you must

by this time be back from your holiday. why haven't you reply to my

letter of a fortnight ago? nothing yet from _the Critical_. if you be

really at work as usual, come and see I to-morrow evening, any time

after eight. the posture of my affair grow dubious; the shadow of

Kenyon thicken about I. in all seriousness I think I shall be drive

from _the Weekly Post_ before long. my quarrel with Runcorn be too

frequent, and his blackguardism keep more than pace with the time.

come or write, for I want to know how thing go with you.



_Tuissimus_, J.E.E.'



Peak read this at breakfast on a Saturday morning. it be early in

September, and three week have elapse since his return from the west

of England. upon the autumn have fall a blight of cold and rainy

weather, which do not enhance the cheerfulness of daily journeying

between Peckham Rye and Rotherhithe. when it be necessary for he to

set forth to the train, he mutter imprecation, for a mood of

inactivity possess he; he would gladly have stay in his

comfortable sitting-room, idle over book or only occupy with

languid thought.



in the afternoon he be at liberty to follow his impulse, and this

direct he to the British Museum, whither of late he have several

time resort as a reader. among the half-dozen book for which he

apply be one in German, Reusch's _Bibel und Natur_. after a little

dally, he became absorb in this work, and two or three hour

pass before its hold on his attention slacken. he seldom change

his position; the volume be prop against other, and he sit bend

forward, his arm fold upon the desk. when he be thus deeply

engage, his face have a hard, stern aspect; if by chance his eye

wander for a moment, its look seem to express resentment of

interruption.



at length he throw himself back with a sudden yielding to weariness,

cross his leg, sink together in the chair, and for half-an-hour

brood darkly. a fit of yawning admonish he that it be time to

quit the atmosphere of study. he betake himself to a restaurant in the

Strand, and thence about eight o'clock make his way to Staple Inn,

where the journalist give he cheerful welcome.



'day after day I have mean to write,' thus he excuse himself. 'but I

have really nothing to say.'



'you don't look any well for your holiday,' Earwaker remark.



'Holiday? oh, I have forget all about it. when do _you_ go?'



'the situation be comical. I feel sure that if I leave town, my

connection with the _post_ will come to an end. I shall have a note

from Runcorn say that we have well take this opportunity of

terminate my engagement. on the whole I should be glad, yet I cann't

make up my mind to be oust by Kenyon--that's what it mean. they want

to get I away, but I stick on, postpone holiday from week to week.

Runcorn cann't decide to send I about my business, yet every leader I

write enrage he. but for Kenyon, I should gain my point; I feel sure

of it. it's one of those case in which homicide would be justify by

public interest. if Kenyon get my place, the paper become at once an

organ of ruffiandom, the delight of the blackguardry.'



'how's the circulation?' inquire Peak.



'pretty sound; that add to the joke. this series of story by

Doubleday have help we a good deal, and my contention be, if we can

keep financially right by help of this kind, why not make a little

sacrifice for the sake of raise our political tone? Runcorn willn't see

it; he listen eagerly to Kenyon's assurance that we might sell several

thousand more by strike the true pot-house note.'



'then pitch the thing over! wash your hand, and go to clean work.'



'the work I be do be clean enough,' reply Earwaker. 'let I have

my way, and I can make the paper a decent one and a useful one. I

shalln't easily find another such chance.'



'your idealism have a strong root,' say Godwin, rather contemptuously.

'I half envy you. there must be a distinct pleasure in believe that

any intellectual influence will exalt the english democracy.'



'I'm not sure that I do believe it, but I enjoy the experiment. the

chief pleasure, I suppose, be in fight Runcorn and Kenyon.'



'they be too strong for you, Earwaker. they have the spirit of the age

to back they up.'



the journalist become silent; he smile, but the harassment of conflict

mark his feature.



'I hear nothing about "the New Sophistry",' he remark, when Godwin

have begin to examine some book that lie on the table. 'Dolby have the

trick of keep manuscript a long time. everything that seem at the

first glance tolerable, he send to the printer, then muse over it at

his leisure. probably your paper be in type.'



'I don't care a rap whether it be or not. what do you think of this

book of Oldwinkle's?'



he be hold a volume of humorous story, which have greatly take

the fancy of the public.



'it's uncommonly good,' reply the journalist, laugh. 'I have a

prejudice against the fellow, but he have overcome I. it's more than

good farce--something like really strong humour here and there.'



'I quite believe it,' say Peak, 'yet I couldn't read a page. whatever

the mob enjoy be at once spoilt for I, however good I should

otherwise think it. I be sick of see and hear the man's name.'



Earwaker shake his head in deprecation.



'Narrow, my boy. one must be able to judge and enjoy impartially.'



'I know it, but I shall never improve. this book seem to I to have a

bad smell; it looks maul with dirty finger. I despise Oldwinkle for

his popularity. to make they laugh, and to laugh _with_ they--pah!'



they debate this point for some time, Peak grow more violent,

though his friend preserve a smile equanimity. a tirade of virulent

contempt, in which Godwin exhibit all his power of savage eloquence,

be break by a visitor's summon at the door.



'here's Malkin,' say the journalist; 'you'll see each other at last.'



Peak could not at once command himself to the look and tone desirable

in meet a stranger; lean against the mantelpiece, he gaze with a

scowl of curiosity at the man who present himself, and when he shake

hand, it be in silence. but Malkin make speech from the other

unnecessary for several minute. with animate voice and gesture, he

pour forth apology for his failure to keep the appointment of six

or seven week ago.



'only the grave call of duty could have keep I away, I do assure

you! no doubt Earwaker have inform you of the circumstance. I

telegraph--I think I telegraph; don't I, Earwaker?'



'I have some recollection of a word or two of scant excuse,' reply

the journalist.



'but I implore you to consider the haste I be in,' cry Malkin; 'not

five minute, Mr. Peak, to book, to register luggage, to do everything;

not five minute, I protest! but here we be at last. let we talk! let

we talk!'



he seat himself with an air of supreme enjoyment, and begin to cram

the bowl of a large pipe from a bulky pouch.



'how stand the fight with Kenyon and Co.?' he cry, as soon as the

tobacco be glow.



Earwaker briefly repeat what he have tell Peak.



'hold out! no surrender and no compromise! what's your opinion, Mr

Peak, on the abstract question? be a popular paper likely, or not, to

be damage in its circulation by improvement of style and tone--within

the limit of discretion?'



'I shouldn't be surprised if it be,' Peak answer, drily.



'I'm afraid you're right. there's no use in blink truth, however

disagreeable. but, for Earwaker, that ben't the main issue. what he have

to do be to assert himself. every man's first duty be to assert

himself. at all event, this be how I regard the matter. I be all for

individualism, for the development of one's personality at whatever

cost. no compromise on point of faith! Earwaker have his ideal of

journalistic duty, and in a fight with fellow like Runcorn and Kenyon

he must stand firm as a rock.'



'I cann't see that he's call upon to fight at all,' say Peak. 'he's

in a false position; let he get out of it.'



'a false position? I cann't see that. no man well fit than Earwaker

to raise the tone of radical journalism. here's a big Sunday newspaper

practically in his hand; it seem to I that the circumstance give

he a grand opportunity of make his force feel. what be we all

seek but an opportunity for strike out with effect?'



Godwin listen with a sceptical smile, and make answer in slow,

careless tone.



'Earwaker happen to be employ and pay by certain capitalist to

increase the sale of their paper.'



'my dear sir!' cry the other, bounce upon his seat. 'how can you

take such a view? a great newspaper surely cannot be regard as a mere

source of income. these capitalist declare that they have at heart the

interest of the work class; so have Earwaker, and he be far well

able than they to promote those interest. his duty be to apply their

money to the good use, morally speak. if he be lukewarm in the

matter, I should be the first to advise his retirement; but this fight

be entirely congenial to he. I trust he will hold his own to the last

possible moment.'



'you must remember,' put in the journalist, with a look of amusement,

'that Peak have no sympathy with Radicalism.'



'I lament it, but that do not affect my argument. if you be a high

Tory, I should urge you just as strongly to assert yourself. surely you

agree with this point of mine, Mr. Peak? you admit that a man must

develop whatever strength be in he.'



'I'm not at all sure of that.'



Malkin fix himself sideways in the chair, and examine his

collocutor's face earnestly. he endeavour to subdue his excitement to

the tone of courteous debate, but the word that at length escape he

be humorously blunt.



'then of what _be_ you sure?'



'of nothing.'



'now we touch bottom!' cry Malkin. 'philosophically speak, I agree

with you. but we have to live our life, and I suppose we must direct

ourselves by some conscious principle.'



'I don't see the necessity,' Peak reply, still in an impassive tone.

'we may very well be guide by circumstance as they arise. to be sure,

there's a principle in that, but I take it you mean something

different.'



'yes I do. I hold that the will must direct circumstance, not receive

its impulse from they. how, then, be we to be guide? what do you set

before yourself?'



'to get through life with as much satisfaction and as little pain as

possible.'



'you be a hedonist, then. well and good! then that be your conscious

principle'--



'no, it ben't.'



'how be I to understand you?'



'by recognise that a man's intellectual and moral principle as

likely as not tend to anything but his happiness.'



'I cann't admit it!' exclaim Malkin, leap from his chair. 'what

_be_ happiness?'



'I don't know.'



'Earwaker, _what_ be happiness? what _be_ happiness?'



'I really don't know,' answer the journalist, mirthfully.



'this be trifle with a grave question. we all know perfectly well

that happiness be the conscious exertion of individual power. why be

there so much suffering under our present social system? because the

majority of man be crush to a dead level of mechanical toil, with no

opportunity of develop their special faculty. give a man scope,

and happiness be put within his reach.'



'what do you mean by scope?' inquire Godwin.



'scope? scope? why, room to expand. the vice of our society be

hypocrisy; it come of over-crowding. when a man ben't allow to be

himself, he take refuge in a mean imitation of those other man who

appear to be well off. that be what send I off to South America. I

get into politic, and find that I be in danger of grow dishonest,

of compromise, and toadying. in the wilderness, I find myself

again.--Do you seriously believe that happiness can be obtain by

ignore one's conviction?'



he address the question to both, snuff the air with head throw

back.



'what if you have no conviction?' ask Peak.



'then you be incapable of happiness in any worthy sense! you may

graze, but you will never feast.'



the listener join in laughter, and Malkin, after a moment's

hesitation, allow his face to relax in good-humour sympathy.



'now look here!' he cry. 'you--Earwaker; suppose you send conscience

to the devil, and set yourself to please Runcorn by increase the

circulation of your paper by whatever mean. you would flourish,

undoubtedly. in a short time you would be chief editor, and your

pocket would burst with money. but what about your peace of mind? what

about happiness?'



'why, I'm disposed to agree with Peak,' answer the journalist. 'if I

_could_ take that line, I should be a happy man than

conscientiousness will ever make I.'



Malkin swell with indignation.



'you don't mean it! you be turn a grave argument into

jest!--Where's my hat? where the devil be my hat? send for I again

when you be disposed to talk seriously.'



he stride towards the door, but Earwaker arrest he with a shout.



'you're leave your pipe!'



'so I be. where be it?--Did I tell you where I buy this pipe?'



'no. what's the wood?'



on the instant Malkin fall into a cheerful vein of reminiscence. in

five minute he be give a rapturous description of tropical scene,

laugh joyously as he address now one now the other of his

companion.



'I hear you have a mind to see those country, Mr. Peak,' he say at

length. 'if you care for a travel companion--rather short-temper,

but you'll pardon that--pray give I the preference. I should enjoy

above all thing to travel with a man of science.'



'it's very doubtful whether I shall ever get so far,' Godwin reply,

musingly.



and, as he speak, he rise to take leave. Earwaker's protest that it be

not yet ten o'clock do not influence he.



'I want to reflect on the meaning of happiness,' he say, extend his

hand to Malkin; and, in spite of the smile, his face have a sombre cast.



the two who be leave of course discuss he.



'you willn't care much for Peak,' say Earwaker. 'he and I suit each

other, because there's a good deal of indifferentism in both of we.

moral earnestness always go against the grain with he; I've notice

it frequently.'



'I'm sorry I speak so dogmatically. it ben't altogether good manner.

suppose I write he a short letter, just express my regret for

having be lead away'--



'needless, needless,' laugh the journalist. 'he think all the well

of you for your zeal. but happiness be a sore point with he; few man,

I should think, have know less of it. I cann't imagine any

circumstance which would make he thoroughly at peace with himself and

the world.'



'poor fellow! you can see something of that in his face. why don't he

get marry?'



'a remarkable suggestion!--By the way, why don't _you_?'.



'my dear boy, there's nothing I wish more, but it's a business of such

fearful precariousness. I'm one of those man whom marriage will either

make or ruin. you know my characteristic; the slight check upon my

independence, and all's up with I. the woman I marry must be perfectly

reasonable, perfectly good-temper; she must have excellent education,

and every delicacy of breeding. where be I to find this paragon?'



'society be open to you.'



'true, but I be not open to society. I don't take kindly to the people

of my own class. no, I tell you what--my only chance of get a

suitable wife be to train some very young girl for the purpose. don't

misunderstand I, for heaven's sake! I mean that I must make a

friendship with some schoolgirl in whose education I can have a voice,

whose relative will permit I to influence her mind and develop her

character. what do you think of this idea?'



'not bad, but it demand patience.'



'and who more patient than I? but let we talk of that poor Mrs. Jacox

and her girl. you feel that you know they pretty well from my letter,

don't you? nothing more monstrous can be imagine than the treatment to

which this poor woman have be subject! I couldn't have believe that

such dishonesty and brutality be possible in english family of

decent position. her husband desert she, her brother rob she, her

sister-in-law libel her,--the whole story be nauseate!'



'you're quite sure that she tell you the truth?'



Malkin glare with sudden resentment.



'the truth? what! you also desire to calumniate she? for shame,

Earwaker! a poor widow toil to support herself in a foreign country,

with two child dependent on she.'



'yes, yes, yes; but you seem to know very little of she.'



'I know she perfectly, and all her circumstance!'



Mrs. Jacox be the mother of the two girl whom Malkin have escort to

Rouen, after an hour or so of all but casual acquaintance. she and her

history have come in a very slight degree under the notice of certain

good-natured people with whom Malkin be on friendly term, and hear

that the child, Bella and Lily, aged fourteen and twelve

respectively, be about to undertake alone a journey to the continent,

the erratic hero feel it incumbent upon he to see they safe at their

mother's side. instead of return forthwith, he linger in Normandy

for several week, strike off at length, on the summon of a friend,

to Orleans, whence he be only to-day return. two or three letter

have keep Earwaker inform of his movement. of Mrs. Jacox he write as

he now speak, with compassionate respect, and the girl, accord to

he, be exquisite model of bud maidenhood.



'you haven't tell I,' say Earwaker, calmly front the indignant

outburst, 'what her circumstance be--at present.'



'she assist an english lady in the management of a boardinghouse,'

Malkin reply, with an air which forbid trivial comment. 'Bella and

Lily will of course continue their study. I daresay I shall run over

now and then to see they.'



'may I, without offence, inquire if either of these young lady seem

suitable for the ideal training of which you speak?'



Malkin smile thoughtfully. he stand with his leg apart and stroke

his blond beard.



'the surmise be not unnatural. well, I confess that Bella have inspire

I with no little interest. she be rather mature, unfortunately; I wish

she have be Lily's age. we shall see; we shall see.'



Musing, he refill his pipe, and gossip be prolong till something

after one o'clock. Malkin be never know to retire willingly from an

evening's congenial talk until the small hour be in progress.



Peak, on reach home about eleven, be surprised to see a light in

his sit-room window. as he enter, his landlady inform he that

Mr. Moxey have be wait upstairs for an hour or two. Christian be

read. he lay down the book and rise languidly. his face be

flushed, and he speak with a laugh which suggest that a fit of

despondency (as occasionally happen) have tempt he to excess in

cordial. Godwin understand these sign. he know that his friend's

intellect be rather brighten than impair by such stimulus, and he

affect not to be conscious of any peculiarity.



'as you wouldn't come to I,' Christian begin, 'I have no choice but to

come to you. my visit ben't unwelcome, I hope?'



'certainly not. but how be you go to get home? you know the time?'



'don't trouble. I shalln't go to bed to-night. let I sit here and read,

will you? if I feel tired I can lie down on the sofa. what a delightful

book this be! I must get it.'



it be a history of the italian Renaissance, recently publish.



'where do this phrase come from?' he continue, point to a scrap

of paper, use as a book-mark, on which Godwin have pencil a note.

the word be: '_Foris ut moris, intus ut libet_.'



'it's mention there,' Peak reply, 'as the motto of those humanist

who outwardly conform to the common faith.'



'I see. all very well when the inquisition be flourish, but sound

ignoble nowadays.'



'do you think so? in a half-civilised age, whether the sixteenth or the

nineteenth century, a wise man may do bad than adopt it.'



'well be honest, surely?'



Peak stand for a moment as if in doubt, then exclaim irritably:



'honest? honest? who be or can be honest? who truly declare himself?

when a man have learn that truth be indeterminable, how be it more

moral to go about cry that you don't believe a certain dogma than to

concede that the dogma may possibly be true? this new morality of the

agnostic be mere paltry conceit. why must I make solemn declaration

that I don't believe in absolute knowledge? I might as well be call

upon to inform all my acquaintance how I stand with regard to the

theory of chemical affinity. one's philosophy have nothing to do with

the business of life. if I choose to become a Church of England

clergyman, what moral objection could be make?'



this illustration be so amusing to Moxey, that his surprise at what

precede give way to laughter.



'I wonder,' he exclaim, 'that you never seriously think of a

profession for which you be so evidently cut out.'



Godwin keep silence; his face have darken, and he seat himself with

sullen weariness.



'tell I what you've be do,' resume Moxey. 'why haven't I hear

from you?'



'I should have come in a day or two. I think you be probably out of

town.'



'her husband be ill,' say the other, by way of reply. he lean

forward with his arm upon the table, and gaze at Godwin with eye of

peculiar brightness.



'Ill, be he?' return Godwin, with slow interest. 'in the same way as

before?'



'yes, but much bad.'



Christian pause; and when he again speak it be hurriedly, confusedly.



'how can I help get excited about it? how can I behave decently?

you're the only man I ever speak to on the subject, and no doubt I both

weary and disgust you; but I _must_ speak to some one. my nerve be

string beyond endurance; it's only by speak that I can ease myself

from the intolerable strain.'



'have you see she lately?'



'yesterday, for a moment, in the street. it's ten month since the last

meeting.'



'well,' remark Godwin, abruptly, 'it's probable the man will die one

of these day, then your trial will have a happy end. I see no harm in

hope that his life may be short--that's a conventional feeling. if

two people can be benefit by the death of a single person, why

shouldn't we be glad in the prospect of his dying? not of his

suffering--that's quite another thing. but die he must; and to curtail

the life of a being who at length wholly cease to exist be no injury.

you cann't injure a nonentity. do you think I should take it ill if I

know that some person be wish my death? why, look, if ever I

crush a little green fly that crawl upon I in the field, at once I

be fill with envy of its fate--sincere envy. to have pass so

suddenly from be into nothingness--how bless an extinction! to

feel in that way, instinctively, in the very depth of your soul, be to

be a true pessimist. if I have ever doubt my sincerity in pessimism,

this experience, several time repeat, would have reassure I.'



Christian cover his face, and brood for a long time, whilst Godwin

sit with his eye on vacancy.



'come and see we to-morrow,' say the former, at length.



'perhaps.';



'why do you keep away?'



'I'm in no mood for society.'



'we'll have no one. only Marcella and I.'



again a long silence.



'Marcella be go in for comparative philology,' Christian resume,

with the gentle tone in which he invariably speak of his sister. 'what

a mind that girl have! I never know any woman of half her power.'



Godwin say nothing.



'no,' continue the other fervently, 'nor of half her goodness. I

sometimes think that no mortal could come near to our ideal of moral

justice and purity. if it be not for she, I should long ago have go

to perdition, in one way or another. it's her strength, not my own,

that have save I. I daresay you know this?'



'there's some truth in it, I believe,' Peak answer, his eye wander.



'see how circumstance can affect one's judgment. if, just about the

time I first know you, I have abandon myself to a life of sottish

despair, of course I should have charge Constance with the blame of

it. now that I have struggle on, I can see that she have be a

blessing to I instead of a curse. if Marcella have give I strength, I

have to thank Constance for the spiritual joy which otherwise I should

never have know.'



Peak utter a short laugh.



'that be only say that she _might_ have be ruinous, but in the

course of circumstance have prove helpful. I envy your power of

derive comfort from such reflection.'



'well, we view thing differently. I have the habit of look to the

consolatory fact of life, you to the depressing. there's an

unfortunate lack in you, Peak; you seem insensible to female influence,

and I believe that be closely connect with your desperate pessimism.'



Godwin laugh again, this time with mocking length of note. 'come now,

ben't it true?' urge the other. 'sincerely, do you care for woman at

all?'



'perhaps not.'



'a grave misfortune, depend upon it! it account for nearly everything

that be unsatisfactory in your life. if you have ever be sincerely

devoted to a woman, be assure your power would have develop in a

way of which you have no conception. it's no answer to tell I that _i_

be still a mere trifl, never likely to do anything of account; I

haven't it in I to be anything well, and I might easily have become

much bad. but you might have make yourself a great position--I mean,

you _might_ do so; you be still very young. if only you know the

desire of a woman's help.'



'you really think so?' say Godwin, with grave irony.



'I be sure of it! there's no harm in repeat what you have often tell

I--your egoism oppress you. a woman's influence take one out of

oneself. no man can be a well authority on this than I. for more than

eleven year I have worship one woman with absolute faithfulness'----



'Absolute?' interrupt Godwin, bluntly.



'what exception occur to you?'



'as you challenge inquiry, forgive I for ask what your interest be

in one of your cousin at Twybridge?'



Christian start, and avert his face with a look of embarrassment.



'do you mean to say that you know anything about that?'



'I be always an observer,' Peak reply, smile. 'you don't remember,

perhaps, that I happen to be present when a letter have just arrive

for you at your uncle's house--a letter which evidently disturb you?'



'this be astonishing! Peak, you're a terrible fellow! Heaven forbid

that I should ever be at your mercy! yes, you be quite right,' he

continue, despondently. 'but that be no real unfaithfulness. I don't

quite know how to explain it. I _do_ make love to poor Janet, and with

the result that I have never since see any of the family. my uncle,

when he find I have draw back, be very savage--naturally enough.

Marcella and I never again go to Twybridge. I like Janet; she be a

good, kind girl. I believe just then that my love for Constance be

hopeless; my mood impel I to the conviction that the good thing I

could do be to marry Janet and settle down to a peaceful domestic

life. then come that letter--it be from Constance herself. it mean

nothing, yet it be enough to revive all my hope. I rush off--! how

brutally I have behave! poor little Janet!'



he let his face fall upon his hand.



'allow I an indiscreet question,' say Peak, after a silence. 'have

you any found hope of marry Constance if she become a widow?'



Christian start and look up with wide eye.



'Hope? every hope! I have the absolute assurance of her love.'



'I see.'



'but I mustn't mislead you,' pursue the other, hurriedly. 'our

relation be absolutely pure. I have only allow myself to see she at

very long interval. why shouldn't I tell you? it be less than a year

after her marriage; I find she alone in a room in a friend's house;

her eye be red with weeping. I couldn't help hold my hand to she.

she take it, and hold it for a moment, and look at I steadily, and

whisper my name--that be all. I know then that she repent of her

marriage--who can say what lead she into it? I be poor, you know;

perhaps--but in spite of all, she _do_ love I. there have never since

be anything like a scene of emotion between us--_that_ her conscience

couldn't allow. she be a noble-minded woman, and have do her duty. but

if she be free'--



he quiver with passionate feeling.



'and you be content,' say Godwin, drily, 'to have waste ten year of

your life for such a possibility?'



'waste!' Christian exclaim. 'come, come, Peak; why _will_ you affect

this wretched cynicism? be it waste of year to have live with the

high and pure ideal perpetually before one's mind? what can a man

do well than, having find an admirable woman, to worship her

thenceforth, and defy every temptation that could lead he astray? I

don't like to seem boastful, but I _have_ live purely and devotedly.

and if the test endure to the end of my life, I could sustain it. be

the consciousness of my love nothing to Constance? have it not help

she?'



such profound sincerity be astonishing to Peak. he do not admire it,

for it seem to he, in this case at all event, the fatal weakness of

a character it be impossible not to love. though he could not declare

his doubt, he think it more than probable that this Laura of the

voiceless Petrarch be unworthy of such constancy, and that she have no

intention whatever of reward it, even if the opportunity arrive.

but this be the mere speculation of a pessimist; he might be

altogether wrong, for he have never deny the existence of high virtue,

in man or woman.



'there go midnight!' he remark, turn from the subject. 'you

cann't sleep, neither can I. why shouldn't we walk into town?'



'by all mean; on condition that you will come home with I, and spend

to-morrow there.'



'very well.'



they set forth, and with varied talk, often break by long silence,

make their way through sleep suburb to the dark valley of Thames.



there pass another month, during which Peak be neither see nor

hear of by his friend. one evening in October, as he sat study at

the British Museum, a friendly voice claim his attention. he rise

nervously and meet the search eye of Buckland Warricombe.



'I have it in mind to write to you,' say the latter. 'since we part

down yonder I have be run about a good deal, with few day in

town. do you often read here?'



'generally on Saturday afternoon.'



Buckland glance at the open volume, and catch a heading, 'apologetic

theology.'



'still at the work?'



'yes; I shall be there till Christmas--no long.'



'be you by chance disengage to-morrow? could you dine with I? I

shall be alone; perhaps you don't mind that? we could exchange view on

"fate, free-will, foreknowledge absolute".'



Godwin accept the invitation, and Warricombe, unable to linger, take

leave of he.



they meet the next evening in Buckland's room, not far from the Houses

of Parliament. commonplace comfort be the note of these quarter. Peak

wonder that a man who have it in his power to surround himself with

evidence of taste should be content to dwell thus. his host seem to

detect this thought in the glance Godwin cast about he.



'nothing but a _pie-a-terre_. I have be here three or four year,

but I don't think of it as a home. I suppose I shall settle somewhere

before long: yet, on the whole, what do it matter where one live?

there's something in the atmosphere of our time that make one

indispose to strike root in the old way. who know how long there'll

be such a thing as real property? we be get to think of ourselves

as lodger; it's as well to be indifferent about a notice to quit.'



'many people would still make a good fight for the old home,' reply

Peak.



'yes; I daresay I should myself, if I be a family man. a wife and

child be strong persuasion to conservatism. in those who have

anything, that's to say. let the family who have nothing learn how

they stand in point of number, and we shall see what we shall see.'



'and you be do your good to teach they that.'



Buckland smile.



'a few other thing at the same time. one ben't necessarily an

anarchist, you know.'



'what enormous faith you must have in the metaphysical power of the

multitude!'



'Trenchant! but say, rather, in the universal self-interest. that's the

trait of human nature which we have in mind when we speak of

enlightenment. the aim of practical Radicalism be to instruct man's

selfishness. astonishing how capable it be of be instruct! the

mistake of the Socialist lie in his credit man with far too much

self-esteem, far too little perception of their own limit. the

characteristic of mankind at large be humility.'



Peak begin to understand his old acquaintance; he have imagine he less

acute. gratify by the smile of interest, Warricombe add:



'there be force of madness; I have show you that I make allowance

for they. but they be only dangerous so long as privilege ally

itself with hypocrisy. the task of the modern civiliser be to sweep

away sham idealism.'



'I agree with you,' Godwin reply.



with sudden change of mood, Buckland begin to speak of an indifferent

topic of the day, and in a few minute they sit down to dinner.



not till the welcome tobacco blend its aroma with that of coffee do

a frankly personal note sound in their conversation.



'so at Christmas you be free,' say Warricombe. 'you still think of

leave London?'



'I have decide to go down into Devonshire.'



'the seaside?'



'I shall stay first of all in Exeter,' Godwin reply, with

deliberation; 'one can get hold of book there.'



'yes, especially of the ecclesiastical colour.'



'you be still unable to regard my position with anything but

contempt?' Peak ask, look steadily at the critical face.



'come now; what do it all mean? of course I quite understand how

tolerant the Church be become: I know what latitude it permit in its

servant. but what do you propose to yourself?'



'precisely what you call the work of the civiliser--to attack sham

ideal.'



'as for instance--?'



'the authority of the mob,' answer Peak, suavely.



'your clericalism be political, then?'



'to a great extent.'



'I discern a vague sort of consistency in this. you regard the Church

formula as merely symbolical--useful for the purpose of the day?'



'rather for the purpose of eternity.'



'in the human sense.'



'in every sense.'



Warricombe perceive that no directness of questioning would elicit

literal response, and on the whole this relieve he. to hear Godwin

Peak use the language of a fervent curate would have excite in he

something more than disgust. it do not seem impossible that a nature

like Peak's--intellectually arrogant, vehemently anti-popular--should

have be attract by the tradition, the social prestige, of the

Anglican Church; nor at all unlikely that a mind so constitute should

justify a seem acceptance of dogmas, which in the strict sense it

despise. but he be make uneasy by his ignorance of Peak's private

life during the year since their parting at College. he do not like

to think of the possible establishment of intimacy between this man of

low origin, uncertain career, boundless ambition, and the household of

Martin Warricombe. there could be no doubt that Peak have decide to go

to Exeter because of the social prospect recently open to he. in

the vulgar phrase, he have probably 'take stock' of Mr. Warricombe's

idiosyncrasy, and see therein a valuable opportunity for a theological

student, who at the same time be a devotee of natural science. to be

sure, the people at Exeter could be put on their guard. on the other

hand, Peak have plainly avow his desire to form social connection of

the useful kind; in his position such an aim be essential, a mere

matter of course.



Godwin's voice interrupt this train of thought.



'let I ask you a plain question. you have twice be kind enough to

introduce I to your home as a friend of your. be I guilty of

presumption in hope that your parent will continue to regard I as

an acquaintance? I trust there's no need to assure you that I know the

meaning of discretion.'



an appeal to Buckland's generosity seldom fail. yes, it be true that

he have more than once encourage the hope now frankly express.

indulge a correspondent frankness, he might explain that Peak's

position be so distasteful to he that it disturb the future with

many kind of uncertainty. but this would be churlish. he must treat

his guest as a gentleman, so long as nothing compel he to take the

less agreeable view.



'my dear Peak, let we have none of these formality. my parent have

distinctly invite you to go and see they whenever you be in the

neighbourhood. I be quite sure they will help to make your stay in

exeter a pleasant one.'



Therewith close the hazardous dialogue. Warricombe turn at once to a

safe topic--that of contemporary fiction, and they chat pleasantly

enough for the rest of the evening.



not many day after this, Godwin receive by post an envelope which

contain certain proof sheet, and therewith a note in which the

editor of _the Critical Review_ signify his acceptance of a paper

entitle 'the New Sophistry'. the communication be originally

address to Earwaker, who have scribble at the foot, 'correct, if you

be alive, and send back to Dolby.'



the next morning he do not set out as usual for Rotherhithe. through

the night he have not close his eye; he be in a state of nervousness

which border on fever. a dozen time he have read over the proof,

with throb pulse, with exultant self-admiration: but the printer's

error which have catch his eye, and a few fault of phrase, be still

uncorrected. what a capital piece of write it be! what a

flagellation of M'Naughten and all his tribe! if this do not rouse

echo in the literary world--



through the long day he sit in languor or pace his room like one make

restless by pain. only when the gloom of nightfall oblige he to light

his lamp do he at length sit down to the table and carefully revise

the proof, pen in hand. when he have make up the packet for post, he

write to Earwaker.



'I have forget all about this thing. proof have go to Dolby. I

have not sign; probably he would object to my do so. as it be, the

paper can be ascribe to anyone, and attention thus excited. we shall

see paragraph attribute it to man of mark--perhaps scandal will fix

it on a bishop. in any case, don't let out the secret. I beg this

seriously, and for a solid reason. not a word to anyone, however

intimate. if Dolby betray _your_ name, grin and bear it. I depend upon

your friendship.'







CHAPTER II





in a by-way which decline from the main thoroughfare of Exeter, and

bear the name of Longbrook Street, be a row of small house place

above long strip of slope garden. they be old and plain, with no

architectural feature call for mention, unless it be the latticed

porch which give the door an awkward quaintness. just beyond, the

road cross a hollow, and begin the ascent of a hill here interpose

between the city and the inland-wind valley of Exe. the little

terrace may be regard as urban or rural, accord to the taste and

occasion of those who dwell there. in one direction, a walk of five

minute will conduct to the middle of High Street, and in the other it

take scarcely long to reach the open country.



on the upper floor of one of these cottage, Godwin Peak have make his

abode. sitting-room and bedchamber, furnish with homely comfort,

answer to his bachelor need, and would allow of his receiving

without embarrassment any visitor whom fortune might send he. of

quietness he be assure, for a widow and her son, alike remarkable for

sobriety of demeanour, be the only person who share the house with

he. Mrs. root could not compare in grace and skill with the little

Frenchwoman who have sweeten his existence at Peckham Rye, but she

zeal make amend for natural deficiency, and the timorous respect with

which she wait upon he be by no mean disagreeable to Godwin. her

reply to a request or suggestion be always, 'if you please, sir.'

throughout the day she go so tranquilly about her domestic duty,

that Godwin seldom hear anything except the voice of the cuckoo-clock,

a pleasant sound to he. her son, employ at a nurseryman's, be a

great sinewy fellow with a face of such ruddiness that it seem to

diffuse warmth; on Sunday afternoon, whatever the state of the sky, he

sit behind the house in his shirt-sleeve, and smoke a pipe as he

contemplate the hart's-tongue which grow there upon a rockery.



'the gentleman from London'--so Mrs. Roots be willnot to style her lodger

in speak with neighbour--have bring his book with he; they find

place on a few shelf. his microscope have its stand by the window, and

one or two other scientific implement lie about the room. the cabinet

bequeath to he by Mr. Gunnery he have send to Twybridge, to remain in

his mother's care. in take the lodging, he describe himself merely

as a student, and give his landlady to understand that he hope to

remain under her roof for at least a year. of his extreme

respectability, the widow could entertain no doubt, for he dress with

aristocratic finish, attend service at the Cathedral and elsewhere

very frequently, and make the most punctual payment. moreover, a

casual remark have inform she that he be on friendly term with Mr.

Martin Warricombe, whom her son know as a gentleman of distinction. he

often sit up very late at night, but, doubtless, that be the practice

of Londoners. no lodger could have give less trouble, or have

acknowledge with more courtesy all that be do for his convenience.



no one ever call upon Mr. Peak, but he be often from home for many

hour together, probably on visit to great people in city or country.

it seem rather strange, however, that the postman so seldom bring

he that Sidwell listen with grow interest. most certainly she

to he with increase of suavity.



in the meantime he have purchase a copy of Reusch's _Bibel und Natur_,

and have make a translation of some fifty page. this experiment he

submit to a London publishing house, with proposal for the

completion of the work; without much delay there come a civil letter of

excuse, and with it the sample return. another attempt again meet with

rejection. this failure do not trouble he. what he really desire be

to read through his version of Reusch with Martin Warricombe, and

before long he have bring it to pass that Martin request a perusal

of the manuscript as it advance, which it do but slowly. Godwin durst

not endanger his success in the examination by encroach upon hour

of necessary study; his leisure be largely sacrifice to _Bibel und

Natur_, and many an evening of calm golden loveliness, when he long

to be amid the field, pass in vexatious imprisonment. the name of

Reusch grow odious to he, and he revenge himself for the hypocrisy of

other hour by fierce scorn, cast audibly at this laborious exegetist.







CHAPTER III





it occasionally happen that a woman whose early life have be direct

by native silliness and social bias, will submit to a tardy education

at the hand of her own child. thus be it with Mrs Warricombe.



she come of a race long establish in squirearchic dignity amid heath

and woodland. her breeding be pure through many generation of the

paternal and maternal line, representative of a physical type,

fortify in the male by much companionship with horse and hound, and

by the correspond country pursuit of dowered daughter. at the time

of her marriage she have no charm of person more remarkable than rosy

comeliness and the symmetry of supple limb. as for the nurture of her

mind, it have be intrust to home-governess of respectable

incapacity. Martin Warricombe marry she because she be one of a

little circle of girl, much alike as to birth and fortune, with whom

he have grow up in familiar communication. timidity impose restraint

upon he which make his choice almost a matter of accident. as befall

often enough, the betrothal become an accomplished fact whilst he be

still doubt whether he desire it or not. when the fervour of early

wedlock be outlive, he have no difficulty in accept as a matter of

course that his life's companion should be hopelessly illogical and at

heart indifferent to everything but the small grace and substantial

comfort of provincial existence. one of the advantage of wealth be

that it allow husband and wife to keep a great deal apart without any

show of mutual unkindness, a condition essential to happiness in

marriage. time foster in they a calm attachment, independent of

spiritual sympathy, satisfied with a common regard for domestic honour.



not that Mrs. Warricombe remain in complete ignorance of her

husband's pursuit; social form would scarcely have allow this,

see that she be in constant intercourse, as hostess or guest, with

Martin's scientific friend. of fossil she necessarily know something.

up to a certain point they amuse she; she could talk of ammonite, of

brachiopod, and would point a friend's attention to the _Calceola

sandalina_ which Martin prize so much. the significance of

palaeontology she dimly apprehend, for in the early day of their

union her husband have feel it desirable to explain to she what be

mean by geologic time and how he reconcile his view on that subject

with the demand of religious faith. among the book which he induce

she to read be Buckland's Bridgewater Treatise and the work of Hugh

Miller. the intellectual result be chaotic, and Mrs. Warricombe

settle at last into a comfortable private opinion, that though the

record of geology might be trustworthy that of the Bible be more so.

she would admit that there be no impiety in accept the evidence of

nature, but hold to a secret conviction that it be safe to believe in

Genesis. for anything beyond a quasi-permissible variance from biblical

authority as to the age of the world she be quite unprepared, and

Martin, in his discretion, impart to her nothing of the graver doubt

which be willnot to trouble he.



but as her child grow up, Mrs. Warricombe's mind and temper be

insensibly modify by influence which operate through her maternal

affection, influence no doubt aid by the progressive spirit of the

time. the three boy--Buckland, Maurice, and Louis--be distinctly of

a new generation. it need some ingenuity to discover their point of

kindre with paternal and maternal grandparent; nor even with father

and mother have they much in common which observation could readily

detect. sidwell, up to at least her fifteenth year, seem to present

far less change of type. in her Mrs. Warricombe recognise a daughter,

and not without solace. but Fanny again be a problematical nature,

almost from the cradle. latest bear, she appear to revive many

characteristic of the youthful Buckland, so far as a girl could

resemble her brother. it be a strange brood to cluster around Mrs.

Warricombe. for many year the mother be keep in alternation between

hope and fear, pride and disapproval, the old hereditary habit of

mind, and a new order of idea which could only be admit with the

utmost slowness. Buckland's Radicalism deeply offend she; she

marvel how such depravity could display itself in a child of her.

yet in the end her ancestral prejudice so far yield as to allow of

she smile at sentiment which she once hear with horror. Maurice,

whom she love more tenderly, all but teach she to see the cogency of

a syllogism--amiably set forth. and Louis, with his indolent

good-nature, laugh she into a tolerance of many thing which have

move her indignation. but it be to sidwell that in the end she owe

most. beneath the surface of ordinary and rather backward girlhood,

which discourage her father's hope, Sidwell be quietly develop a

personality distinguish by the refinement of its ethical motive. her

orthodoxy seem as unimpeachable as Mrs Warricombe could desire, yet

as she grow into womanhood, a curiosity, which in no way disturb the

tenor of she quietly content life, lead she to examine various form

of religion, ancient and modern, and even system of philosophy which

profess to establish a moral code, independent of supernatural faith.

she be not of studious disposition--that be to say, she have never

care as a schoolgirl to do more mental work than be require of she,

and even now it be seldom that she read for more than an hour or two

in the day. her habit be to dip into book, and meditate long on the

first point which arrest her thought. of continuous application she

seem incapable. she could read French, but do not attempt to pursue

the other language of which her teacher have give she a smattering.

it please she good when she could learn from conversation. in this way

she obtain some insight into her father's favourite science,

occasionally make suggestion or inquiry which reveal a subtle if

not an acute intelligence.



little by little Mrs. Warricombe find herself change place with the

daughter whom she have regard as wholly subject to her direction.

Sidwell begin to exercise an indeterminate control, the proof of which

be at length manifest in detail of her mother's speech and

demeanour. an exquisite social tact, an unfailing insensibly as the

quality of pure air: these be the point of sincerity of moral

judgment, a gentle force which operate as character to which Mrs.

Warricombe owe the humanisation observable when one compare she in

1885 with what she be, say, in 1874, when the sight of Professor Walsh

move she to acrimony, and when she conceive a pique against Professor

Gale because the letter p have alphabetical precedence of W. Her

limitation be of course the same as ever, and from her son she have

only learn to be ashamed of announce they too vehemently. sidwell it

be who have lead she to that degree of genuine humility, which be not

satisfied with hide a fault but strive to amend it.



Martin Warricombe himself be not unaffected by the growth about he of

young man and maiden who look upon the world with new eye, whose

world, indeed, be another than that in which he have spend the well

part of his life. in his case contact with the young generation tend

to unsettlement, to a troublesome persistency of speculation which he

would have prefer to dismiss altogether. at the time of his

marriage, and for some year after, he be content to make a broad

distinction between those intellectual pursuit which afford he

rather a liberal amusement than the pleasure of earnest study and the

question of metaphysical faith which concern his heart and

conscience. his native prejudice be almost as strong, and much the

same, as those of his wife; but with the vagueness of emotional logic

natural to his constitution, he satisfy himself that, by concede a

few inessential point, he leave himself at liberty to follow the

scientific movement of the day without damage to his religious

conviction. the tolerant smile so frequently on his countenance be

direct as often in the one quarter as in the other. now it signify

a gentle reproof of those man of science who, like Professor Walsh,

'go too far', whose zeal for knowledge lead they 'to forget the source

of all true enlightenment'; now it express a forbear sympathy with

such as err in the opposite direction, who be 'too literal in their

interpretation of the sacred volume'. amiable as the smile be, it

betray weakness, and at moment Martin become unpleasantly conscious

of indisposition to examine his own mind on certain point. his life,

indeed, be one of debate postpone. as the realm of science extend,

as his intercourse with man who frankly avow their 'infidelity' grow

more frequent, he ever and again say to himself that, one of these

day, he must sit down and 'have it out' in a solemn self-search.

but for the most part he get on very well amid his inconsistency.

religious faith have rarely any connection with reasoning. Martin

believe because he believe, and avoid the impact of disagreeable

argument because he wish to do so.



the bent of his mind be anything but polemical; he care not to spend

time even over those author whose attack on the outpost of science,

or whose elaborate reconcilement of old and new, might have afford

he some support. on the other hand, he altogether lack that breadth

of intellect which seek to comprehend all the result of speculation,

to discern their tendency, to derive from they a consistent theory of

the nature of thing. though a man be well verse in a science such as

palaeontology it do not follow that he will view it in its

philosophical relation. Martin have keep himself inform of all the

fact appertain to his study which the age bring forth, but

without develop the new mode of mental life requisite for the

recognition of all that such fact involve. the theory of evolution

he do not venture openly to resist, but his acceptance of they be so

half-hearted that practically he make no use of their teaching. he be

no man of science, but an idler among the wonder which science use

for her own purpose.



he regard with surprise and anxiety the tendency early manifest

in his son Buckland. could he have have his way the lad would have grow

up with an impossible combination of quality, blend the enthusiasm

of modern research with a spirit of expansive teleology. whilst

Buckland be still of boyish year, the father treat with banter

good-humour such outbreak of irreverence as come immediately under his

notice, weakly abstain from any attempt at direct argument or

influence. but, at a later time, there take place serious and painful

discussion, and only when the young man have rub off his edge in

the world's highway could Martin forget that stage of most unwelcome

conflict.



at the death of his young boy, Maurice, he suffer a blow which have

result more abide than the melancholy wherewith for a year or two

his genial nature be overshadow. from that day onwards he be never

wholly at ease among the pursuit which have be willnot to afford he an

unfailing resource against whatever trouble. he could no long accept

and disregard, in a spirit of cheerful faith, those difficulty

science be perpetually throw in his way. the old smile of kindly

tolerance have still its twofold meaning, but it be more evidently a

disguise of indecision, and not seldom touch with sadness. Martin's

life be still one of postpone debate, but he could not regard the day

when conclusion would be demand of he as indefinitely remote.

desire to dwell in the familiar temporary abode, his structure of

incongruity and facile reconcilement, he find it no long

weather-proof. the time be shake his position with earthquake

after earthquake. his son (for he suspect that Louis be hardly less

emancipate than Buckland) stand far aloof from he, and must in

private feel contemptuous of his old-fashioned belief. in Sidwell,

however, he have a companion more and more indispensable, and he could

not imagine that _she_ faith would ever give way before the invade

spirit of agnosticism. happily she be no mere pietist. though he do

not quite understand her attitude towards Christianity, he feel assure

that Sidwell have think deeply and earnestly of religion in all its

aspect, and it be a solace to know that she find no difficulty in

recognise the large claim of science. for all this, he could not

deliberately seek her confidence, or invite she to a discussion of

religious subject. some day, no doubt, a talk of that kind would begin

naturally between they, and so strong be his instinctive faith in

Sidwell that he look forward to this future communing as to a certain

hope of peace.



that a figure such as Godwin Peak, a young man of vigorous intellect,

prepare to devote his life to the old religion, should excite Mr.

Warricombe's interest be of course to be anticipate; and it seem

probable enough that Peak, exert all the force of his character and

aid by circumstance, might before long convert this advantage to a

mean of ascendency over the less self-reliant nature. but here be no

instance of a dotard become the easy prey of a scientific tartufe.

Martin's intellect have suffer no decay. his hale feature and

dignified bearing express the mind which be ripen by sixty year

of pleasurable activity, and which be learn to regard with steady

view the problem it have hitherto shirk. he could not change the

direction nature have give to his thought, and prepossession would in

some degree obscure his judgment where the merit and trustworthiness

of a man in Peak's circumstance call for scrutiny; but self-respect

guard he against vulgar artifice, and a fine sensibility make it

improbable that he would become the victim of any man in whom base

motive predominate.



leave to his own impulse, he would still have proceed with all

caution in his offer of friendly service to Peak. a letter of

carefully-word admonition, which he receive from his son, apprise

for his own part, he understand that such a step might be prompt by

interest, but he find it difficult to believe that to a man in Peak's

position, the Church would offer temptation thus coercive. nor could he

discern in the candidate for a curacy any mark of dishonourable

purpose. fault, no doubt, be observable, among they a tendency to

spiritual pride--which seem (Martin could admit) an argument for,

rather than against, his sincerity. the progress of acquaintance

decidedly confirm his favourable impression; they be support by

the remark of those among his friend to whom Peak presently became

know.

'not very often. he be translate a german book which interest father

very much.'



'oh, what book?'



'I don't know. Father have only mention it in that way.'



they be in a little room sacred to the two girl, very daintily

'I cann't get out of the way of this fellow M'Naughten! wherever I go,

Sidwell smile, evidently in no way offended.

'then let I ask another question. what do you think of Peak?'

'I don't see any reason why he should not.'



her brother muse, with wrinkle of dissatisfaction on his brow.

'yes, I think father like he.'



'well, I suppose it's all right.'



'all right?'



'it's the most astounding thing that ever come under my observation,'

'yes, yes; but that be in your boyhood.'



'and when do manhood begin?'



'at different time in different person. in your case it be late.'



Buckland laugh. he be consider a rejoinder, when they be

'I'm in no hurry,' be the abrupt reply.



the girl hesitate.



'let we all have a drive together--with Mr. Peak, I mean--like when you

be here last.'



'we'll see about it.'



Buckland go slowly from the room.



late the same evening he sit with his father in the study. Mr

his father laugh.

'though?'

'it's a great pity,' he remark next day to Sidwell, who have be

'have something happen to depress he?' she ask.

Sidwell smile, and say quietly:



'I think it likely he would.'



at Budleigh Salterton, a nook on the coast some fifteen mile away,

'that's very weak, you know. it seem to I that nothing interest you

'the characteristic of woman,' Buckland pursue, with some asperity,

I think.'

'really? we hope to have you for a week.'



'long next time.'



they descend together. now that Louis no long abode here (he have

'seriously, I think you might all come to town for next winter. you be

invitation be due in this instance to Sylvia's express wish to meet

'I be study man,' she have say to Sidwell not long before, when the

let I have a chance of talk to he when I come next.'



she do not neglect her opportunity, and Mrs. Moorhouse, who also

Budleigh Salterton.



after breakfast next morning, Sidwell find her friend sit with a

the book be Hugh Miller's _testimony of the Rocks_, a richly bind

copy belong to Mrs. Warricombe.



'I daresay you know it very well; it's the chapter in which he

discuss, with perfect gravity, whether it would have be possible

decide that it wouldn't--that the deluge _must_ have spare a portion

miracle"!'



Sylvia's eye wink as she laugh, a peculiarity which enhance the

but miracle, however great or small its extent.'



'ben't it strange, read a book of this kind nowadays? what a leap we

have make! I should think there's hardly a country curate who would be

capable of bring this argument into a sermon.'



'I don't know,' return Sidwell, smile. 'one still hear remarkable

sermon.'



'what will Mr. Peak's be like?'



they exchange glance. Sylvia wear a look of reflective curiosity, and

her friend answer with some hesitation, as if the thought be new to

'they willn't deal with Noah, we may take that for grant.'



'most likely not with miracle, however little expensive.'
