Christianity.'



'do you think he strong as a moralist?' inquire Sylvia.



'he have very decide opinion about the present state of our

'so I find. but be there any distinctly moral force in he?'



'Father think so,' Sidwell reply, 'and so do our friend the

Lilywhites.'



Miss Moorhouse ponder awhile.



'he be a great problem to I,' she declare at length, knit she

believe in the dogma of Christianity.'



Sidwell be startle.



'would he think of become a clergyman?'



'oh, why not? don't they recognise nowadays that the spirit be enough?'



there be silence. sidwell let her eye wander over the sunny grass to

the red-flower creeper on the near side of the house.

'I cann't reconcile it with what I know of Mr. Peak.'

'he impress you as a rationalist?'



'you not?'



'I confess I have take his belief for grant. oh, think! he couldn't

keep up such a pretence. however you justify it, it imply conscious

'Buckland cann't credit anyone with sincerity except an aggressive

'but I think he allow honest credulity.'

'several.'



Sylvia remove her hat, and begin to fan herself gently with the brim.

here, in the shade, bee be hum; from the house come faint note

'I think not.'



'she be bitter against religion of every kind.'

'no, no. Marcella be about eighteen then, and have neither father nor

CHAPTER IV





this summer Peak become a semi-graduate of London University. to avoid

the risk of a casual meeting with acquaintance, he do not go to

London, but sit for his examination at the near provincial centre.

the revival of boyish tremor at the successive stage of this business

be anything but agreeable; it remind he, with humiliating force,

how far he have stray from the path indicate to his self-respect

manhood. Defeat would have strengthen in overwhelming revolt all the

impulse which from time to time urge he to abandon his servile

course. but there be no chance of his fail to satisfy the

examiner. with 'Honours' he have now nothing to do; enough for his

purpose that in another year's time he would write himself Bachelor of

Arts, and thus simplify the clerical preliminary. in what quarter he

be to look for a curacy remain uncertain. meanwhile his enterprise

seem to prosper, and success embolden his hope.



hope which be no long vague, but have define themselves in a way

which circumstance make inevitable. though he have consistently guard

himself against the obvious suggestion arise out of his intercourse

with the Warricombe family, though he still emphasise every

discouraging fact, and strove to regard it as axiomatic that nothing

could be more perilous to his future than a hint of presumption or

self-interest in word or deed beneath that friendly roof, it be come

to pass that he think of Sidwell not only as the type of woman

pursue by his imagination, but as herself the object of his converging

desire. comparison of she with other have no result but the deepening

of that impression she have at first make upon he. Sidwell exhibit

all the quality which most appeal to he in her class; in addition,

she have the charm of a personality which he could not think of common

occurrence. he be yet far from understand she; she exercise his

power of observation, analysis, conjecture, as no other person have

ever do; each time he see she (be it but for a moment) he come away

with some new perception of her excellence, some hitherto unmarked

grace of person or mind whereon to meditate. he have never approach a

woman who possess this power at once of fascinate his sense and

control his intellect to a glad reverence. whether in her presence

or muse upon she in solitude, he find that the unspare naturalism

of his scrutiny be powerless to degrade that sweet, pure being.



Rare, under any circumstance, be the passionate love which control

every motive of heart and mind; rarer still that form of it which, with

no assurance of reciprocation, devote exclusive ardour to an object

only approachable through declare obstacle. Godwin Peak be not

frame for romantic languishment. in general, the more complex a man's

mechanism, and the more pronounce his habit of introspection, the less

capable be he of love with vehemence and constancy. hero of passion

be for the most part primitive nature, nobly temper; in our time

they tend to extinction. grow vulgarism on the one hand, and on the

other a development of the psychological conscience, be unfavourable

to any relation between the sex, save those which originate in pure

animalism, or in reasoning less or more generous. never having

experience any feeling which he could dignify with the name of love,

Godwin have no criterion in himself whereby to test the emotion now

beset he. in a man of his age this be an unusual state of thing,

for when the ardour which will bear analysis have at length declare

itself, it be willnot to be moderate by the regretful memory of that

fugacious essence which give to the first frenzy of youth its

irrecoverable delight. he could not say in reply to his impulse: if

that be love which overmaster I, this must be something either more

or less exalted. what he _do_ say be something of this kind: if

desire and tenderness, if frequency of dream rapture, if the calm

approval of the mind and the heart's most exquisite, most painful

throbbing, constitute love,--then assuredly I love Sidwell. but if to

love be to be possess with madness, to lose all taste of life when

hope refuse itself, to meditate frantic folly, to deem it

inconceivable that this woman should ever lose her dominion over I, or

another reign in her stead,--then my passion fall short of the true

testrum, and I be only dally with fancy which might spring up as

often as I encounter a charming girl.



all thing consider, to encourage this amorous preoccupation be

probably the height of unwisdom. the lover be ready at delude

himself, but Peak never lose sight of the extreme unlikelihood that he

should ever become Martin Warricombe's son-in-law, of the thousand

respect which forbid his hoping that Sidwell would ever lay her hand

in his. that deep-rooted sense of class which have so much influence on

his speculative and practical life assert itself, with rigid

consistency, even against his own aspiration; he attribute to the

Warricombes more prejudice on this subject than really exist in they.

he, it be true, belong to no class whatever, acknowledge no

subordination save that of the hierarchy of intelligence; but this

could not obscure the fact that his brother sell seed across a

counter, that his sister have marry a haberdasher, that his uncle

(notoriously) be somewhere or other supply the public with cheap

repast. girl of Sidwell's delicacy do not misally themselves, for

they take into account the fact that such misalliance be fraught with

element of unhappiness, affect husband as much as wife. no need to

dwell upon the scruple suggest by his moral attitude; he would never

be call upon to combat they with reference to Sidwell's future.



what, then, be he about? for what advantage be he play the

hypocrite? would he, after all, be satisfied with some such wife as the

average curate may hope to marry?



a hundred time he review the broad question, by the light of his six

month' experience. be Sidwell Warricombe his ideal woman, absolutely

speak? why, no; not with all his glow of feeling could he persuade

himself to declare she that. satisfy up to a certain point, admit

to the sphere of wealthy refinement, he now have leisure to think of yet

high grade, of the woman who be not only exquisite creature by

social comparison but rank by divine right among the foremost of their

race. Sidwell be far from intolerant, and hold her faith in a

sincerely ethical spirit. she judge nobly, she often see with clear

vision. but must not something of kindly condescension always blend

with his admire devotedness? be it but possible to win the love of

a woman who look forth with eye thoroughly purge from all mist of

tradition and conventionalism, who be at home among art and science,

who, like himself, acknowledge no class and bow to no authority but

that of the supreme human mind!



such woman be to be find in every age, but how many of they shine

with the distinctive ray of womanhood? these be so rare that they have

a place in the page of history. the truly emancipate woman--it be

Godwin's conviction--be almost always asexual; to he, therefore,

utterly repugnant. if, then, he be not content to waste his life in a

vain search for the priceless jewel, which be win and wear only by

fortune's supreme favourite, he must acquiesce in the imperfect

marriage commonly the lot of man whose intellect allow they but little

companionship even among their own sex: for that matter, the lot of

most man, and necessarily so until the new effort in female education

shall have overcome the vice of wedlock as hitherto sanction. nature

provide the hallucination which fling a lover at his mistress's foot.

for the chill which follow upon attainment she care nothing--let

society and individual make their account with that as good they may.

even with a wife such as Sidwell the process of disillusion would

doubtless have to be face, however liberal one's allowance in the

forecast.



reflection of this colour be useful; they help to keep within

limit the growth of agitate desire. but there be season when

Godwin surrender himself to luxurious reverie, hour of summer

twilight which forbid analysis and listen only to the harmony of

passion. then be Sidwell's image glorify, and all the delight

promise by such love as hers fire his imagination to intolerable

confess that she have give her heart--to feel her supple finger

intertwine with his that clasp they--to hear the word in which a

mind so admirable, instinct so delicate, would make expression of

their tenderness! to live with Sidwell--to breathe the fragrance of

that flower of womanhood in wed intimacy--to prove the devotion of a

nature so profoundly chaste! the visionary transport be too poignant;

in the end it drive he to a fierce outbreak of despair wrath. how

could he dream that such bliss would be the reward of despicable

artifice, of calculated dishonour? bear a rebel, how could his be the

fate of those happy man who be at one with the order of thing? the

prophecy of a heart wring with anguish foretell too surely that for he

be no rapturous love, no joy of noble wedlock. Solitude, now and for

ever, or perchance some base alliance of the flesh, which would involve

his later day in sordid misery.



in mood of discouragement he think with envy of his old self, his

life in London lodging, his freedom in obscurity. it belong to the

pathos of human nature that only in look back can one appreciate the

true value of those long tract of monotonous ease which, when we be

future; only at a distance do we perceive that the exemption from

comparison with most of those disturbance know as moment of joy. a

wise man would have entertain no wish but that he might grow old in

that same succession of day and week and year. without anxiety

concern his material need (certainly the most substantial of

a moderate studiousness, with friend who offer he an ever-ready

philosophy be surely capable of teach he that the sorrow and

anxiety he thus escape make more than an offset against the

satisfaction he must forego. Reason have no part in the fantastic

change to which his life have submit, nor be he ever support by a

hope which would bear his cool investigation.



and yet hope have her period of control, for there be time when the

mind wearie of rationality, and, as it be in self-defense, in

obedience to the instinct of progressive life, crave a specious

comfort. it seem undeniable that Mr. Warricombe regard he with

growth of interest, invite his conversation more unreservedly. he

begin to understand Martin's position with regard to religion and

science, and thus could utter himself more securely. at length he

this opportunity present itself. they sit together in the study, and

Martin be in a graver mood than usual, not much disposed to talk, but

a willing listener. there have be mention of a sermon at the

of science would dispel all antagonism between it and revelation.



'the difficulty of the unbeliever,' say Peak, endeavour to avoid

course, of two kind; there's the theory of evolution, and there's

modern biblical criticism. the more I study these objection, the less

able I be to see how they come in conflict with belief in Christianity

as a reveal religion.'



'yet you probably have your time of doubt?' remark the other, touch

for the first time on this personal matter.



'oh, yes; that be inevitable. it only mean that one's development be

imperfect. Most man who confirm themselves in agnosticism be keep at

that point by arrest moral activity. they give up the intellectual

question as wearisome, and accept the point of view which flatter

their prejudice: thereupon follow a blunting of the sensibility on

the religious side.'



'there be man constitutionally unfit for the reception of spiritual

truth,' say Martin, in a troubled tone. he be play with a piece of

phenomenon, or scrutiny of authority, but from the declaration make to

we by the spiritual faculty. the man of science can only become a

it difficult even to conceive. one wish to impress upon he the

reply: I know nothing of that spiritual voice. and if that be true,

one cann't help he by argument.'



Peak have construct for himself, out of his reading, a plausible

yet to himself it sound so futile, so nugatory, that he have to harden

that be to say, we must draw evidence of our faith from its latent

capacity, its unsuspected affinity, its prevision, its

adaptability, comprehensiveness, sympathy, adequacy to human need.'



'that put very well what I have always feel,' reply Mr Warricombe.

'yet there will remain the objection that such a faith may be of purely

human origin. if evolution and biblical criticism seem to overthrow all

the historic evidence of Christianity, how convince the objector that

the faith itself be divinely give?'



'but I cannot hold for a moment,' exclaim Peak, in the word which he

know his interlocutor desire to hear, 'that all the historic evidence

have be destroy. that indeed would shake our position.'



he enlarge on the point, with display of learn, yet studiously

avoid the tone of pedantry.



'Evolution,' he remark, when the dialogue have again extend its

scope, 'do not touch the evidence of design in the universe; at most

it can correct our imperfect view (hand down from an age which have

no scientific teaching because it be not ripe for it) of the mode in

which that design be execute, or rather be still be execute.

evolutionist have not succeed in explain life; they have merely

discover a new law relate to life. if we must have an explanation,

there be nothing for it but to accept the notion of a deity. indeed,

how can there be religion without a divine author? religion be base on

the idea of a divine mind which reveal itself to we for moral end.

the christian revelation, we hold, have be develop gradually, much

Hitherto, the final outcome of evolution be the soul in a bodily

tenement. may it not be that the perfect soul alone survive in the

last step of the struggle for existence?'



Peak have be talk for more than a quarter of an hour. under stress

friendly smile.



'you fear,' he say, 'that this last speculation may seem rather

fanciful to I?'



Godwin be bite his lip fiercely, and could not command himself to

utterance of a word.



'by no mean, I assure you,' add the other. 'it appeal to I very

strongly.'



Peak rise from his chair.



'it strike I,' he say, 'that I have be preach a sermon rather

than take part in a conversation. I'm afraid it be the habit of man

who live a good deal alone to indulge in monologue.'



on his return home, the sight of _Bibel und Natur_ and his sheet of

laborious manuscript fill he with disgust. it be two or three day

before he could again apply himself to the translation. yet this

already they have discuss in detail the first half of the book. how a

comprehend. often enough the debate be perilously suggestive of

previous state of thing and creation as establish by the Hexaemeron?

do light exist or not, previous to the _thohu wabohu_? then, again,

what kind of 'day' be the three which pass before the birth of the

sun? special interest, of course, attach to the successive theory

of theology on the origin of geologic strata. first come the 'theory of

restitution', which explain unbiblical antiquity by declare that

the strata belong to a world before the Hexaimeron, a world which have

be destroy, and succeed by the new creation. less objectionable

be the 'concordistic theory', which interpret the 'six day' as so

many vast period of creative activity. but Reusch himself give

preference to the 'ideal theory', the supporter whereof (diligently

adapt themselves to the progress of science) hold that the six day

be not to be understand as consecutive period at all, but merely as

six phase of the Creator's work.



by the exercise of watchfulness and dexterity, Peak manage for the

most part to avoid expression of definite opinion. his attitude be

guard, and sometimes allow himself to profess that he see nothing

cause Martin to smile.



'it willn't do, it willn't do,' he say, quietly. 'far well apply his

and, indeed, there seem much reason for believe that Martin,

his view of the Bible appear to harmonise with that which Peak put

(be it necessary to have admit the miracle? Godwin ask himself.

at all event Mr. Warricombe nod approvingly.)

they be not within its province. such thing be discoverable by

divine revelation.'



to Godwin it be a grinding of the air, but the listener appear to

think it profitable.



with his clerical friend, Mr. Lilywhite, he rarely touch on matter

such remote time and region. I love the sunlight and the green field

well.'



after one of these walk, he remark to Mrs. Lilywhite:



'it's my impression that Mr. Peak have somehow be mislead in his choice

of a vocation. I don't think he'll do as a churchman.'



'why not, Henry?' ask his wife, with gentle concern, for she still

speak of Peak's 'quiet moral force'.

explain what I feel, but I don't think he will take Orders.'



call at the vicarage one afternoon in September, Godwin find Mrs

Lilywhite alone. she startle he by say at once:



'an old acquaintance of yours be with we yesterday, Mr. Peak.'



'who could that be, I wonder?'



he smile softly, control his impulse to show quite another

'you remember Mr. Bruno Chilvers?'



'oh, yes!'



there be a constriction in his throat. struggle to overcome it, he

'but I should have think he have no recollection of I.'

least he would pass the house, and soothe or inflame his emotion by

rest for a moment thus near to Sidwell.



what? he have believe himself incapable of erotic madness? and he

press his forehead against the stone of the wall to relieve his sick

dizziness.



it be Sidwell or death. into what a void of hideous futility would his

life be cast, if this desire prove vain, and he be leave to combat

alone with the memory of his dishonour! with Sidwell the reproach could

be outlive. she would understand he, pardon he--and thereafter a

glorify existence, rival that of whosoever have be most exultant

among the son of man!









Part IV







CHAPTER I





Earwaker's struggle with the editor-in-chief of _the Weekly Post_ and

the journalist Kenyon come to its natural close about a month after

Godwin Peak's disappearance. only a vein of obstinacy in his character

have keep he so long in a position he know to be untenable. from the

first his sympathy with Mr. Runcorn's politic have be doubtful, and

experience of the working of a Sunday newspaper, which appeal to the

ignobly restive, could not encourage his adhesion to this form of

Radicalism. he anticipate dismissal by retirement, and Kenyon, a man

of coarsely vigorous fibre, at once step into his place.



now that he have leisure to review the conflict, Earwaker understand

that circumstance have but hasten his transition from a moderate

ardour in the parliamentary cause of the people, to a regretful

neutrality regard all political movement. birth ally he with the

proletarian class, and his sentiment in favour of democracy be

unendangered by the disillusion which must come upon every

intellectual man bring into close contact with public affair. the

course of an education essentially aristocratic (Greek and Latin can

have no other tendency so long as they be the privilege of the few)

have not affect his natural bent, nor be he the man to be drive into

reaction because of obstacle to his faith inseparable from human

weakness. he have learn that the emancipation of the poor and untaught

must proceed more slowly than he once hope--that be all. restore to

generous calm, he could admit that such man as Runcorn and Kenyon--the

one with his polyarchic commercialism, the other with his demagogic

violence--have possibly a useful part to play at the present stage of

thing. he, however, could have no place in that camp. too indiscreetly

he have hoist his standard of idealism, and by stubborn resistance of

insuperable force he have merely bring forward the least satisfactory

element of his own character. 'hold on!' cry Malkin. 'fight the

groveller to the end!' but Earwaker have begin to see himself in a

light of ridicule. there be just time to save his self-respect.



he be in no concern for his daily bread. with narrow resource in

the world of print, he might have be compel, like many another

journalist, to swallow his objection and write as Runcorn dictate;

for the humble folk at home could not starve to allow he the luxury

of conscientiousness, whatever he might have be dispose to do on his

own account. happily, his pen have a scope beyond politic, and by

work steadily for review, with which he be already connect, he

would be able to keep his finance in reasonable order until,

perchance, some hopeful appointment offer itself. in a mood of much

cheerfulness he turn for ever from party uproar, and focus his mind

upon those interest of humanity which so rarely coincide with the aim

of any league among man.



half a year go by, and at length he grant himself a short holiday,

the first in a twelvemonth. it take the form of a voyage to Marseilles,

and thence of a leisurely ramble up the Rhone. before return, he

spend a day or two in Paris, for the most part beneath cafe' awning,

or on garden seat--an indulgence of contented laziness.



on the day of his departure, he climb the tower of Notre Dame, and

linger for half-an-hour in pleasant solitude among the stone

monster. his reverie be break by an english voice, loud and animate:



'come and look at this old demon of a bird; he have always be a

favourite of mine.--Sure you're not tired, Miss Bella? when you want to

rest, Miss Lily, mind you say so at once. what a day! what a sky!--When

I be last up here I have my hat blow away. I watch it as far as

Montmartre. a fact! never know such a wind in my life--unless it be

that tornado I tell you about--hollo! by the power, if that ben't

Earwaker! confound you, old fellow! how the deuce do you do? what a

glorious meeting! hadn't the least idea where you were!--Let I have

the pleasure of introduce you to Mrs. Jacox--and to Miss Jacox--and

to Miss Lily. they all know you thoroughly well. now who would have

thought of our meeting up here! glorious!'



it be with some curiosity that Earwaker regard the companion of his

friend Malkin--whose proximity be the last thing he could have

imagine, as only a few week ago he have hear of the restless fellow's

whose wrong have make such an impression on Malkin, announce herself,

under the lucent sky, it seem a wrong to their bud maidenhood.



'Mrs. Jacox be on the point of return to England,' Malkin explain.

'I go to London to-night.'



'and we to-morrow. on Friday I'll look you up. stay, cann't you dine

in the after-glow of dinner, Malkin become still more confidential.

'by all that's suspicious, I cann't help think the same! he have an

they leave the restaurant, and in a few hour Earwaker be again on

at Staple Inn a pile of letter await he, among they a note from

next year perhaps.'

'yes, I'm afraid I have. but, by-the-bye, no news of Peak, I suppose?'



Earwaker relate the story he have hear from Malkin, add:



'you must remember that they meet only once in London; Malkin might very

well mistake another man for Peak.'

have go over there. if so, what on earth can he be up to? why _should_

'_Cherchez la femme_,' say the journalist, with a smile. 'I can devise

'but I cann't see that it would be an explanation at all. Grant

the journalist utter croak laughter.

'I think it not impossible.'



'you amaze me!--But no, no; you be wrong, Earwaker. wrong in your

and with a shrug of impatience, the journalist turn to another

how many such being do one encounter in a lifetime?









CHAPTER II





in different way, Christian and Marcella Moxey have both be lonely

since their childhood. as a schoolgirl, Marcella seem to she

companion conceited and repellent; only as the result of reflection in

after year do Sylvia Moorhouse express so favourable an opinion of

she. in all thing she affect singularity; especially it be she

delight to utter democratic and revolutionary sentiment among hearer

who, belong to a rigidly conservative order, hold such opinion

impious. arrive at womanhood, she affect scorn of the belief and

habit cherish by her own sex, and shrink from association with the

other. Godwin Peak be the first man with whom she converse in the

tone of friendship, and it take a year or more before that point be

reach. as her intimacy with he establish itself, she be observe

to undergo change which seem very significant in the eye of her few

acquaintance. disregard of costume have be one of her

characteristic, but now she move gradually towards the opposite

extreme, till her dress be occasionally more noticeable for

richness than for good taste.



Christian, for kindre reason, be equally debarred from the pleasure

and profit of society. at school, his teacher consider he clever,

his fellow for the most part look down upon he as a sentimental

weakling. the death of his parent, when he be still a lad, leave he

to the indifferent care of a guardian nothing akin to he. he begin

life in an uncongenial position, and have not courage to oppose the

drift of circumstance. the romantic attachment which absorb his good

year naturally have a debilitate effect, for love be never yet a

supporter of the strenuous virtue, save when it have survive fruition

and be bless by reason. in most man a fit of amorous mooning work

its own cure; energetic rebound be soon inevitable. but Christian be

so constitute that a decade of year could not exhaust his capacity

for sentimental languishment. he make it a point of honour to seek no

female companionship which could imperil his faith. unfortunately, this

avoidance of the society which would soon have make he a happy

renegade, be but too easy. Marcella and he practically encourage each

other in a life of isolation, though to both of they such an existence

be anything but congenial. their difficulty be of the same nature

as those which have always beset Godwin Peak; they have no relative with

whom they care to associate, and none of the domestic friend who, in

the progress of time, establish and extend a sphere of genuine intimacy.



Most people who be capable of independent thought rapidly outgrow the

stage when compromise be abhor; they accept, at first reluctantly,

but ere long with satisfaction, that code of polite intercourse which,

as Steele say, be 'an expedient to make fool and wise man equal'. it

be Marcella's ill-fate that she could neither learn tolerance nor

persuade herself to affect it. the emancipated woman have few

opportunity of relieve her mind than a man in correspond

position; if her temper be aggressive she must renounce general

society, and, if not content to live alone, ally herself with some

group of declare militant. by correspondence, or otherwise, Marcella

might have bring herself into connection with woman of a sympathetic

type, but this effort she have never make. and chiefly because of her

acquaintance with Godwin Peak. in he she concentrate her interest;

he be the man to whom her heart go forth with every kind of fervour.

so long as there remain a hope of move he to reciprocal feeling

she do not care to go in search of female companion. year after year

she sustain herself in solitude by this faint hope. she have lose

sight of the two or three schoolfellow who, though not so zealous as

herself, would have welcome she as an interesting acquaintance; and

the only woman who assiduously seek she be Mrs. Morton, the wife of

one of Christian's friend, a good-natured but silly person bent on

make know that she follow the 'high law'.



Godwin's disappearance sink she in profound melancholy. through the

black week of January and February she scarcely leave the house, and on

the plea of illness refuse to see any one but her brother. between

Christian and she there be no avowed confidence, but each know the

other's secret; their mutual affection never speak itself in word, yet

none the less it be indispensable to their life. deprive of his

sister's company, Christian must have yield to the vice which have

already too strong a hold upon he, and have become a maudlin drunkard.

leave to herself, Marcella have but slender support against a grim

temptation already beckon she in night of sleeplessness. of the

two, her nature be the more tragic. circumstance aid, Christian

might still forget his melancholy, abandon the whisky bottle, and pass

a lifetime of amiable uxoriousness, varied with scientific enthusiasm.

but for Marcella, frustrate in the desire with which every impulse of

her be have identify itself, what future could be imagine?



when a day or two of sunlight (the ray through a semi-opaque

atmosphere which London have to accept with gratitude) have announce

that the seven-month' winter be overcome, and when the newspaper

begin to speak, after their fashion, of picture await scrutiny,

of the circumstance to Christian, and add:



'I should like to have that. let we go and see the price.'



the work be already sell. Christian, happy that his sister could be

still, she see more of the Walworths and find they not illiberal.

'I don't think I shall. they be too respectable.'

his sister let her head lie back against the chair, her face avert. a

'by-the-bye, I be tell that Malkin (Earwaker's friend, you know) see

Peak not long ago--in America.'

CHAPTER III





three or four year ago, when already he have conceive the idea of

CHAPTER IV





Martin Warricombe be reconcile to the prospect of a metropolitan

Part V







CHAPTER I





the cuckoo clock in Mrs. Roots's kitchen have just strike three. a wind

roar from the north-east, and light thicken beneath a sky which

make threat of snow. Peak be in a mood to enjoy the crackling fire; he

settle himself with a book in his easy-chair, and think with

pleasure of two hour' reading, before the appearance of the homely

teapot.



Christmas be just over--one cause of the feeling of relief and

quietness which possess he. no one have invite he for Christmas Eve

or the day that follow, and he do not regret it. the letter he have

receive from Martin Warricombe be assurance enough that those he

desire to remember he still do so. he have think of use this

season for his long postpone visit to Twybridge, but reluctance

prevail. all popular holiday irritated and depress he; he loathe

the spectacle of multitude in Sunday garb. it be all over, and the

sense of that afford he a brief content.



this book, which he have just bring from the circulate library, be

altogether to his taste. the author, Justin Walsh, he know to be a

brother of Professor Walsh, long ago the object of his rebellious

admiration. matter and treatment rejoice he. no intellectual delight,

though he be capable of it in many form, so stir his spirit as

that afford he by a vigorous modern writer joyously assail the

old morality. Justin Walsh be a modern of the modern; at once man

of science and man of letter; defiant without a hint of popular

cynicism, scornful of english reticence yet never gross. '_Oui,

repondit Pococurante, il est beau d'ecrire ce qu 'on pense; c'est le

privilege de l'homme_.' this stand by way of motto on the title-page,

and Godwin feel his nerve thrill in sympathetic response.



what a fine fellow he must be to have for a friend! now a man like this

surely have companionship enough and of the kind he wish? he write

like one who associate freely with the educate class both at home

and abroad. be he marry? where would _he_ seek his wife? the fitting

CHAPTER II

CHAPTER III

CHAPTER IV





and from the Moxeys Buckland have derive his information. what be it

'no--indeed, no! you be a young lady, and a brilliant one.'

glance at he significantly. how good of she to allow he this

delicious afternoon!



'Mr. Moxey,' she say, after meditate a little, 'why haven't you

marry? I should have think you would have marry long ago.'



he be stricken dumb. her jerky laugh come as a shock upon his hearing.



'Married----?'



'what be there astonishing in the idea?'



'but--i--how can I answer you?'



the pretty, characterless face betray some unusual feeling. she

look at he furtively; seem to suppress a tendency to laugh.



'I mustn't pry into secret,' she simper.



'but there be no secret!' christian pant, lay down his teacup for

fear he should drop it. 'whom should I--could I have marry?'



Constance also put aside her cup. she be bewilder, and just a little

abash. with courage which come he know not whence, Christian bent

forward and continue speak:



'whom could I marry after that day when I meet you in the little

drawing-room at the Robinsons'?'



she stare in genuine astonishment, then be embarrassed.



'you cannot--cannot have forgotten----?'



'you surely don't mean to say, Mr. Moxey, that you have remember? oh,

I'm afraid I be a shocking flirt in those day!'



'but I mean _after_ your marriage--when I find you in tears'----



'please, please don't remind I!' she exclaim, giggle nervously.

'oh how silly!--of I, I mean. to think that--but you be make fun of

I, Mr. Moxey?'



Christian rise and go to the window. he be not only shake by his

tender emotion--something very like repugnance have begin to affect

he. if Constance be feign, it be in very bad taste; if she speak

with sincerity--what a woman have he worship! it do not occur to he

to lay the fault upon his own absurd romanticism. after eleven year'

persistence in one point of view, he could not suddenly see the affair

with the eye of common sense.



he turn and approach she again.



'do you not know, then,' he ask, with quiet dignity, 'that ever since

the day I speak of, I have devote my life to the love I then feel? all

these year, have you not understand I?'



Mrs. Palmer be quite unable to grasp idea such as these. neither her

reading nor her experience prepare she to understand what Christian

mean. courtship of a married woman be intelligible enough to she; but

a love that fear to soil itself, a devotion from afar, encourage by

only the faint hope of reward other than the most insubstantial--of

that she have as little conception as any woman among the wealthy vulgar.



'do you really mean, Mr. Moxey, that you--have keep unmarried for _my_

sake?'



'you don't know that?' he ask, hoarsely.



'how could I? how be I to imagine such a thing? really, be it proper?

how could you expect I, Mr. Moxey----?'



for a moment she look offended. but her real feeling be

astonishment and amusement, not unmingled with an idle gratification.



'I must ask you to pardon I,' say Christian, whose forehead gleam

with moisture.



'no, don't say that. I be really so sorry! what an odd mistake!'



'and I have hope in vain--since you be free----?'



'oh, you mustn't say such thing! I shall never dream of marry

again--never!'



there be a matter-of-fact vigour in the assertion which prove that

Mrs. Palmer speak her genuine thought. the tone could not be

interpret as devotion to her husband's memory; it mean, plainly and

simply, that she have have enough of marriage, and delight in her

freedom.



Christian could not say another word. disillusion be complete. the

voice, the face, be those of as unspiritual a woman as he could

easily have meet with, and his life's story be that of a fool.



he take his hat, hold out his hand, with 'good-bye, Mrs. Palmer.' the

cold politeness leave she no choice but again to look offended, and with

merely a motion of the head she reply, 'good-bye, Mr. Moxey.'



and therewith permit he to leave the house.







CHAPTER II





on call at Earwaker's chamber one February evening, Malkin become

aware, from the very threshold of the outer door, that the domicile be

not as he have know it. with the familiar fragrance of Earwaker's

special 'mixture' blend a suggestion of new upholstery. the little

vestibule have somehow put off its dinginess, and an unwontedly

brilliant light from the sitting-room reveal change of the interior

which the visitor remark with frank astonishment.



'what the deuce! have it happen at last? be you go to be marry?'

he cry, stare about he at unrecognised chair, table, and

bookcase, at whitened ceiling and pleasantly paper wall, at

picture and ornament which he know not.



the journalist shake his head, and smile contentedly.



'an idea that come to I all at once. my editorship seem to inspire

it.'



after a year of wait upon Providence, Earwaker have receive the

offer of a substantial appointment much more to his taste than those he

have previously hold. he be now literary editor of a weekly review

which make no kind of appeal to the untaught multitude.



'I have decide to dwell here for the rest of my life,' he add,

look round the wall. 'one must have a homestead, and this shall be

mine; here I have set up my penate. it's a portion of space, you know;

and what more can be say of Longleat or Chatsworth? a house I shall

never want, because I shall never have a wife. and on the whole I

prefer this situation to any other. I be well within reach of

everything urban that I care about, and as for the country, that be too

good to be put to common use; let it be keep for holiday. there's an

atmosphere in the old Inns that please I. the new flat be

insufferable. how can one live sandwich between a music-hall singer

and a female politician? for lodging of any kind no sane man have ever

a word of approval. reflect on all these thing, I have establish

myself in perpetuity.'



'just what I cann't do,' exclaim Malkin, fling himself into a

broad, deep, leather-cover chair. 'yet I have leaning that way. only

a few day ago I sit for a whole evening with the map of England open

before I, wonder where would be the good place to settle down--a

few year hence, I mean, you know; when Bella be old enough.--That

remind I. next Sunday be her birthday, and do you know what? I wish

you'd go down to Wrotham with I.'



'many thank, but I think I have well not.'



'oh, but do! I want you to see how Bella be get on. she's grow

wonderfully since you see she in Paris--an inch tall, I should think.

I don't go down there very often, you know, so I notice these change.

really, I think no one could be more discreet than I be, under the

circumstance. a friend of the family; that's all. just drop in for

a casual cup of tea now and then. Sunday will be a special occasion, of

course. I say, what be your view about early marriage? do you think

seventeen too young?'



'I should think seven-and-twenty much well.'



Malkin break into fretfulness.



'let I tell you, Earwaker, I don't like the way you habitually speak

of this project of mine. plainly, I don't like it. it's a very serious

matter indeed--eh? what? why be you smile?'



'I agree with you as to its seriousness.'



'yes, yes; but in a very cynical and offensive way. it make I

confoundedly uncomfortable, let I tell you. I don't think that's very

friendly on your part. and the fact be, if it go on I'm very much

afraid we shalln't see so much of each other as we have do. I like you,

Earwaker, and I respect you; I think you know that. but occasionally

you seem to have too little regard for one's feeling. no, I don't feel

able to pass it over with a joke.--There! the deuce take it! I've

bite off the end of my pipe.'



he spit out a piece of amber, and look ruefully at the broken stem.



'take a cigar,' say Earwaker, fetch a box from a cupboard.



'I don't mind.--Well--what be I say? oh yes; I be quarrel with

you. now, look here, what fault have you to find with Bella Jacox?'



'none whatever. she seem to I a very amiable child.'



'child! Pooh! pshaw! and fifteen next Sunday, I tell you. she's a young

lady, and to tell you the confound plain truth, I'm in love with she.

I be, and there's nothing to be ashamed of. if you smile, we shall

quarrel. I warn you, Earwaker, we shall quarrel.'



the journalist, instead of smile, give forth his deep laugh.

Malkin turn very red, scowl, and throw his cigar aside.



'you really wish I to go on Sunday?' Earwaker ask, in a pleasant

voice.



the other's countenance immediately clear.



'I shall take it as a great kindness. Mrs. Jacox will be delighted.

meet I at Holborn Viaduct at 1.25. no, to make sure I'll come here at

one o'clock.'



in a few minute he be chat as unconcernedly as ever.



'talk of settle down, my brother Tom and his wife be on the point

of go to New Zealand. necessity of business; may be out there for

the rest of their life. do you know that I shall think very seriously

of follow they some day? with Bella, you know. the fact of the

matter be, I don't believe I could ever make a solid home in England.

why, I cann't quite say; partly, I suppose, because I have nothing to

do. now there's a good deal to be say for go out to the colony. a

man feel that he be help the spread of civilisation; and that's

something, you know. I should compare myself with the greek and roman

colonist--something inspirit in that thought--what? why shouldn't I

find a respectable newspaper, for instance? yes, I shall think very

seriously of this.'



'you wouldn't care to run over with your relative, just to have a

look?'



'it occur to I,' Malkin reply, thoughtfully. 'but they sail in

ten day, and--well, I'm afraid I couldn't get ready in time. and then

I've promise to look after some little affair for Mrs. Jacox--some

trifle money matter. but later in the year--who know?'



Earwaker half repent of his promise to visit the Jacox household, but

there be no possibility of excuse himself. so on Sunday he journey

with his friend down to Wrotham. Mrs. Jacox and her child be very

comfortably establish in a small new house. when the companion

enter they find the mother alone in her sitting-room, and she

receive they with an effusiveness very distasteful to Earwaker.



'now you shouldn't!' be her first exclamation to Malkin. 'indeed you

shouldn't! it's really very naughty of you. o Mr. Earwaker! who ever

take so much pleasure in do kindness? do look at this _beautiful_

book that Mr. Malkin have send as a present to my little Bella. o Mr.

Earwaker!'



the journalist be at once strike with her tone and manner as she

address Malkin. he remark that phrase, 'my little Bella', and it

occur to he that Mrs. Jacox have be grow young since he make

her acquaintance on the tower of Notre Dame. when the girl present

themselves, they also appear to he more juvenile; Bella, in

particular, be dress with an exaggeration of childishness decidedly

not become. one have but to look into her face to see that she

answer perfectly to Malkin's description; she be a young lady, and

no child. a very pretty young lady, moreover; give to colour, but

with no silly simper; intelligent about the eye and lip; modest, in a

natural and sweet way. he converse with she, and in do so be

disagreeably affect by certain glance she occasionally cast towards

her mother. one would have say that she fear censure, though it be

hard to see why.



on the return journey Earwaker make known some of his impression,

though not all.



'I like the girl,' he say, 'Bella especially. but I cann't say much

good of their mother.'



they be opposite each other in the railway carriage. Malkin lean

forward with earnest, anxious face.



'that's my own trouble,' he whisper. 'I'm confoundedly uneasy about

it. I don't think she's bring they up at all in a proper way.

Earwaker, I would pay down five thousand pound for the possibility of

take Bella away altogether.'



the other muse.



'but, mind you,' pursue Malkin, 'she's not a _bad_ woman. by no mean!

thoroughly good-hearted I'm convince; only a little weak here.' he

tap his forehead. 'I respect she, for all she have suffer, and her

way of go through it. but she ben't the ideal mother, you know.'



on his way home, Malkin turn into his friend's chamber 'for five

minute'. at two in the morning he be still there, and his talk in the

meanwhile have be of nothing but scheme for protect Bella against

her mother's more objectionable influence. on take leave, he ask:



'any news of Peak yet?'



'none. I haven't see Moxey for a long time.'



'do you think Peak will look you up again, if he's in London?'



'no, I think he'll keep away. and I half hope he will; I shouldn't

quite know how to behave. ten to one he's in London now. I suppose he

couldn't stay at Exeter. but he may have leave England.'



they part, and for a week do not see each other. then, on Monday

evening, when Earwaker be very busy with a mass of manuscript, the

well-know knock sound from the passage, and Malkin receive

admission. the look he wear be appalling, a look such as only some

fearful catastrophe could warrant.



'be you busy?' he ask, in a voice very unlike his own.



Earwaker could not doubt that the trouble be this time serious. he

abandon his work, and give himself wholly to his friend's service.



'an awful thing have happen,' Malkin begin. 'how the deuce shall I

tell you? oh, the ass I have make of myself! but I couldn't help it;

there seem no way out of it.'



'well? what?'



'it be last night, but I couldn't come to you till now. by Jove! I

veritably thought of send you a note, and then kill myself. early

this morning I be within an ace of suicide. believe I, old friend.

this be no farce.'



'I'm wait.'



'yes, yes; but I cann't tell you all at once. sure you're not busy? I

know I pester you. I be down at Wrotham yesterday. I haven't mean to

go, but the temptation be too strong. I get there at five o'clock, and

find that the girl be go to have tea with some young friend.

well, I ben't altogether sorry; it be a good opportunity for a little

talk with their mother. and I _have_ the talk. but, oh, ass that I be!'



he smite the side of his head savagely.



'can you guess, Earwaker? can you give a shot at what happen?'



'perhaps I might,' reply the other, gravely.



'well?'



'that woman ask you to marry she.'



Malkin leap from his chair, and sink back again.



'it come to that. yes, upon my word, it come to that. she say she have

fall in love with I--that be the long and short of it. and I have

never say a word that could suggest--oh, confound it! what a frightful

scene it be!'



'you take a final leave of she?'



Malkin stare with eye of anguish into his friend's face, and at

length whisper thickly:



'I say I would!'



'what? take leave?'



'marry she!'



Earwaker have much ado to check an impatiently remonstrant laugh. he

pause awhile, then begin his expostulation, at first treat the

affair as too absurd for grave argument.



'my boy,' he conclude, 'you have get into a preposterous scrape, and I

see only one way out of it. you must flee. when do your brother start

for the Antipodes?'



'Thursday morning.'



'then you go with he; there's an end of it.'



Malkin listen with the blank, despairing look of a man condemn to

death.



'do you hear I?' urge the other. 'go home and pack. on Thursday I'll

see you off.'



'I cann't bring myself to that,' come in a groan from Malkin. 'I've

never yet do anything to be seriously ashamed of, and I cann't run

away after promise marriage. it would weigh upon I for the rest of

my life.'



'Humbug! would it weigh upon you less to marry the mother, and all the

time be in love with the daughter? to my mind, there's something

peculiarly loathsome in the suggestion.'



'but, look here; Bella be very young, really very young indeed. it's

possible that I have delude myself. perhaps I don't really care for

she in the way I imagine. it's more than likely that I might be

content to regard she with fatherly affection.'



'even suppose that, with what sort of affection do you regard Mrs

Jacox?'



Malkin writhe on his chair before reply.



'you mustn't misjudge she!' he exclaim. 'she be no heartless schemer.

the poor thing almost cry her eye out. it be a frightful scene. she

reproach herself bitterly. what _could_ I do? I have a tenderness for

she, there's no deny that. she have be so vilely use, and have

bear it all so patiently. how abominable it would be if I deal she

another blow!'



the journalist raise his eyebrow, and utter inarticulate sound.



'be anything say about Bella?' he ask, abruptly.



'not a word. I'm convinced she don't suspect that I think of Bella

like that. the fact be, I have mislead she. she think all along that

my chief interest be in _her_.'



'indeed? then what be the ground of her self-reproach that you speak

of?'



'how defective you be in the appreciation of delicate feeling!' cry

Malkin frantically, start up and rush about the room. 'she

reproach herself for having permit I to get entangle with a

widow old than myself, and the mother of two child. what could be

simple?'



Earwaker begin to appreciate the danger of the situation. if he

insist upon his view of Mrs. Jacox's behaviour (though it be not the

harsh that the circumstance suggest, for he be dispose to

believe that the widow have really lose her heart to her kind, eccentric

champion), the result would probably be to confirm Malkin in his

resolution of self-sacrifice. the man must be save, if possible, from

such calamity, and this would not be effect by merely demonstrate

that he be on the highroad to ruin. it be necessary to try another

tack.



'it seem to I, Malkin,' he resume, gravely, 'that it be you who be

deficient in right feeling. in offer to marry this poor woman, you

do she the grave wrong.'



'what? how?'



'you know that it be impossible for you to love she. you know that you

will repent, and that she will be aware of it. you be not the kind of

man to conceal your emotion. Bella will grow up, and--well, the state

of thing willn't tend to domestic felicity. for Mrs Jacox's own sake, it

be your duty to put an end to this folly before it have go too far.'



the other give earnest ear, but with no sign of shake conviction.



'yes,' he say. 'I know this be one way of look at it. but it

assume that a man cann't control himself, that his sense of honour

ben't strong enough to keep he in the right way. I don't think you

quite understand I. I be not a passionate man; the proof be that I

have never fall in love since I be sixteen. I think a great deal of

domestic peace, a good deal more than of romantic enthusiasm. if I

marry Mrs. Jacox, I shall make she a good and faithful husband,--so

much I can safely say of myself.'



he wait, but Earwaker be not ready with a rejoinder.



'and there's another point. I have always admit the defect of my

character--an inability to settle down. now, if I run away to New

Zealand, with the sense of having dishonour myself, I shall be a mere

Wandering Jew for the rest of my life. all hope of redemption will be

over. of the two course now open to I, that of marriage with Mrs.

Jacox be decidedly the less disadvantageous. grant that I have make

a fool of myself, I must abide by the result, and make the good of it.

and the plain fact be, I _cann't_ treat she so disgracefully; I _cann't_

burden my conscience in this way. I believe it would end in suicide; I

do, indeed.'



'this sound all very well, but it be weakness and selfishness.'



'how can you say so?'



'there's no proving to so short-sight a man the result of his

mistaken course. I've a good mind to let you have your way just for the

satisfaction of say afterwards, "don't I tell you so?" you propose

to behave with abominable injustice to two people, put yourself

aside. don't it occur to you that Bella may already look upon you as

her future husband? haven't you do your good to plant that idea in

her mind?'



Malkin start, but quickly recover himself.



'no, I haven't! I have behave with the utmost discretion. Bella think

of I only as of a friend much old than herself.'



'I don't believe it!'



'Nonsense, Earwaker! a child of fifteen!'



'the other day you have quite a different view, and after see she

again I agree with you. she be a young girl, and if not already in

love with you, be on the way to be so.'



'that will come to nothing when she hear that I be go to be her

step-father.'



'far more likely to develop into a grief that will waste the good part

of her lifetime. she will be shock and make miserable. but do as you

like. I be tired of argue.'



Earwaker affect to abandon the matter in disgust. for several minute

there be silence, then a low voice sound from the corner where

Malkin stand lean.



'so it be your honest belief that Bella have begin to think of I in

that way?'



'I be convinced of it.'



'but if I run away, I shall never see she again.'



'why not? _she_ willn't run away. come back when thing have square

themselves. write to Mrs. Jacox from the end of the earth, and let she

understand that there be no possibility of your marry she.'



'tell she about Bella, you mean?'



'no, that's just what I don't mean. avoid any mention of the girl. come

back when she be seventeen, and, if she be willing, carry she off to be

happy ever after.'



'but she may have fall in love with someone else.'



'I think not. you must risk it, at all event.'



'look here!' Malkin come forward eagerly. 'I'll write to Mrs. Jacox

to-night, and make a full confession. I'll tell she exactly how the

case stand. she's a good woman; she'll gladly sacrifice herself for

the sake of her daughter.'



Earwaker be firm in resistance. he have no faith whatever in the

widow's capacity for self-immolation, and foresaw that his friend would

be draw into another 'frightful scene', result probably in a

marriage as soon as the licence could be obtain.



'when be you to see she again?' he inquire.



'on Wednesday.'



'will you undertake to do nothing whatever till Wednesday morning, and

then to have another talk with I? I'll come and see you about ten

o'clock.'



in the end Malkin be constrain into make this engagement, and not

long after midnight the journalist manage to get rid of he.



on Tuesday afternoon arrive a distracted note. 'I shall keep my

promise, and I willn't try to see you till you come here tomorrow. but I

be sore beset. I have receive _three_ letter from Mrs. Jacox, all

long and horribly pathetic. she seem to have a presentiment that I

shall forsake she. what a beast I shall be if I do! Tom come here

to-night, and I think I shall tell he all.'



the last sentence be a relief to the reader; he know nothing of Mr

Thomas Malkin, but there be a fair presumption that this gentleman

would not see his brother bent on make such a notable fool of himself

without vigorous protest.



at the appoint hour next morning, Earwaker reach his friend's

lodging, which be now at Kilburn. on enter the room he see, not

the familiar figure, but a solid, dark-face, black-whisker man, whom

a faint resemblance enable he to identify as Malkin the young.



'I be expect you,' say Thomas, as they shake hand. 'my brother be

completely floor. when I get here an hour ago, I insist on his

lie down, and now I think he's asleep. if you don't mind, we'll let

he rest for a little. I believe he have hardly close his eye since

this unfortunate affair happen.'



'it rejoice I to hear that he be go to ask your advice. how do

matter stand?'



'you know Mrs. Jacox?'



Thomas be obviously a man of discretion, but less intellectual than

his brother; he speak like one who be accustomed to the management of

affair. at first he be inclined to a polite reserve, but Earwaker's

conversation speedily put he more at ease.



'I have quite make up my mind,' he say presently, 'that we must take

he away with we to-morrow. the voyage will bring he to his sense.'



'of course he resist?'



'yes, but if you will give I your help, I think we can manage he. he

be not very strong-willed. in a spasmodic way he can defy everyone, but

the steady pressure of common sense will prevail with he, I think.'



they have talk for half-an-hour, when the door open and the object

of their benevolent care stand before they. he be clothe in a

dressing-gown, and his disordered hair heighten the look of illness

which his feature present.



'why don't you call I?' he ask his brother, irritably. 'Earwaker, I

beg a thousand pardon! I'm not very well; I've overslept myself.'



'yes, yes; come and sit down.'



Thomas make an offer to leave they.



'don't go,' say Malkin. 'no need whatever. you know why Earwaker have

be so kind as to come here. we may as well talk it over together.'



he sit on the table, swinge a tassel of his dressing-gown round and

round.



'now, what do you really think of do?' ask the journalist, in a

kind voice.



'I don't know. I absolutely do not know. I'm unutterably wretched.'



'in that case, will you let your brother and I decide for you? we have

no desire but for your good, and we be perfectly at one in our

judgment.'



'of course I know what you will propose!' cry the other, excitedly.

'from the prudential point of view, you be right, I have no doubt. but

how can you protect I against remorse? if you have receive letter

such as these three,' he pull they out of a pocket, 'you would be as

miserable as I be. if I don't keep my promise, I shall never know

another moment of peace.'



'you certainly willn't if you _do_ keep it,' remark Thomas.



'no,' add Earwaker, 'and one if not two other person will be put

into the same case. whereas by boldly face these reproach of

conscience, you do a great kindness to the other.'



'if only you could assure I of that!'



'I _can_ assure you. that be to say, I can give it as my unassailable

conviction.'



and Earwaker once more enlarge upon the theme, state it from every

point of view that serve his purpose.



'you're make a mountain out of a mole-heap,' be the confirmatory

remark that come from Thomas.  'this respectable lady will get over her

sorrow quickly enough, and some day she'll be only too glad to have you

for a son-in-law, if Miss Bella still please you.'



'it's only right,' urge Earwaker, in pursuance of his subtler

intention, 'that you should bear the bad of the suffering, for the

trouble have come out of your own thoughtlessness. you be fond of

say that you have behave with the utmost discretion; so far from

that you have be outrageously indiscreet. I foresaw that something of

this kind might come to pass'----



'then why the devil don't you warn I?' shout Malkin, in an agony of

nervous strain.



'it would have be useless. in fact, I foresaw it too late.'



the discussion continue for an hour. by careful insistence on the idea

of self-sacrifice, Earwaker by degree demolish the argument his

friend keep put forward. Thomas, who have go impatiently to the

window, turn round with word that be mean to be final.



'it's quite decide. you begin your preparation at once, and to-morrow

morning you go on board with we.'



'but if I don't go to Wrotham this afternoon, she'll be here either

to-night or the first thing to-morrow. I'm sure of it!'



'by four or five o'clock,' say Earwaker, 'you can have break up the

camp. you've often do it at short notice. go to an hotel for the

night.'



'I must write to the poor woman.'



'do as you like about that.'



'who be to help she, if she get into difficulty--as she's always

do? who be to advise she about Bella's education? who be to pay--I

mean, who will see to----? oh, confound it!'



the listener glance at each other.



'be her affair in order?' ask Earwaker. 'has she a sufficient

income?'



'for ordinary need, quite sufficient. But'----



'then you needn't be in the least uneasy. let she know where you be,

when the equator be between you. watch over her interest from a

distance, if you like. I can as good as promise you that Bella will

wait hopefully to see her friend again.'



Malkin succumb to argument and exhaustion. face Earwaker with a

look of pathetic appeal, he ask hoarsely:



'will you stand by I till it's over? have you time?'



'I can give you till five o'clock.'



'then I'll go and dress. ring the bell, Tom, and ask they to bring up

some beer.'



before three have strike, the arrangement for flight be complete. a

heavily-laden cab bore away Malkin's personal property; within sit the

unhappy man and his faithful friend.



the next morning Earwaker go down to Tilbury, and say farewell to

the traveller on board the steamship Orient. Mrs. Thomas have already

take her brother-in-law under her special care.



'it's only three child to look after, instead of two,' she remark,

in a laughing aside to the journalist. 'how grateful he will be to you

in a few day! and I'm sure _we_ be already.'



Malkin's eye be no long quite lustreless. at the last moment he

talk with animation of 'two year hence', and there be vigour in the

wave of his hand as the vessel start seaward.







CHAPTER III





Peak lose no time in leave Exeter. to lighten his baggage, and to get

rid of possession to which hateful memory attach, he sell all his

book that have any bearing on theology. the incomplete translation of

_Bibel und Natur_ he commit to the flame in Mrs Roots's kitchen,

scatter its black remnant with savage thrust of the poker. Whilst

engage in packing, he debate with himself whether or not he should

take leave of the few acquaintance to whom he be indebted for

hospitality and other kindness. the question be: have Buckland

Warricombe already warn these people against he? probably it have

seem to Buckland the wise course to be content with drive the

hypocrite away; and, if this be so, regard for the future dictate a

retirement from exeter which should in no way resemble secret flight.

Sidwell's influence with her parent would perhaps withhold they from

make his disgrace know, and in a few year he might be glad that he

have behave with all possible prudence. in the end, he decide to write

to Mr. Lilywhite, say that he be oblige to go away at a moment's

notice, and that he fear it would be necessary altogether to change

the scheme of life which he have have in view. this be the good way.

from the Lilywhites, other people would hear of he, and perchance

their conjecture would be charitable.



without much hesitation he have settle his immediate plan. to London

he would not return, for he dread the temptation to which the

proximity of Sidwell would expose he, and he have no mind to meet with

Moxey or Earwaker. as it be now imperative that he should find work of

the old kind, he could not do well than go to Bristol, where, from

the safe ground of a cheap and obscure lodging, he might make

inquiry, watch advertisement, and so on. he already know of

establishment in Bristol where he might possibly obtain employment.

live with the utmost economy, he need not fall into difficulty for

more than a year, and before then his good repute with the Rotherhithe

firm would ensure he some position or other; if not in Bristol, then

at Newcastle, St. Helen's--any great centre of fume and malodorous

industry. he be ready to work, would delight in work. idleness be now

the intolerable thing.



so to Bristol he betake himself, and there make his temporary abode.

after spend a few week in fruitless search for an engagement, he at

length pay his oft-postpone visit to Twybridge. in the old home he

feel completely a stranger, and his relative strengthen the feeling

by declare he so change in appearance that they hardly know his

face. with his mother only could he talk in anything like an intimate

way, and the falsehood with which he be oblige to answer she

question all but destroy the pleasure he would otherwise have find

in be affectionately tend. his sister, Mrs Cusse, be happy in her

husband, her child, and a flourish business. Oliver be make

money, and enjoy distinction among the shopkeeping community. his

aunt still deal in millinery, and keep up her acquaintance with

respectable family. to Godwin all be like a dream dream for the

second time. he could not acknowledge any actual connection between

these people and himself. but their characteristic no long gravely

offend he, and he willingly recognise the homespun worth which

their life display. it be clear to he that by no possible agency

of circumstance could he have be hold in normal relation with his

kinsfolk. however smooth his career, it must have waft he to an

immeasurable distance from Twybridge. Nature have decree that he be to

resemble the animal which, once rear, go forth in complete

independence of birthplace and the tie of blood. it be a harsh fate,

but in what have not fate be harsh to he? the one consolation be

that he alone suffer. his mother be no doubt occasionally trouble

by solicitude on his account, but she could not divine his inward

misery, and an assurance that he have no material care suffice to

set her mind at ease.



'you be very like your father, Godwin,' she say, with a sigh. 'he

couldn't rest, however well he seem to be get on. there be

always something he want, and yet he don't know what it be.'



'yes, I must be like he,' Godwin reply, smile.



he stay five day, then return to Bristol. a week after that, his

mother forward to he a letter which have come to Twybridge. he at

once recognise the writing, and break the envelope with curiosity.



'if you should be in London [the note begin], I beg you to let I see

you. there be something I have to say. to speak to you for a few

minute I would come any distance. don't accuse I of behave

treacherously; it be not my fault. I know you would rather avoid I,

but do consent to hear what I have to say. if you have no intention of

come to London, will you write and let I know where you be live?



what could Marcella have to say to he? nothing surely that he at all

care to hear. no doubt she imagine that he might be in ignorance of

the circumstance which have lead to Buckland Warricombe's discovery; she

wish to defend herself against the suspicion of 'treachery'. he

laugh carelessly, and throw her note aside.



two month pass, and his effort to find employment be still vain,

though he have receive conditional promise. the solitude of his life

grow burdensome. several time he begin a letter to Sidwell, but his

difficulty in writing be so great that he destroy the attempt. in

truth, he know not how to address she. the word he pen be tumid,

meaningless. he could not send profession of love, for his heart

seem to be suffer a paralysis, and the laborious artificiality of

his style must have be evident. the only excuse for break silence

would be to let she know that he have resume honest work; he must wait

till the opportunity offer. it do not distress he to be without

news of she. if she wish to write, and be only withhold by ignorance

of his whereabouts, it be well; if she have no thought of send he a

word, it do not matter. he love she, and consciously nourish hope,

but for the present there be nothing intolerable in separation. his

state of mind result partly from nervous reaction, and in part from a

sense that only by silent suffering could his dignity in Sidwell's eye

be ultimately restore. between the evil past and the hopeful future

must be a complete break.



his thought keep turn to London, though not because Sidwell might

still be there. he feel urgent need of speak with a friend. Moxey

be perhaps no long to be consider one; but Earwaker would be

tolerant of human weakness. to have a long talk with Earwaker would

help he to recover his mental balance, to understand himself and his

position well. so one morning in March, on the spur of the moment, he

take train and be once more in the metropolis. on his way he have

determine to send a note to Earwaker before call at Staple Inn. he

write it at a small hotel in Paddington, where he take a room for the

night, and then spend the evening at a theatre, as the good way of

kill time.



by the first post next morning come a card, whereon Earwaker have

write: 'be here, if you can, at two o'clock. shall be glad to see

you.'



'so you have be new-furnish!' Godwin remark, as he be admit

to the chamber. 'you look much more comfortable.'



'I'm glad you think so. it be the general opinion.'



they have shake hand as though this be one of the ordinary meeting

of old time, and their voice scarcely belie the appearance. peak

move about the study, glance at picture and book, Earwaker eye

he the while with not unfriendly expression. they be sincerely glad

to see each other, and when Peak seat himself it be with an audible

sigh of contentment.



'and what be you do?' he inquire.



the journalist give a brief account of his affair, and Peak brighten

with pleasure.



'this be good news. I know you would shake off the ragamuffin before

long. give I some of your back number, will you? I shall be curious

to examine your new style.'



'and you?--Come to live in London?'



'no; I be at Bristol, but only wait. there's a chance of an

analyst's place in Lancashire; but I may give the preference to an

opening I have hear of in Belgium. well to go abroad, I think.'



'perhaps so.'



'I have a question to ask you. I suppose you talk about that

_critical_ article of mine _before_ you receive my request for

silence?'



'that's how it be,' Earwaker reply, calmly.



'yes; I understand. it don't matter.'



the other puff at his pipe, and move uneasily.



'I be take for grant,' Peak continue, 'that you know how I have

spend my time down in Devonshire.'



'in outline. need we trouble about the detail?'



'no. but don't suppose that I should feel any shame in talk to you

about they. that would be a confession of base motive. you and I have

study each other, and we can exchange thought on most subject with

to their logical issue. an opportunity offer of achieve the supreme

end to which my life be direct, and what scruple could stand in my

the reward of successful artifice. to live be to pursue the object of

have gain everything I really desire. I could tell you the strange

story, but there a scruple _do_ interpose. if we live another twenty

year--but now I can only talk about myself.'



'and this hope of which you speak,' say Earwaker, with a grave smile,

'point you at present to sober work among your retort and test-tube?'



'yes, it do.'



'good. then I can put faith in the result.'



'yet the hope begin in a lie,' rejoin Peak, bitterly. 'it will always

be pleasant to look back upon that, willn't it? you see: by no

conceivable honest effort could I have gain this point. life utterly

deny to I the satisfaction of my strong instinct, so long as I

plod on without cause of shame; the moment I deny my faith, and

put on a visage of brass, great possibility open before I. of

course I understand the moralist's position. it behove I, though I

know that a barren and solitary track would be my only treading to the

end, to keep courageously onward. if I cann't _believe_ that any such

duty be impose upon I, where be the obligation to persevere, the

morality of do so? that be the bad hypocrisy. I have be honest,

inasmuch as I have act in accordance with my actual belief.'



'M--m--m,' muttered Earwaker, slowly. 'then you have never be

trouble with a twinge of conscience?'



'with a thousand! I have be rack, martyr. what have that to do

with it? do you suppose I attach any final significance to those

torment? Conscience be the same in my view as an inherit disease

which may possibly break out on any most innocent physical

indulgence.--What end have I be pursue? be it criminal? be it mean?

I want to win the love of a woman--nothing more. to do that, I have

have to behave like the grovel villain who have no desire but to fill

his pocket. and with success!--You understand that, Earwaker? I have

succeed! what respect can I have for the common morality, after this?'



'you have succeed?' the other ask, thoughtfully. 'I could have

imagine that you have be in appearance successful'----



he pause, and Peak resume with vehemence:



'no, not in appearance only. I cann't tell you the story'----



'I don't wish you to'----



'but what I have win be win for ever. the triumph no long rest on

deceit. what I insist upon be that by deceit only be it render

possible. if a starve man succeed in steal a loaf of bread, the

food will benefit he no less than if he have purchase it; it be good,

true sustenance, no matter how he get it. to be sure, the man may

prefer starvation; he may have so strong a metaphysical faith that

death be welcome in comparison with what he call dishonour. I--I have

no such faith; and million of other man in this country would tell the

blunt truth if they say the same. I have _use mean_, that's all. the

old way of candour lead I to bitterness and cursing; by dissimulation I

have win something more glorious than tongue can tell.'



it be in the endeavour to expel the subtle enemy of his peace that

Godwin dwell so defiantly upon this view of the temptation to which he

have yield. since his farewell interview with Sidwell, he know no rest

from the torment of a mocking voice which bid he bear in mind that

all his dishonour have be superfluous, see that whilst he play

the part of a zealous Christian, Sidwell herself be drift far

and far from the old religion. this voice mingle with his dream,

and leave not a wake hour untrouble. he refuse to believe it, strove

against the suggestion as a half-despair man do against the

persistent thought of suicide. if only he could obtain Earwaker's

assent to the plan he put forward, it would support he in disregard of

idle regret.



'it be impossible,' say the journalist, 'for anyone to determine

whether that be true or not--for you, as much as for anyone else. be

glad that you have shake off the evil and retain the good, no use in

say more than that.'



'yes,' declare the other, stubbornly, 'there be good in expose false

view of life. I ought to have come utterly to grief and shame, and

instead'----



'Instead----? well?'



'what I have tell you.'



'which I interpret thus: that you have permission to redeem your

character, if possible, in the eye of a woman you have grievously

mislead.'



Godwin frown.



'who suggest this to you, Earwaker?'



'you; no one else. I don't even know who the woman be of whom you

speak.'



'Grant you be right. as an honest man, I should never have win she

faint interest.'



'it be absurd for we to talk about it. think in the way that be most

helpful to you,--that, no doubt, be a reasonable rule. let we have do

with all these obscurity, and come to a practical question. can I be

of any use to you? would you care, for instance, to write an article

now and then on some scientific matter that have a popular interest? I

think I could promise to get that kind of thing print for you. or

would you review an occasional book that happen to be in your line?'



Godwin reflect.



'thank you,' he reply, at length. 'I should be glad of such work--if

I can get into the mood for do it properly. that willn't be just yet;

but perhaps when I have find a place'----



'think it over. write to I about it.'



Peak glance round the room.



'you don't know how glad I be,' he say, 'that your prosperity show

itself in this region of bachelordom. if I have see you in a

comfortable house, marry to a woman worthy of you--I couldn't have

be sincere in my congratulation: I should have envy you so

fiercely.'



'you're a strange fellow. twenty year hence--as you say just now--you

will one way or another have got rid of your astounding illusion. at

fifty--well, let we say at sixty--you will have a chance of see

thing without these preposterous sexual spectacle.'



'I hope so. every stage of life have its power and enjoyment. when I

be old, I hope to perceive and judge without passion of any kind. but

be that any reason why my youth should be frustrate? we have only one

life, and I want to live mine throughout.'



soon after this Peak rise. he remember that the journalist's time be

valuable, and that he no long have the right to demand more of it than

could be grant to any casual caller. Earwaker behave with all

friendliness, but their relation have necessarily suffer a change.

More than a year of separation, spend by the one in accumulate

memory of dishonour, have give the other an enviable position among

man; Earwaker have his place in the social system, his grow circle of

friend, his congenial labour; perhaps--notwithstanding the tone in

which he speak of marriage--his hope of domestic happiness. all this

with no sacrifice of principle. he be fortunate in his temper, moral

and intellectual; partly direct circumstance, partly guide by

their pressure, he advance on the way of harmonious development.

nothing great would come of his endeavour, but what he aim at he

steadily perfect. and this in spite of the adverse condition under

which he begin his course. nature have be kind to he; what more could

one say?



when he go forth into the street again, Godwin feel his heart sink.

his solitude be the more complete for this hour of friendly dialogue.

no other companionship offer itself; if he linger here, it must be

as one of the drift crowd, as an idle and envious spectator of the

business and pleasure rife about he. he durst not approach that

quarter of the town where Sidwell be live--if indeed she still

remain here. happily, the vastness of London enable he to think of

she as at a great distance; by keep to the district in which he now

wander he be practically as remote from she as when he walk the

street of Bristol.



yet there be one person who would welcome he eagerly if he choose to

visit she. and, after all, might it not be as well if he hear what

Marcella have to say to he? he could not go to the house, for it would

be disagreeable to encounter Moxey; but, if he write, Marcella would

speedily make an appointment. after an hour or two of purposeless
