that really concern he; in any case, it be a final meeting with

Marcella, to whom he perhaps owe this much courtesy.



the reply be as prompt as that from Earwaker. by the morning post come

meet no one here.'



by eleven o'clock he be at Notting Hill; in the drawing-room, he sit

perhaps it be nothing more than gladness at see he again.



'have you come to live in London?' she ask, when they have shake

hand without a word.



'I be only here for a day or two.'



'my letter reach you without delay?'



'yes. it be send from Twybridge to Bristol. I don't reply then, as I

have no prospect of be in London.'



'will you sit down? you can stay for a few minute?'



he seat himself awkwardly. now that he be in Marcella's presence, he

'I want to speak of what happen in this house about Christmas time,'

you know that anything which happen here have any importance for I?'



'I don't know that it have. but I feel sure that Mr. Warricombe mean to

speak to you about it.'



'yes, he do.'



'but do he tell you the exact truth? or be you lead to suppose that I

have break my promise to you?'



unwilling to introduce any mention of Sidwell, Peak prefer to

simplify the story by attribute to Buckland all the information he

'I understand,' he reply, 'that Warricombe have come here in the hope

of learn more about I, and that certain fact come out in general

that the truth should soon or later come out. he always suspect I.'



'but I want you to know,' say Marcella, 'that I have no willing part in

it. I promise you not to speak even to my brother, and I should never

Warricombe come here, neither Christian nor I would have enlighten

'why trouble about it? I quite believe your account.'



'you _do_ believe it? you know I would not have injure you?'



'I be sure you have no wish to,' Godwin reply, in as unsentimental a

'yes. chiefly that.'



'let I put your mind at rest,' pursue the other, with quiet

just as it should have do. Warricombe relieve I from a false

position. if _he_ haven't do so, I must very soon have do it for

myself. let we rejoice that thing work together for such obvious good.

a few more lesson of this kind, and we shall acknowledge that the

world be the good possible.'



he laugh, but the tense expression of Marcella's feature do not

relax.



'you say you be live in Bristol?'



'for a time.'



'have you abandon Exeter?'



the word imply something that Marcella could not utter more plainly.

her face complete the question.



'and the clerical career as well,' he answer.



but he know that she seek more than this, and his voice again break

the silence.



'perhaps you have hear that already? be you in communication with

Miss Moorhouse?'



she shake her head.



'but probably Warricombe have tell your brother----?'



'what?'



'oh, of his success in rid exeter of my objectionable presence.'



'Christian haven't see he again, nor have I.'



'I only wish to assure you that I have suffer no injury. my

'content?' she smile incredulously. 'for how long?'



'who can say? I have lose the habit of look far forward.'



Marcella keep silence so long that he conclude she have nothing more to

say to he. it be an opportunity for take leave without emotional

stress, and he rise from his chair.



'don't go yet,' she say at once. 'it ben't only this that I'----



her voice be check.



'can I be of any use to you in Bristol?' Peak ask, determined to

avoid the trial he see approach.



'there be something more I want to say,' she pursue, seem not to

hear he. 'you pretend to be content, but I know that be impossible.

you talk of go back to a dull routine of toil, when what you most

again she fail to command her voice. Godwin raise his eye, and be

Godwin be not so much surprised as disconcert. he know that

Marcella's nature be of large mould, and that whether she act for

ardour with which she plead, and the magnitude of the benefaction she

hope to draw he within her power by the force of such an obligation,

or if in truth she seek only to appease her heart with the exercise

of generosity.



'you will let I?' she pant forth, watch he with brilliant eye.

to me,--except that it can do _you_ good. but you can thank I if you

will. I be not above be thank.' she laugh unnaturally. 'go and

travel at first, as you wish to. write I a short letter every

month--every two month, just that I may know you be enjoy your

life. it be agree, ben't it?'



she hold her hand to he, but Peak draw away, his face avert.



'how can you give I the pain of refuse such an offer?' he exclaim,

with remonstrance which be all but anger. 'you know the thing be

utterly impossible. I should be ridiculous if I argue about it for a

'I cann't see that it be impossible.'



'then you must take my word for it. but I have no right to speak to you

in that way,' he add, more kindly, see the profound humiliation

which fall upon she. 'you mean to come to my aid at a time when I

seem to you lonely and miserable. it be a generous impulse, and I do

indeed thank you. I shall always remember it and be grateful to you.'



Marcella's face be again in shadow. its lineament harden to an

expression of cold, stern dignity.



'I have make a mistake,' she say. 'I think you above common way of

think.'



'yes, you put I on too high a pedestal,' Peak answer, try to

speak humorously. 'one of my fault be that I be apt to mistake my own

position in the same way.'



'you think yourself ambitious. oh, if you know really great ambition!

go back to your laboratory, and work for wage. I would have save you

from that.'



the tone be not vehement, but the word bit all the deep for their

unimpassioned accent. Godwin could make no reply.



'I hope,' she continue, 'we may meet a few year hence. by that time

you will have learn that what I offer be not impossible. you will

wish you have dare to accept it. I know what your _ambition_ be. wait

make to understand that. you will throw away all the good part of your

life. meet I in a few year, and tell I the story of the interval.'



'I will engage to do that, Marcella.'



'you will? but not to tell I the truth. you will not dare to tell the

truth.'



'why not?' he ask, indifferently. 'decidedly I shall owe it you in

return for your frankness to-day. till then--good-bye.'



she do not refuse her hand, and as he move away she watch he with

a smile of slight good-nature.



on the morrow Godwin be back in Bristol, and there he dwelt for

another six month, a period of mental and physical lassitude. Earwaker

correspond with he, and urge he to attempt the work that have be

propose, but such effort be beyond his power.



he see one day in a literary paper an announcement that Reusch's _Bibel

und Natur_ be about to be publish in an english translation. so

someone else have successfully finish the work he undertake nearly two

year ago. he amuse himself with the thought that he could ever have

persevere so long in such profitless labour, and with a contemptuous

laugh he mutter '_Thohu wabohu_.'



just when the winter have set in, he receive an offer of a post in

chemical work at St. Helen's, and without delay travel northward.

the appointment be a poor one, and seem unlikely to be a step to

anything well, but his resource would not last more than another

half year, and employment of whatever kind come as welcome relief to

the tedium of his existence. establish in his new abode, he at length

write to Sidwell. she answer he at once in a short letter which he

might have show to anyone, so calm be its expression of interest,

so uncompromise its word of congratulation. it begin 'dear Mr.

Peak', and end with 'yours sincerely'. well, he have use the same

formality, and have utter his feeling with scarcely more of warmth.

disappointment trouble he for a moment, and for a moment only. he be

so far from Exeter, and far still from the life that he have lead

there. it seem to he all but certain that Sidwell write coldly, with

the intention of discourage his hope. what hope be he so foolish as

to entertain? his position poorer than ever, what could justify he in

write love-letter to a girl who, even if willing to marry he, must

not do so until he have a suitable home to offer she?



since his maturity, he have never know so long a freedom from passion.

one day he write to Earwaker: 'I begin to your independence with regard

to woman. it would be a strange thing if I become a convert to that way

of thinking, but once or twice of late I have imagine that it be

happen. my mind have all but recover its tone, and I be able to

read, to think--I mean really to _think_, not to muse. I get through

big and solid book. presently, if your offer still hold good, I shall

send you a scrap of write on something or other. the pestilent

atmosphere of this place seem to invigorate I. last Saturday evening

I take train, get away into the hill, and spend the Sunday

geologising. and a curious experience befell me,--one I have long, long

ago, in the Whitelaw day. sit down before some interesting strata,

I lose myself in something like nirvana, grow so subject to the idea of

vastness in geological time that all human desire and purpose

shrivel to ridiculous unimportance. awake for a minute, I try to

realise the passion which not long ago rent and rack I, but I be

flatly incapable of understand it. will this philosophic state

whether to hope or fear it.'

flatter his pride. 'let she learn that, after all, I be her superior.

let she fear to lose I. then, if her love be still to be depend

upon, she will before long find a way to our union. it be in her power,

if only she will it.'



so he sit down and write a short letter which seem to he a model of

dignified expression.







CHAPTER IV





Sidwell take no one into her confidence. the case be not one for

Part VII







CHAPTER I





at the close of a sultry day in September, when factory fume hang low

over the town of St. Helen's, and twilight thicken luridly, and the

air taste of sulphur, and the noise of the street, muffle in their

joint effect, have individually an ominous distinctness, Godwin Peak

walk with languid step to his lodging and the meal that there

await he. his vitality be at low ebb. the routine of his life

disgust he; the hope of release be a mockery. what be to be the

limit of this effort to redeem his character? how many year before the

past could be forget, and his claim to the style of honourable be

deem secure? rubbish! it be an idea out of old-fashioned romance.

what he be, he be, and no extent of dog duration at St. Helen's or

elsewhere, could affect his personality. what, practically, be to be

the end? if Sidwell have no money of her own, and no expectation from

her father, how could she ever become his wife? woman like this kind

of thing, this indefinite engagement to marry when something should

happen, which in all likelihood never would happen--this fantastic

mutual fidelity with only the airy reward. especially woman of a

certain age.



a heavy cart seem to be rumble in the next street. no, it be

thunder. if only a good rattle storm would sweep the bituminous

atmosphere, and allow a breath of pure air before midnight.



she could not be far from thirty. of course there prevail much

conventional nonsense about woman's age; there be plenty of woman who

reckon four decade, and yet retain all the essential charm of their

sex. and as a man get old, as he begin to persuade himself that at

forty one have scarce reach the prime of life----



the storm be come on in earnest. big drop begin to fall. he

quicken his pace, reach home, and ring the bell for a light.



his landlady come in with the announcement that a gentleman have call

to see he, about an hour ago; he would come again at seven o'clock.



'what name?'



none have be give. a youngish gentleman, speak like a Londoner.



it might be Earwaker, but that be not likely. Godwin sit down to his

plain meal, and after it light a pipe. thunder be still roll, but now

in the distance. he wait impatiently for seven o'clock.



to the minute, sound a knock at the house-door. a little delay, and

there appear Christian Moxey.



Godwin be surprised and embarrassed. his visitor have a very grave

face, and be thin, paler, than three year ago; he appear to

hesitate, but at length offer his hand.



'I get your address from Earwaker. I be oblige to see you--on

business.'



'business?'



'may I take my coat off? we shall have to talk.'



they sit down, and Godwin, unable to strike the note of friendship lest

he should be meet with repulse, break silence by regret that Moxey

should have have to make a second call.



'oh, that's nothing! I go and have dinner.--Peak, my sister be dead.'



their eye meet; something of the old kindness rise to either face.



'that must be a heavy blow to you,' murmur Godwin, possess with a

strange anticipation which he would not allow to take clear form.



'it be. she be ill for three month.' whilst stay in the country

last June she meet with an accident. she go for a long walk alone one

day, and in a steep lane she come up with a carter who be try to

make a wretched horse drag a load beyond its strength. the fellow be

perhaps half drunk; he stand there beat the horse unmercifully.

Marcella couldn't endure that kind of thing--impossible for she to pass

on and say nothing. she interfere, and try to persuade the man to

lighten his cart. he be insolent, attack the horse more furiously

than ever, and kick it so violently in the stomach that it fall. even

then he wouldn't stop his brutality. Marcella try to get between he

and the animal--just as it lash out with its heel. the poor girl be

so badly injure that she lie by the roadside until another carter take

she up and bring she back to the village. three month of accurse

suffering, and then happily come the end.'



a far, faint echoing of thunder fill the silence of their voice.

heavy rain splash upon the pavement.



'she say to I just before her death,' resume Christian, '"I have ill

luck when I try to do a kindness--but perhaps there be one more

chance." I don't know what she mean till afterwards. Peak, she have

leave nearly all her money to you.'



Godwin know it before the word be speak. his heart leap, and only

the dread of be observe enable he to control his feature. when

his tongue be release he say harshly:



'of course I cann't accept it.'



the word be utter independently of his will. he have no such

thought, and the sound of his voice shake he with alarm.



'why cann't you?' return Christian.



'I have no right--it belong to you, or to some other relative--it

would be'----



his stammering break off. flush and chill run through he; he could

not raise his eye from the ground.



'it belong to no one but you,' say Moxey, with cold persistence. 'she

last wish be to do you a kindness, and I, at all event, shall never

consent to frustrate her intention. the legacy represent something

more than eight hundred a year, as the investment now stand. this will

make you independent--of everything and everybody.' he look meaningly

at the listener. 'her own life be not a very happy one; she do what

she could to save yours from a like doom.'



Godwin at last look up.



'do she speak of I during her illness?'



'she ask I once, soon after the accident, what have become of you. as

I know from Earwaker, I be able to tell she.'



a long silence follow. Christian's voice be soft when he resume.



'you never know she. she be the one woman in ten thousand--at once

strong and gentle; a fine intellect, and a heart of rare tenderness.

but because she have not the kind of face that'----



he check himself.



'to the end her mind keep its clearness and courage. one day she

remind I of Heine--how we have talk of that "conversion" on the

mattress-grave, and have pity the noble intellect subdue by disease.

"I shalln't live long enough," she say, "to incur that danger. what I

have think ever since I could study, I think now, and shall to the

if I have lack that courage.'



'it be right,' mutter Godwin.



'and I wear no mourning, you see. all that kind of thing be ignoble. I

be rob of a priceless companionship, but I don't care to go about

invite people's pity. if only I could forget those month of

suffering! some day I shall, perhaps, and think of she only as she

'no. our cousin Janet be often with we.' Christian speak with avert

Lowton, ten mile or more from Kingsmill. Janet come over very often.'



Godwin muse on this development of the girl whom he remember so

in the field by Twybridge, when we first meet?'



the old friendliness be reappear in his manner, he be yield to

the impulse to be communicative, confidential, which have always

'I remember,' Godwin murmur.



'if only my word then have have any weight with you! and if only I have

act upon my own advice! just for those few week I be sane; I

the last word be in a low tone. Godwin glance at the speaker,

whose sadness be not banish, but illumine with a ray of calm hope.

'I understand.'

Heaven be thank that she be forgive enough to do so! what would

have become of I now?'



'how be you go to live, then?' Godwin ask, absently.



'how? my income be sufficient'----

any case, I don't think she could for long; her strength ben't equal to

'you think that?' exclaim the other, eagerly. 'I have try to see it

in that light. if I don't feel so despicable!'



'she, I take it, don't think you so,' Godwin mutter.



'but how can she understand? I have try to tell she everything, but

she refuse to listen. perhaps Marcella tell she all she care to know.'



'no doubt.'



each brood for a while over his own affair, then Christian revert

to the subject which concern they both.



'let we speak frankly. you will take this gift of Marcella's as it be

mean?'



how _be_ it mean? critic and analyst as ever, Godwin could not be

content to see in it the simple benefaction of a woman who die love

he. be it not rather the last subtle device of jealousy? Marcella

know that the legacy would be a temptation he could scarcely

resist--and know at the same time that, if he accept it, he

practically renounce his hope of marry Sidwell Warricombe.

Doubtless she have learn as much as she need to know of Sidwell's

position. refuse this bequest, he be as far as ever from the

possibility of ask Sidwell to marry he. profit by it, he stand

for ever indebted to Marcella, must need be grateful to she, and some

day, assuredly, would reveal the truth to whatever woman become his

wife. conflict of reasoning and emotion make it difficult to answer

Moxey's question.



'I must take time to think of it,' he say, at length.



'well, I suppose that be right. but--well, I know so little of your

circumstances'----



'be that strictly true?' Peak ask.



'yes. I have only the vague idea of what you have be do since

you leave we. of course I have try to find out.'



Godwin smile, rather gloomily.



'we willn't talk of it. I suppose you stay in St. Helen's for the night?'



'there's a train at 10.20. I have well go by it.'



'then let we forget everything but your own cheerful outlook. at ten,

I'll walk with you to the station.'



reluctantly at first, but before long with a quiet abandonment to the

joy that would not be suppress, Christian talk of his future wife.

in Janet he find every perfection. her mind be something more than

the companion of his own. already she have begin to inspire he with a

hopeful activity, and to foster the element of true manliness which he

be conscious of possess, though they have never yet have free play.

with a sense of luxurious safety, he submit to her influence,

know none the less that it be in his power to complete she

imperfect life. studiously he avoid the word 'ideal'; from such

vaporous illusion he have turn to the world's actuality; his

prospect near enough to be soberly examine.



a hurry to catch the train facilitated parting. Godwin promise to

write in a few day.



he take a roundabout way back to his lodging. the rain be over, the

sky have become placid. he be conscious of an effect from Christian's

indulged,--the joy of liberty and of an outlook wholly new. sidwell

the luring of 'ideal' that prompt he to turn away from his long

there must be no more untruthfulness. sidwell must have all the fact

lay before she, and make her choice.



without a clear understanding of what he be go to write, he sit

down at eleven o'clock, and begin, 'dear Miss Warricombe'. why not

'Dear Sidwell'? he take another sheet of paper.



'dear Sidwell,--To-night I can remember only your last word to I when

we part. I cannot address you coldly, as though half a stranger. thus

long I have keep silence about everything but the outward event of my

life; now, in tell you of something that have happen, I must speak

as I think.



'early this evening I be surprise by a visit from Christian Moxey--a

name you know. he come to tell I that his sister (she of whom I once

speak to you) be dead, and have bequeath to I a large sum of money.

he say that it represent an income of eight hundred pound.



'I know nothing of Miss Moxey's illness, and the news of she will come

to I as a surprise. in word or deed, I never seek more than her

simple friendship--and even _that_ I believe myself to have forfeit.

scruple which I do not in truth respect. Christian Moxey tell I that

his sister's desire be to enable I to live the life of a free man;

and if I have any duty at all in the matter, surely it do not

the gift release I from the necessity of lead a hopeless

existence--leave I at liberty to direct my life how I will.

passionate hope of three year ago still rule my life. be _your_ love

whether you understand I well enough to have forgive and forget

those hateful thing in the past. be it yet too soon? do you wish I

still to wait, still to prove myself? be your interest in the free man

less than in the slave? for my life have be one of slavery and

exile--exile, if you know what I mean by it, from the day of my birth.



'Dearest, grant I this great happiness! we can live where we will. I

be not rich enough to promise all the comfort and refinement to which

travel; we can make a home in any european city. it would be idle to

there be no soil upon my spirit. I offer you love as noble as any man

be capable of. think--think well--before reply to I; let your true

self prevail. you _do_ love I, dearest.----



your ever, Godwin Peak.'



at first he write slowly, as though engage on a literary composition,

with erasion, insertion. fact once state, he allow himself to

forget how Sidwell would most likely view they, and thereafter his pen

hastened: fervour inspire the last paragraph. Sidwell's image have

become present to he, and exercise all--or nearly all--its old

influence.



the letter must be copy, because of that laboured beginning. copy

one's own word be at all time a disenchant drudgery, and when the

end be reach Godwin sign his name with hasty contempt. what answer

could he expect to such an appeal? how vast an improbability that

Sidwell would consent to profit by the gift of Marcella Moxey!



yet how otherwise could he write? with what show of sincerity could he

do so, but simply state the fact that his refusal be beyond recall.

logically, he have choose the only course open to him,--for to refuse

independence be impossible.



a wheezy clock in his landlady's kitchen be strike two. for very

fear of have to revise his letter in the morning, he put it into its

envelope, and go out to the near pillar-post.



that be do. whether Sidwell answer with 'yes' or with 'no', he be

a free man.



on the morrow he go to his work as usual, and on the day after that.

the third morning might bring a reply--but do not. on the evening of

the fifth day, when he come home, there lie the expect letter. he

feel it; it be light and thin. that hideous choking of suspense--well,

it run thus:



'I cannot. it be not that I be trouble by your accept the legacy.

you have every right to do so, and I know that your life will justify

the hope of she who thus befriend you. but I be too weak to take

this step. to ask you to wait yet long, would only be a fresh

cowardice. you cannot know how it shame I to write this. in my very

heart I believe I love you, but what be such love worth? you must

despise I, and you will forget I. I live in a little world; in the

great world where your place be, you will win a love very different.



S. W.'



Godwin laugh aloud as the paper drop from his hand.



well, she be not the heroine of a romance. have he expect she to

leave home and kindre--the 'little world' so infinitely dear to

she--and go forth with a man deeply dishonour? very young girl have

be know to do such a thing; but a thoughtful mature woman----!

Present, his passion have dominate she: and perhaps her nerve only.

but she have have time to recover from that weakness.



a woman, like most woman of cool blood, temperate fancy. a domestic

woman; the ornament of a typical English home.



most likely it be true that the matter of the legacy do not trouble

she. in any case she would not have consent to marry he, and

_therefore_ she know no jealousy. her love! why, truly, what be it

worth?



(much, much! of no less than infinite value. he know it, but this be

not the moment for such a truth.)



a cup of tea to steady the nerve. then thought, planning,

world-building.



he be awake all night, and Sidwell's letter lie within reach.--Did

_she_ sleep calmly? have she never stretch out her hand for _his_

letter, when all be silent? there be man who would not take such a

refusal. a scheme to meet she once more--the appeal of passion, face to

face, heart to heart--the mean of escape ready--and then the 'great

world'----



but neither be he cast in heroic mould. he have not the

self-confidence, he have not the hot, youthful blood. a critic of life,

an analyst of mood and motive; not the man who dare and act. the

only important resolve he have ever carry through be a scheme of

ignoble trickery--to end in frustration.



'the great world'. it be a phrase that have be in his own mind once

or twice since Moxey's visit. to point he thither be doubtless the

one service Sidwell could render he. and in a day or two, that phrase

be all that remain to he of her letter.



on a Sunday afternoon at the end of October, Godwin once more climb

the familiar stair at Staple Inn, and be welcome by his friend

Earwaker. the visit be by appointment. Earwaker know all about the

legacy; that it be accept; and that Peak have only a few day to

spend in London, on his way to the continent.



'you be regenerate,' be his remark as Godwin enter.



'do I look it? just what I feel. I have shake off a good (or a bad)

ten year.'



the speaker's face, at all event in this moment, be no long that of

a man at hungry issue with the world. he speak cheerily.

hear it say: if only I could begin life again with all the experience

I have gain! that be what I _can_ do. I can break utterly with the

past, and I have learn how to live in the future.'



'break utterly with the past?'



'in the practical sense. and even morally to a great extent.'



Earwaker push a box of cigar across the table. Godwin accept the

'here be something that will interest you--possibly you have see it.'



he begin to read aloud:



'"on the 23rd inst. be celebrate at St. Bragg's, Torquay, the

marriage of the Rev. Bruno Leathwaite Chilvers, late Rector of St

Margaret's, Exeter, and the Hon. Bertha Harriet Cecilia Jute, eldest

daughter of the late Baron Jute. the ceremony be conduct by the Hon.

and Rev. J. C. Jute, uncle of the bride, assist by the Rev. F.

Miller, the Very Rev. Dean Pinnock, the Rev. H. S. Crook, and the Rev.

William Tomkinson. the bride be give away by Lord Jute. Mr Horatio

Dukinfield be good man. the bridal dress be of white brocade, drape

with Brussels lace, the corsage be trim with lace and adorn

with orange blossom. the tulle veil, fasten with three diamond

star, the gift of"----Well, shall I go on?'



'the triumph of Chilvers!' murmur Godwin. 'I wonder whether the Hon.

Bertha be past her fortieth year?'

marry a man like chilver! how sacred the union must be!--Pray take a

the bridegroom's present to they be diamond and ruby brooch."'

it seem. what do he aim at next, I wonder?'



Earwaker cast meaning glance at his friend.



'I understand you,' say Godwin, at length. 'you mean that this merely

illustrate my own ambition. well, you be right, I confess my

shame--and there's an end of it.'



he puff at his cigar, resume presently:



'but it would be untrue if I say that I regret anything.

constitute as I be, there be no other way of learn my real need

and capability. much in the past be hateful to I, but it all have its

use. there be man--why, take your own case. you look back on life, no

doubt, with calm and satisfaction.'



'rather, with resignation.'



Godwin let his cigar fall, and laugh bitterly.



'your resignation have keep pace with life. I be always a rebel. my

good quality--I mean what I say--have always wreck I. now that I

haven't to fight with circumstance, they may possibly be make

'but what form be your happiness to take?'

I shall make friend; with money at command, one may hope to succeed in

find they in Paris, Rome.--Earwaker, think of my be able to speak

pray for the soul of that poor dead woman. I don't speak to you of she;

but do you imagine I be brutally forgetful of she to whom I owe all

this?'



'I do you justice,' return the other, quietly.



'I believe you can and do.'



'how grand it be to go forth as I be now go!' Godwin resume, after

a long pause. 'nothing to hide, no sham, no pretence. let who will

inquire about I. I be an independent Englishman, with so and so much a

year. in England I have one friend only--that be you. the result, you

see, of all these year savage strive to knit myself into the social

fabric.'



'well, you will invite I some day to your villa at Sorrento,' say

Earwaker, encouragingly.



'that I shall!' Godwin's eye flash with imaginative delight. 'and

before very long. never to a home in England!'



'by-the-bye, a request. I have never have your portrait. sit before you

leave London.'



'no. I'll send you one from Paris--it will be well do.'



'but I be serious. you promise?'



'you shall have the thing in less than a fortnight.'



the promise be keep. Earwaker receive an admirable photograph, which

he insert in his album with a curious sense of satisfaction. a face

by which every intelligent eye must be arrest; which no two observer

would interpret in the same way.



'his mate must be somewhere,' think the man of letter, 'but he will

never find she.'







CHAPTER II





in his acceptance of Sidwell's reply, Peak do not care to ask himself

whether the delay of its arrival have any meaning one way or another.

Decency would hardly have permit she to answer such a letter by

return of post; of course she wait a day or so.



but the interval mean more than this.



Sylvia Moorhouse be stay with her friend. the death of Mrs

Moorhouse, and the marriage of the mathematical brother, have leave

Sylvia homeless, though not in any distressing sense; her inclination

be to wander for a year or two, and she remain in England only until

the needful arrangement could be conclude.



'you have well come with I,' she say to Sidwell, as they walk

together on the lawn after luncheon.



the other shake her head.



'indeed, you have better.--What be you do here? what be you go

to make of your life?'



'I don't know.'



'precisely. yet one ought to live on some kind of plan. I think it be

time you get away from Exeter; it seem to I you be find its

atmosphere _morbific_.'



Sidwell laugh at the allusion.



'you know,' she say, 'that the reverend gentleman be shortly to be

married?'



'oh yes, I have hear all about it. but be he forsake the Church?'



'retire only for a time, they say.'



'forgive the question, Sidwell--do he honour you with a proposal?'



'indeed, no!'



'some one tell I it be imminent, not long ago.'



'quite a mistake,' Sidwell answer, with her grave smile. 'Mr Chilvers

have a singular manner with woman in general. it be mean, perhaps, for

subtle flattery; he may have think it the most suitable return for

the female worship he be accustom to receive.'



Mr. Warricombe be come towards they. he bring a new subject of

conversation, and as they talk the trio draw near to the gate which

lead into the road. the afternoon postman be just enter; Mr

Warricombe take from he two letter.



'one for you, Sylvia, and--one for you, Sidwell.'



a slight change in his voice cause Sidwell to look at her father as he

hand she the letter. in the same moment she recognise the writing of

the address. it be Godwin Peak's, and undoubtedly her father know it.



with a momentary hesitation Mr. Warricombe continue his talk from the

point at which he have break off, but he avoid his daughter's look,

and Sidwell be too well aware of an uneasiness which have fall upon

he. in a few minute he bring the chat to an end, and walk away

towards the house.



Sidwell hold her letter tightly. conversation be no long possible

for she; she have a painful throbbing of the heart, and feel that she

face must be play traitor. fortunately, Sylvia find it necessary to

write a reply to the missive she have receive, and her companion be

soon at liberty to seek solitude.



for more than an hour she remain alone. however unemotional the

content of the letter, its arrival would have perturb she seriously,

as in the two previous instance; what she find on open the

envelope throw she into so extreme an agitation that it be long before

she could subdue the anguish of disorder in all her sense. she have

try to believe that Godwin Peak be henceforth powerless to affect

she in this way, write what he would. the romance of her life be over;

time have bring the solution of difficulty to which she look

forward; she recognise the inevitable, as doubtless do Godwin also.

but all this be self-deception. the passionate letter delight as

much as it torture she; in secret her heart have desire this, though

reason suppress and deny the hope. no long need she remember with

pang of shame the last letter she have write, and the cold response;

once again thing be as they should be--the lover plead before

she--she with the control of his fate. the injury to her pride be

heal, and in the thought that perforce she must answer with a final

'no', she find at first more of solace than of distress.



Subsidence of physical suffering allow she to forget this emotion, in

its nature unavowable. she could think of the news Godwin send, could

torment herself with interpretation of Marcella Moxey's behaviour, and

view in detail the circumstance which enable Godwin to urge a formal

suit. among her various thought there recur frequently a regret

that this letter have not reach she, like the other two, unobserved.

her father have now learn that she be in correspondence with the

disgrace man; to keep silence would be to cause he grave trouble; yet

how much well if fortune have only once more favour she, so that the

story might have remain her secret, from begin to end.



for be not this the end?----



at the usual time she go to the drawing-room, and somehow succeed

in conversing as though nothing have disturb she. Mr Warricombe be

not see till dinner. when he come forth, Sidwell notice his air of

preoccupation, and that he avoid address she. the evening ask

too much of her self-command; she again withdraw, and only come back

when the household be ready for retire. in bid her father

goodnight, she force herself to meet his gaze; he look at she with

troubled inquiry, and she feel her cheek redden.

'no. let we go to the glass-house.'

the wind, rarely silent in this part of Devon, blow boisterously from

'I haven't hear of it.'

'very well. on that point we be at rest, then. the step must be take

the wind roar, and for some minute no other sound be audible. by

this [Updater's note: the word "time" miss?], all the inmate of the

'I'm not sure that I quite understand you. you have make up your mind

'because I be a coward.'



'if my feeling in any matter be as strong as that, I should allow it

'because your will be strong. you, Sylvia, would never (in my

position) have grant he that second interview. you would have know

her voice fail. Sylvia, affect by the outbreak of emotion in one

'dear, do you wish I to help you against what you call your cowardice?

I cannot take it upon I to encourage you until your own will have

Sidwell be long silent.



'I will go with you,' she say at last, in a low voice. 'I will ask he

to grant I perfect liberty for a year.'



when she come down next morning it be Sidwell's intention to seek a

'I have give up my intention of go away with Sylvia. later,

long be reveal to he.







CHAPTER III





Earwaker spend Christmas with his relative at Kingsmill. his father

and mother both live; the latter very infirm, unable to leave the

house; the former a man of seventy, twist with rheumatism, his face

rugge as a countenance pick out by fancy on the trunk of a big old

oak, his hand scar and deform with labour. their old age be

restful. the son who have make himself a 'gentleman', and who in London

sit at the table of the high-bear, the wealthy, the famous, see to it

that they lack no comfort.



a bright, dry morning invite the old man and the young to go forth

together. they walk from the suburb countryward, and their

conversation be of the time when a struggle be be make to bear the

expense of those three year at Whitelaw--no bad investment, as it

prove. the father speak with a strong Midland accent, use word of

dialect by no mean disagreeable to the son's ear--for dialect be a

very different thing from the bestial jargon which on the lip of the

London vulgar pass for English. they be laugh over some half

grim reminiscence, when Earwaker become aware of two people who be

approach along the pavement, they also in merry talk. one of they he

know; it be Christian Moxey.



too much interested in his companion to gaze about he, Christian come

quite near before his eye fall on Earwaker. then he start with a

pleasant surprise, change instantly to something like embarrassment

when he observe the aged man. Earwaker be willing to smile and go by,

have the other consent; but a well impulse prevail in both. they

stop and strike hand together.



'my father,' say the man of letter, quite at his ease.



Christian be equal to the occasion; he shake hand heartily with the

battered toiler, then turn to the lady at his side.



'janet, you guess who this is.--My cousin, Earwaker, Miss Janet Moxey.'



Doubtless Janet be aware that her praise have suffer no diminution

when sing by Christian to his friend. her eye just fall, but in a

moment be ready with their frank, intelligent smile. Earwaker

experience a pang--ever so slight--suggest a revision of his

philosophy.



they talk genially, and part with good wish for the New Year.



two day later, on reach home, Earwaker find in his letter-box a

scrap of paper on which be scribble a few barely legible line.

'here I be!' he at length decipher. 'got into Tilbury at eleven this

morning. where the devil be you? write to Charing Cross Hotel.' no

signature, but none be need. Malkin's return from New Zealand have

be signal in advance.



that evening the erratic gentleman burst in like a whirlwind. he be

the picture of health, though as far as ever from endue the

comfortable flesh which accompany robustness in man of calm

temperament. after violent greeting, he sit down with abrupt gravity,

and begin to talk as if in continuance of a dialogue just interrupt.



'now, don't let we have any misunderstanding. you will please remember

that my journey to England be quite independent of what take place two

year and a half ago. it have _nothing whatever_ to do with those

circumstance.'



Earwaker smile.



'I tell you,' pursue the other, hotly, 'that I be here to see

_you_--and one or two other old friend; and to look after some

more interesting than she be. but--you be at leisure, I hope,

'for an hour or two.'



'oh, confound it! when a friend come back from the end of the

earth!--Yes, yes; I understand. you be a busy man; forgive my

hastiness. well now, I be go to say that I shall probably call upon

put my relation with that family on a proper footing. our

merely that of a friend; let it be understand.'

'I daresay not,' assented Earwaker.



'you think so? very well; I have act wisely. Bella be still a child,

you know--compare with a man of my age. she be seventeen and a few

age. when we be marry, I think I shall bring she to Europe for a

'oh no! thing haven't get so far as that. what a hasty fellow you be,

Earwaker! I tell you distinctly'----



he talk till after midnight, and at leave-taking apologise profusely

for waste his friend's valuable time.



Earwaker await with some apprehension the result of Malkin's visit to

friend's intention to return to New Zealand, when, on come home late

'Earwaker, I have something very serious to tell you. give I just a

quarter of an hour.'



what calamity do this tone portend? the eccentric man seat himself

with slow movement. see by a good light, his face be not gloomy, but

very grave.



'listen to I, old friend,' he begin, slide forward to the edge of

'you impress that upon I.'



'good! I have never make love to Miss Maccabe, and I doubt whether she

have ever think of I as a possible husband.'



'well?'



'don't be impatient. I want you to grasp the fact. it be important,

because--I be go to marry Bella Jacox.'



'you don't say so?'



'why not?' cry Malkin, suddenly pass to a state of excitement.

'what objection can you make? I tell you that I be absolutely free to

the journalist calm he, and thereupon have to hear a glowing account

remember our last joint visit! ha, ha!'

'my dear fellow, she rejoice! a wonderful nobility in that poor little

Europe, complete her education. don't you approve of that?'



on Sunday, the two travel down into Kent. this time they be

distinctly an interesting man. Bella have a very clear recollection of

he resume presently.

be fairly well teach, but not, you see, under my supervision. I

I might call myself this or that for the sake of seem respectable,

educational theory to the test.'



by way of novel experience, Earwaker, not long after this, convert

but trouble be yet in store. Malkin be strongly oppose to a

'my good fellow, don't make a fool of yourself. woman be bear for one

thing only, the Church of England marriage service. how can you seek to

defeat the end of their existence? give in to the inevitable. Grin and

'I cann't! I willn't! it shall be a runaway match! I have rather suffer the

rack than go through an ordinary wedding!'



Dire be the conflict. down at Wrotham there be flood of tear. in

church, but in the great possible privacy. no carriage, no gala

dress, no invitation, no wedding feast; the bare indispensable

formality. and so it come to pass. Earwaker and the girl's governess

be the only stranger present, when, on a morning of June, Malkin and

Bella be declare by the Church to be henceforth one and indivisible.

the bride wear a graceful travel costume; the bridegroom be in

corresponding attire.



'Heaven be thank, that's over!' exclaim Malkin, as he issue from

the portal. 'Bella, we have twenty-three minute to get to the railway

station. don't cry!' he whisper to she. 'I cann't stand that!'



'no, no; don't be afraid,' she whisper back. 'we have say good-bye

already.'



'Capital! that be very thoughtful of you.--Goodbye, all! shall write

from Paris, Earwaker. nineteen minute; we shall just manage it!'



he spring into the cab, and away it clatter.



a letter from Paris, a letter from Strasburg, from Berlin,

Munich--letter about once a fortnight. from Bella also come an

occasional note, a pretty contrast to the incoherent enthusiasm of her

husband's composition. midway in September she announce their

departure from a retreat in Switzerland.



'we be in the utmost excitement, for it be now decide that in three

day we start for Italy! the heat have be terrific, and we have wait

on what seem to I the threshold of Paradise until we could hope to

enjoy the delight beyond. we go first to Milan. my husband, of course,

know Italy, but he share my impatience. I be to entreat you to write

to Milan, with as much news as possible. especially have you hear

anything more of Mr. Peak?'



November the pair spend in Rome, and thence be despatch the

following in Malkin's hand:



'this time I be _not_ mistaken! I have see Peak. he don't see I;

perhaps wouldn't have know I. it be in Piale's reading-room. I have

sit down to _the Times_, when a voice behind I sound in such a

curiously remind way that I couldn't help look round. it be

Peak; not a doubt of it. I might have be uncertain about his face,

but the voice bring back that conversation at your room too

unmistakably--long ago as it be. he be talk to an American, whom

evidently he have meet somewhere else, and have now recognise. "I've have

a fever," he say, "and cann't quite shake off the result. be in

Ischia for the last month. I'm go north to Vienna." then the two

walk away together. he look ill, sallow, wear out. let I know if

you hear.'



on that same day, Earwaker receive another letter, with the roman

post-mark. it be from Peak.



'I have have nothing particular to tell you. a month ago I think I

should never write to you again; I get malarial fever, and lay

desperately ill at the _Ospedale Internazionale_ at Naples. it come of

some monstrous folly there's no need to speak of. a new and valuable

experience. I know what it be to look steadily into the eye of Death.



'even now, I be far from well. this keep I in low spirit. the other

day I be half decide to start for London. I be miserably alone, want

to see a friend. what a glorious place Staple Inn seem to I as I lay

in the hospital! Proof how low I have sink: I think longingly of

Exeter, of a certain house there--never mind!



'I write hastily. an invitation from some musical people have decide I

to strike for Vienna. up there, I shall get my health back. the people

be of no account--boarding-house acquaintance--but they may lead to

well. I never in my life suffer so from loneliness.'



this be the eighteenth of November. on the twenty-eighth the postman

deliver a letter of an appearance which puzzle Earwaker. the stamp

be Austrian, the mark 'Wien'. from Peak, therefore. but the writing

be unknown, plainly that of a foreigner.



the envelope contain two sheet of paper. the one be cover with a

long communication in German; on the other stand a few word of

English, write, or rather scrawl, in a hand there be no

recognise:



'Ill again, and alone. if I die, act for I. write to Mrs. Peak,

Twybridge.'



Beneath be add, 'J. E. Earwaker, Staple Inn, London.'



he turn hurriedly to the foreign writing. Earwaker read a german book

as easily as an English, but german manuscript be a terror to he. and

the present correspondent write so execrably that beyond _Geehrter

Herr_, scarcely a word yield sense to his anxious eye. ha! one he

have make out--_gestorben_.



crumple the paper into his pocket, he hasten out, and knock at

the door of an acquaintance in another part of the Inn. this be a man

who have probably more skill in german cursive. between they, they

extract the essence of the letter.



he who write be the landlord of an hotel in Vienna. he report that

an english gentleman, name Peak, just arrive from Italy, have take a

bedroom at that house. in the night, the stranger become very ill, send

for a doctor, and write the line enclose, the purport whereof he at

the same time explain to his attendant. on the second day Mr. Peak

die. among his effect be find circular note, and a sum of loose

money. the body be about to be inter. probably Mr. Earwaker would

receive official communication, as the british consul have be

informed of the matter. to whom should _bill_ be send?



the man of letter walk slowly back to his own abode.



'dead, too, in exile!' be his thought. 'poor old fellow!'
